Experimental Design and Power

The Problem

Dr. Sarah Nguyen’s lab wants to run an RNA-seq experiment. They plan to compare gene expression in treated versus untreated cell lines to identify transcriptional responses to a new drug candidate. The budget allows for 12 sequencing libraries.

The question that will determine whether this experiment succeeds or fails: how should those 12 libraries be allocated?

Option A: 2 conditions x 6 biological replicates = 12 libraries Option B: 2 conditions x 3 biological replicates x 2 technical replicates = 12 Option C: 4 conditions x 3 biological replicates = 12 libraries Option D: 2 conditions x 2 biological replicates x 3 time points = 12 libraries

Each option answers a different question with different statistical power. Option B is almost always wrong (technical replicates add little value). Option A maximizes power for a single comparison. Option C enables multiple comparisons but with less power for each. Option D enables a time course but is severely underpowered.

This chapter is about making these decisions rigorously, using real variance estimates from pilot data and explicit power calculations. We will show that a poorly designed experiment wastes money, time, and biological material — and produces results that cannot be trusted.

Why It Matters

The cost of an underpowered experiment is not just statistical. It is ethical. Animals are sacrificed, patient samples are consumed, years of work are invested, and papers are published with results that do not replicate. A 30-minute power analysis before the experiment can prevent all of this.

Here is what happens when experiments are underpowered:

  1. Wasted resources. If your experiment has 20% power, you have an 80% chance of finding nothing — even when a real effect exists. The money, samples, and time are gone.

  2. Winner’s curse. The effects that DO reach significance in underpowered studies are systematically overestimated. Follow-up studies will show smaller effects, creating the illusion that the original finding “failed to replicate.”

  3. Publication bias. Only “significant” results get published, creating a literature full of inflated effects from underpowered studies. This distorts the field’s understanding of effect sizes.

  4. Ethical concerns. Using biological samples (especially patient tissue or animal models) in experiments too small to yield reliable conclusions is ethically questionable.

The solution is to calculate the required sample size BEFORE the experiment, using realistic effect size and variance estimates from pilot data or the literature.

The Data

We will use two datasets for this chapter:

  1. GEO GSE183947 — breast cancer RNA-seq FPKM values. We will extract real variance estimates to use in power calculations for RNA-seq experiments.

  2. gnomAD constraint metrics — gene-level constraint scores from the Genome Aggregation Database, useful for variant detection power analysis.

# Download breast cancer RNA-seq data for variance estimation
let expr_url = "https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/series/GSE183nnn/GSE183947/suppl/GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz"
let expr_file = download(expr_url, "data/GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz")
let expr = read_csv(expr_file)

println("Expression dataset: " + to_string(len(expr)) + " genes x " + to_string(ncols(expr)) + " columns")

# Download gnomAD constraint metrics
let gnomad_url = "https://storage.googleapis.com/gcp-public-data--gnomad/release/4.1/constraint/gnomad.v4.1.constraint_metrics.tsv"
let gnomad_file = download(gnomad_url, "data/gnomad_constraint.tsv")
let gnomad = read_tsv(gnomad_file)

println("gnomAD constraint dataset: " + to_string(len(gnomad)) + " genes x " + to_string(ncols(gnomad)) + " columns")
println("gnomAD columns:")
each(columns(gnomad), |c| println("  " + c))

Exploration

Estimating variance from pilot data

The most critical input to any power calculation is the variance. For RNA-seq, we need the within-group variance of log-transformed expression values.

# Extract expression columns (skip the gene ID column)
let all_cols = columns(expr)
let gene_col = all_cols[0]
let sample_cols = filter(all_cols, |c| c != gene_col)

println("Gene ID column: " + gene_col)
println("Sample columns: " + to_string(len(sample_cols)))
each(sample_cols, |s| println("  " + s))

# For each gene, calculate the within-group variance
# Use the first 3 samples as one group (for pilot estimation)
let pilot_cols = slice(sample_cols, 0, 3)

let gene_variances = map(range(0, min(len(expr), 5000)), |i| {
  let row = slice(expr, i, i + 1)
  let vals = map(pilot_cols, |c| {
    col_values(row, c) |> head(1) |> to_float()
  })
  # Log-transform (adding pseudocount)
  let log_vals = map(vals, |x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let m = mean(log_vals)
  let v = variance(log_vals)
  let s = stdev(log_vals)
  record(mean_expr: m, variance: v, stdev: s)
})

# Summary of variance estimates
let all_vars = map(gene_variances, |g| g.variance)
let all_sds = map(gene_variances, |g| g.stdev)
let all_means = map(gene_variances, |g| g.mean_expr)

# Filter to expressed genes (mean log2 expression > 1)
let expressed = filter(gene_variances, |g| g.mean_expr > 1.0)
let expr_vars = map(expressed, |g| g.variance)
let expr_sds = map(expressed, |g| g.stdev)

println("Variance estimates for expressed genes (n=" + to_string(len(expressed)) + "):")
println("  Mean variance: " + to_string(round(mean(expr_vars), 4)))
println("  Median variance: " + to_string(round(median(expr_vars), 4)))
println("  Mean SD: " + to_string(round(mean(expr_sds), 3)))
println("  Median SD: " + to_string(round(median(expr_sds), 3)))
println("  Q25 SD: " + to_string(round(quantile(expr_sds, 0.25), 3)))
println("  Q75 SD: " + to_string(round(quantile(expr_sds, 0.75), 3)))
println("  Q90 SD: " + to_string(round(quantile(expr_sds, 0.90), 3)))

# The distribution of variance across genes is crucial
# It tells us what effect sizes we can detect at different power levels

histogram(expr_sds, "plots/gene_sd_distribution.svg")
  |> save_plot("plots/gene_sd_distribution.svg")

# Plot variance vs mean expression
# Lowly expressed genes tend to have higher variance
scatter(all_means, all_sds, "plots/mean_vs_sd.svg")
  |> save_plot("plots/mean_vs_sd.svg")

println("Mean-variance relationship:")
let high_expr = filter(gene_variances, |g| g.mean_expr > 5.0)
let low_expr = filter(gene_variances, |g| g.mean_expr > 1.0 && g.mean_expr <= 5.0)
println("  High expression (>5 log2): median SD = " + to_string(round(median(map(high_expr, |g| g.stdev)), 3)))
println("  Low expression (1-5 log2): median SD = " + to_string(round(median(map(low_expr, |g| g.stdev)), 3)))

Understanding effect sizes in RNA-seq

# Effect size in RNA-seq is typically measured as log2 fold change
# Cohen's d = log2FC / pooled_SD

# What log2 fold changes are biologically meaningful?
# |log2FC| = 0.5 means ~1.4-fold change (small)
# |log2FC| = 1.0 means ~2-fold change (moderate)
# |log2FC| = 2.0 means ~4-fold change (large)
# |log2FC| = 3.0 means ~8-fold change (very large)

let fc_table = [0.5, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 3.0]
println("Log2FC to fold-change conversion:")
each(fc_table, |lfc| {
  let fc = pow(2.0, lfc)
  println("  log2FC=" + to_string(lfc) + " -> " + to_string(round(fc, 2)) + "-fold change")
})

# Using the median SD from our pilot data, compute Cohen's d
let pilot_sd = median(expr_sds)
println("")
println("Pilot data median SD: " + to_string(round(pilot_sd, 3)))
println("Cohen's d for different fold changes:")
each(fc_table, |lfc| {
  let d = lfc / pilot_sd
  println("  log2FC=" + to_string(lfc) + ": d=" + to_string(round(d, 2)))
})

The Analysis

Power calculation from first principles

Power is the probability of rejecting the null hypothesis when it is false. It depends on four quantities:

  • Effect size (how big the difference is)
  • Sample size (how many observations per group)
  • Significance level (alpha, usually 0.05)
  • Variance (how noisy the data are)
# Power for a two-sample t-test
# The non-centrality parameter is: delta = d * sqrt(n/2)
# where d = Cohen's d, n = sample size per group
#
# Power = P(|T| > t_crit | delta)
# Using the normal approximation:
# Power approximately = Phi(delta - z_alpha/2)
#   where z_alpha/2 = 1.96 for alpha=0.05 (two-sided)

# Calculate power for different sample sizes and effect sizes
let alpha = 0.05
let z_alpha = qnorm(1.0 - alpha / 2.0, 0.0, 1.0)

let sample_sizes = [3, 4, 5, 6, 8, 10, 12, 15, 20, 25, 30, 50]
let effect_sizes = [0.5, 0.8, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0]

println("Power table (alpha=" + to_string(alpha) + ", two-sided t-test):")
println("")

# Header
let header = "  n/group"
each(effect_sizes, |d| {
  let h = header + "  d=" + to_string(d)
})
println("n/group | d=0.5  d=0.8  d=1.0  d=1.5  d=2.0")
println("--------|--------------------------------------")

each(sample_sizes, |n| {
  let powers = map(effect_sizes, |d| {
    let delta = d * sqrt(n / 2.0)
    let power = pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
    round(power * 100, 1)
  })
  let line = to_string(n)
  let power_strs = map(powers, |p| to_string(p) + "%")
  println("  " + to_string(n) + "     | " + join(power_strs, "  "))
})

Power curves

# Plot power as a function of sample size for different effect sizes

let n_range = range(3, 51)

# For d = 1.0 (a 2-fold change with typical variance)
let power_d1 = map(n_range, |n| {
  let delta = 1.0 * sqrt(n / 2.0)
  pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
})

# For d = 0.5 (a moderate effect)
let power_d05 = map(n_range, |n| {
  let delta = 0.5 * sqrt(n / 2.0)
  pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
})

# For d = 1.5 (a large effect)
let power_d15 = map(n_range, |n| {
  let delta = 1.5 * sqrt(n / 2.0)
  pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
})

scatter(n_range, power_d1, "plots/power_curve_d1.svg")
  |> save_plot("plots/power_curve_d1.svg")

# Find the sample size needed for 80% power
println("Sample size for 80% power:")
each([0.5, 0.8, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0], |d| {
  let n_needed = 3
  let found = false
  each(range(3, 200), |n| {
    if !found then {
      let delta = d * sqrt(n / 2.0)
      let power = pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
      if power >= 0.80 then {
        println("  d=" + to_string(d) + ": n=" + to_string(n) + " per group")
        found = true
      }
    }
  })
})

RNA-seq specific power calculation

# For RNA-seq, we use the variance estimates from our pilot data
# The typical workflow:
# 1. Get variance estimates from pilot data or similar published data
# 2. Decide on the minimum fold-change you want to detect
# 3. Calculate power across sample sizes
# 4. Choose the smallest n that gives >= 80% power

# Use the real variance estimates from GSE183947
let typical_sd = median(expr_sds)
let high_var_sd = quantile(expr_sds, 0.75)
let low_var_sd = quantile(expr_sds, 0.25)

println("Variance estimates from pilot data:")
println("  Low-variance genes (Q25 SD): " + to_string(round(low_var_sd, 3)))
println("  Typical genes (median SD): " + to_string(round(typical_sd, 3)))
println("  High-variance genes (Q75 SD): " + to_string(round(high_var_sd, 3)))

# Power for detecting 2-fold change (log2FC = 1)
let target_lfc = 1.0
println("")
println("Power to detect 2-fold change (log2FC=1):")
println("n/group | Low-var  Typical  High-var")
println("--------|----------------------------")

each([3, 4, 5, 6, 8, 10, 12, 15, 20], |n| {
  let powers = map([low_var_sd, typical_sd, high_var_sd], |sd| {
    let d = target_lfc / sd
    let delta = d * sqrt(n / 2.0)
    let power = pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
    to_string(round(power * 100, 1)) + "%"
  })
  println("  " + to_string(n) + "     | " + join(powers, "    "))
})

What fraction of genes can you detect at each sample size?

# For a fixed fold-change threshold, different genes have different
# power because they have different variances
# This is a more realistic way to think about RNA-seq power:
# "What fraction of truly DE genes will I detect?"

let target_lfcs = [1.0, 1.5, 2.0]
let test_ns = [3, 5, 8, 12, 20]

println("Fraction of expressed genes detectable at 80% power:")
println("(assuming all DE genes have the specified log2FC)")
println("")

each(target_lfcs, |lfc| {
  println("log2FC = " + to_string(lfc) + " (" + to_string(round(pow(2.0, lfc), 1)) + "-fold):")
  each(test_ns, |n| {
    # For each gene, calculate whether power >= 0.80
    let detectable = filter(expr_sds, |sd| {
      let d = lfc / sd
      let delta = d * sqrt(n / 2.0)
      let power = pnorm(delta - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
      power >= 0.80
    })
    let pct = round(100.0 * len(detectable) / len(expr_sds), 1)
    println("  n=" + to_string(n) + ": " + to_string(pct) + "% of genes detectable")
  })
  println("")
})

The cost of underpowered studies

# Simulate the consequences of different sample sizes
# on a genome-wide study

# Set up: 1000 genes, 100 truly DE (10%)
# True DE genes have log2FC = 1.5
let n_genes = 1000
let n_de = 100
let n_null = n_genes - n_de
let true_lfc = 1.5
let gene_sd = typical_sd

println("Simulation: " + to_string(n_genes) + " genes, " + to_string(n_de) + " truly DE (log2FC=" + to_string(true_lfc) + ")")
println("")

each([3, 5, 8, 12, 20], |n| {
  # Simulate null genes
  let null_pvals = map(range(0, n_null), |i| {
    let g1 = rnorm(n, 8.0, gene_sd)
    let g2 = rnorm(n, 8.0, gene_sd)
    let res = ttest(g1, g2)
    res.p_value
  })

  # Simulate DE genes
  let de_pvals = map(range(0, n_de), |i| {
    let g1 = rnorm(n, 8.0, gene_sd)
    let g2 = rnorm(n, 8.0 + true_lfc, gene_sd)
    let res = ttest(g1, g2)
    res.p_value
  })

  # Apply BH correction
  let all_pvals = concat(null_pvals, de_pvals)
  let all_adjusted = p_adjust(all_pvals, "BH")

  # Split back into null and DE
  let null_adj = slice(all_adjusted, 0, n_null)
  let de_adj = slice(all_adjusted, n_null, n_genes)

  let fp = len(filter(null_adj, |p| p < 0.05))
  let tp = len(filter(de_adj, |p| p < 0.05))
  let fn_count = n_de - tp
  let total_sig = fp + tp

  let fdr = if total_sig > 0 then round(100.0 * fp / total_sig, 1) else 0.0
  let power = round(100.0 * tp / n_de, 1)
  let sensitivity = power

  println("n=" + to_string(n) + " per group:")
  println("  True positives: " + to_string(tp) + "/" + to_string(n_de) + " (power=" + to_string(power) + "%)")
  println("  False positives: " + to_string(fp) + "/" + to_string(n_null))
  println("  False negatives: " + to_string(fn_count) + "/" + to_string(n_de))
  println("  Total significant: " + to_string(total_sig))
  println("  Observed FDR: " + to_string(fdr) + "%")
  println("")
})

Biological vs technical replicates

# This is the most important design concept in genomics
# and the most commonly violated

# BIOLOGICAL REPLICATES: independent biological samples
# - Different patients, different mice, different cell cultures
# - Capture biological variability
# - THESE ARE WHAT COUNT FOR SAMPLE SIZE

# TECHNICAL REPLICATES: repeated measurements of the same sample
# - Same RNA extracted twice, same library sequenced twice
# - Capture technical variability
# - DO NOT increase your effective sample size for biological questions

# Demonstrate why:
# Scenario 1: 6 biological replicates, 1 technical rep each = n=6
# Scenario 2: 3 biological replicates, 2 technical reps each = n=3 (NOT n=6!)

let bio_sd = 1.5   # biological variability (between individuals)
let tech_sd = 0.3   # technical variability (between replicates)

# Total SD with biological replicates:
# Each observation is independent, SD = sqrt(bio_sd^2 + tech_sd^2)
let total_sd = sqrt(bio_sd * bio_sd + tech_sd * tech_sd)
println("Standard deviation components:")
println("  Biological SD: " + to_string(bio_sd))
println("  Technical SD: " + to_string(tech_sd))
println("  Total SD: " + to_string(round(total_sd, 3)))
println("")
println("Proportion of variance from biology: " + to_string(round(100.0 * bio_sd * bio_sd / (total_sd * total_sd), 1)) + "%")
println("Proportion of variance from technology: " + to_string(round(100.0 * tech_sd * tech_sd / (total_sd * total_sd), 1)) + "%")

# Averaging technical replicates reduces tech_sd but NOT bio_sd
# With k technical reps per biological sample:
# SD of average = sqrt(bio_sd^2 + tech_sd^2/k)
each([1, 2, 3, 5, 10], |k| {
  let avg_sd = sqrt(bio_sd * bio_sd + tech_sd * tech_sd / k)
  let reduction = round(100.0 * (1.0 - avg_sd / total_sd), 1)
  println("  k=" + to_string(k) + " tech reps: SD=" + to_string(round(avg_sd, 3)) + " (" + to_string(reduction) + "% reduction)")
})

println("")
println("Conclusion: doubling technical reps barely helps because")
println("biology dominates the variance. Spend the budget on more")
println("biological replicates instead.")

# Compare the two allocation strategies
# Budget: 12 libraries total

# Strategy A: 6 bio reps x 1 tech rep per group = n=6
let n_bio_a = 6
let sd_a = total_sd
let d_a = 1.0 / sd_a
let delta_a = d_a * sqrt(n_bio_a / 2.0)
let power_a = pnorm(delta_a - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)

# Strategy B: 3 bio reps x 2 tech reps per group = n=3 (with averaged tech reps)
let n_bio_b = 3
let sd_b = sqrt(bio_sd * bio_sd + tech_sd * tech_sd / 2.0)
let d_b = 1.0 / sd_b
let delta_b = d_b * sqrt(n_bio_b / 2.0)
let power_b = pnorm(delta_b - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)

println("Comparison for detecting 2-fold change:")
println("Strategy A (6 bio reps, 1 tech rep):")
println("  Effective SD: " + to_string(round(sd_a, 3)))
println("  Power: " + to_string(round(power_a * 100, 1)) + "%")
println("")
println("Strategy B (3 bio reps, 2 tech reps):")
println("  Effective SD: " + to_string(round(sd_b, 3)))
println("  Power: " + to_string(round(power_b * 100, 1)) + "%")
println("")
println("Strategy A has " + to_string(round(power_a * 100 - power_b * 100, 1)) + " percentage points more power")
println("for the SAME total cost (12 libraries)")

Paired vs unpaired designs

# Paired designs (before/after treatment on the same individual)
# remove inter-individual variability, dramatically increasing power

# In a paired design:
# We test the DIFFERENCES within each pair
# The variance of the difference = 2 * within_individual_variance
# NOT 2 * total_variance (which includes between-individual variance)

let between_ind_sd = 2.0  # large variability between individuals
let within_ind_sd = 0.5   # small variability within individuals (over time)
let unpaired_sd = sqrt(between_ind_sd * between_ind_sd + within_ind_sd * within_ind_sd)
let paired_diff_sd = sqrt(2.0) * within_ind_sd  # SD of paired differences

println("Paired vs Unpaired design:")
println("  Between-individual SD: " + to_string(between_ind_sd))
println("  Within-individual SD: " + to_string(within_ind_sd))
println("  Unpaired test SD: " + to_string(round(unpaired_sd, 3)))
println("  Paired test SD (of differences): " + to_string(round(paired_diff_sd, 3)))
println("")

# Power comparison
let target = 1.0  # detect log2FC = 1
each([5, 8, 10, 15, 20], |n| {
  let d_unpaired = target / unpaired_sd
  let d_paired = target / paired_diff_sd

  let delta_unp = d_unpaired * sqrt(n / 2.0)
  let delta_pr = d_paired * sqrt(n)  # paired test uses n, not n/2

  let power_unp = pnorm(delta_unp - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
  let power_pr = pnorm(delta_pr - z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)

  println("  n=" + to_string(n) + " per group: Unpaired=" + to_string(round(power_unp * 100, 1)) + "%, Paired=" + to_string(round(power_pr * 100, 1)) + "%")
})

println("")
println("Paired designs are dramatically more powerful when")
println("between-individual variability is high")

Blocking and randomization

# Blocking: grouping experimental units to control known sources
# of variability
#
# Example: processing RNA-seq libraries in two batches
# BAD: all treated in batch 1, all control in batch 2
#   -> confounds treatment with batch
# GOOD: half treated + half control in each batch
#   -> batch effect is orthogonal to treatment

# Randomization within blocks:
# Within each batch, randomly assign samples to conditions
# This prevents unconscious bias

# Example design for 12 samples (6 treated, 6 control) in 2 batches:
println("Balanced block design:")
println("")
println("Batch 1:")
println("  Treated:  T1, T2, T3")
println("  Control:  C1, C2, C3")
println("")
println("Batch 2:")
println("  Treated:  T4, T5, T6")
println("  Control:  C4, C5, C6")
println("")
println("Each batch has equal numbers of treated and control")
println("Batch effect cancels out in the treatment comparison")

# The variance reduction from blocking:
# Total variance = treatment_variance + batch_variance + error_variance
# With blocking: we REMOVE batch_variance from the error term
# Without blocking: batch_variance inflates the error term

let batch_sd = 0.8
let error_sd = 1.0
let total_unblocked = sqrt(batch_sd * batch_sd + error_sd * error_sd)
let total_blocked = error_sd

println("")
println("Variance with and without blocking:")
println("  Unblocked SD: " + to_string(round(total_unblocked, 3)))
println("  Blocked SD: " + to_string(round(total_blocked, 3)))
println("  Variance reduction: " + to_string(round(100.0 * (1.0 - total_blocked * total_blocked / (total_unblocked * total_unblocked)), 1)) + "%")

Pseudoreplication: the most common design mistake

# Pseudoreplication occurs when non-independent observations are
# treated as independent replicates
#
# Common examples in genomics:
#
# 1. CELLS from the same individual treated as biological replicates
#    If you grow 3 wells from the same cell line and call them n=3,
#    you have pseudoreplication. The true n=1 (one cell line).
#
# 2. MULTIPLE measurements per mouse
#    Taking 3 tissue samples from 1 mouse does not give n=3.
#    It gives n=1 with 3 technical replicates.
#
# 3. SINGLE-CELL data
#    1000 cells from one donor is NOT n=1000 for between-donor
#    comparisons. It is n=1 with 1000 cells per donor.
#
# 4. SEQUENCING LANES
#    Running the same library on 2 lanes gives 2 FASTQ files
#    but n=1. Merge and analyze as a single sample.

println("Pseudoreplication example:")
println("")
println("A study reports: 'n=30 cells per condition'")
println("But all 30 cells came from ONE donor per condition")
println("")
println("Truth: n=1 per condition (one donor)")
println("Claim: n=30 per condition (treating cells as replicates)")
println("")
println("Consequence: variance is underestimated by ~30x")
println("All p-values are far too small")
println("Almost everything appears 'significant'")

# Demonstrate the damage
let donor_mean_treated = 8.0
let donor_mean_control = 8.0  # no real difference
let between_donor_sd = 1.5
let within_donor_sd = 0.3     # cell-to-cell within one donor

# Correct analysis: compare donor means (n=1 vs n=1)
# No test is possible with n=1 per group!

# Pseudoreplicated analysis: treat cells as independent
let pseudo_treated = rnorm(30, donor_mean_treated, within_donor_sd)
let pseudo_control = rnorm(30, donor_mean_control, within_donor_sd)
let pseudo_result = ttest(pseudo_treated, pseudo_control)
println("")
println("Pseudoreplicated t-test (WRONG):")
println("  p-value: " + to_string(round(pseudo_result.p_value, 4)))
println("  This p-value is meaningless because cells are not independent")

Power for variant detection

# Different question: How many samples do I need to detect a variant
# at a given allele frequency?

# For a rare variant with frequency f in n individuals:
# The number of copies follows Binomial(2n, f) for diploid organisms
# We need at least 1 copy to detect the variant
# P(detect) = 1 - P(0 copies) = 1 - (1-f)^(2n)

let frequencies = [0.01, 0.005, 0.001, 0.0005, 0.0001]
let cohort_sizes = [100, 500, 1000, 5000, 10000, 50000, 100000]

println("Probability of observing at least one copy of a variant:")
println("(by allele frequency and cohort size)")
println("")
println("Cohort   | f=1%    f=0.5%  f=0.1%  f=0.05% f=0.01%")
println("---------|-------------------------------------------")

each(cohort_sizes, |n| {
  let probs = map(frequencies, |f| {
    let p_detect = 1.0 - pow(1.0 - f, 2.0 * n)
    to_string(round(p_detect * 100, 1)) + "%"
  })
  println("  " + to_string(n) + " | " + join(probs, "  "))
})

Using gnomAD constraint data for design

# gnomAD constraint metrics tell us which genes are intolerant
# to loss-of-function (LoF) mutations
# This is relevant for study design: constrained genes have fewer
# variants, requiring larger samples to assess

# Explore the constraint data
println("gnomAD constraint metrics columns:")
each(head(columns(gnomad), 20), |c| println("  " + c))

head(gnomad, 5)

# Key metrics:
# oe_lof: observed/expected ratio for LoF variants
# pLI: probability of being LoF-intolerant
# LOEUF: LoF observed/expected upper bound fraction (95% CI upper bound)

# A gene with LOEUF < 0.35 is considered LoF-constrained
# These genes are likely to be haploinsufficient

# Analyze the distribution of constraint metrics
let loeuf_vals = col_values(gnomad, "oe_lof_upper")
let loeuf_clean = filter(loeuf_vals, |x| !is_none(x))
let loeuf_float = map(loeuf_clean, |x| to_float(x))
let loeuf_valid = filter(loeuf_float, |x| x > 0.0 && x < 10.0)

println("LOEUF distribution (n=" + to_string(len(loeuf_valid)) + " genes):")
println("  Mean: " + to_string(round(mean(loeuf_valid), 3)))
println("  Median: " + to_string(round(median(loeuf_valid), 3)))
println("  Q10: " + to_string(round(quantile(loeuf_valid, 0.10), 3)))
println("  Q90: " + to_string(round(quantile(loeuf_valid, 0.90), 3)))

let constrained = filter(loeuf_valid, |x| x < 0.35)
let unconstrained = filter(loeuf_valid, |x| x >= 0.35)
println("  Constrained (LOEUF < 0.35): " + to_string(len(constrained)) + " genes")
println("  Unconstrained (LOEUF >= 0.35): " + to_string(len(unconstrained)) + " genes")

histogram(loeuf_valid, "plots/loeuf_distribution.svg")
  |> save_plot("plots/loeuf_distribution.svg")

# For rare disease studies: if your candidate gene is LoF-constrained,
# you need fewer affected individuals to be confident that a de novo
# LoF variant is pathogenic (because such variants are depleted in
# the general population)

# Expected number of de novo LoF variants per gene per generation:
# Approximately 1.5e-8 per nucleotide per generation
# For a gene with ~1000 coding nucleotides: ~1.5e-5 de novo LoFs expected

let mutation_rate_per_bp = 1.5e-8
let coding_length = 1000.0
let de_novo_rate = mutation_rate_per_bp * coding_length

println("De novo LoF variant rate per gene per generation:")
println("  " + to_string(de_novo_rate))

# In a cohort of N trios (affected child + parents):
# Expected de novo LoF variants in gene G = N * de_novo_rate
# Finding >= 2 de novo LoF variants in the same gene is significant
# if the gene is constrained

each([100, 500, 1000, 5000, 10000], |n_trios| {
  let expected = n_trios * de_novo_rate
  let p_ge_2 = 1.0 - ppois(1, expected)
  println("  " + to_string(n_trios) + " trios: E[de novo]=" + to_string(round(expected, 4)) + ", P(>=2)=" + to_string(round(p_ge_2, 6)))
})

GWAS power calculation

# For GWAS, the effect sizes are much smaller than RNA-seq
# A typical odds ratio for a common variant is 1.1-1.3
# This requires thousands to hundreds of thousands of samples

# Power for a GWAS association test (simplified):
# Uses a chi-square approximation

# Key parameters:
# - Minor allele frequency (MAF)
# - Effect size (odds ratio or beta)
# - Sample size
# - Alpha (typically 5e-8 for genome-wide significance)

let gwas_alpha = 5e-8  # genome-wide significance threshold
let z_gwas = qnorm(1.0 - gwas_alpha / 2.0, 0.0, 1.0)

println("GWAS power analysis:")
println("Genome-wide significance threshold: p < " + to_string(gwas_alpha))
println("Required Z-score: " + to_string(round(z_gwas, 2)))
println("")

# For a quantitative trait:
# Power depends on variance explained (R^2)
# R^2 = 2 * MAF * (1-MAF) * beta^2

let mafs = [0.05, 0.10, 0.20, 0.30, 0.40]
let betas = [0.05, 0.10, 0.20]  # effect size in SD units

println("Minimum sample size for 80% power in GWAS:")
println("(quantitative trait, genome-wide significance)")
println("")

each(betas, |beta| {
  println("Effect size beta = " + to_string(beta) + " SD:")
  each(mafs, |maf| {
    let r2 = 2.0 * maf * (1.0 - maf) * beta * beta
    # Approximate n for 80% power:
    # n approximately = (z_alpha + z_beta)^2 / r2
    # where z_beta = 0.842 for 80% power
    let z_beta = 0.842
    let n_needed = ceil((z_gwas + z_beta) * (z_gwas + z_beta) / r2)
    println("  MAF=" + to_string(maf) + ": n=" + to_string(round(n_needed, 0)))
  })
  println("")
})

Interpretation

The power analyses in this chapter reveal several important truths:

1. n = 3 gives 20-30% power for moderate effects. This means you miss 70-80% of real biology. For RNA-seq, n = 6 per group is a practical minimum for discovery; n = 12 is needed for robust detection of 2-fold changes.

2. Biological replicates dominate technical replicates. When biological variance accounts for 95%+ of total variance (as is typical), adding technical replicates is almost worthless. Always maximize biological replicates.

3. Paired designs can be dramatically more powerful. When between-individual variability is high (which it usually is), a paired design effectively removes it, often doubling or tripling the effective sample size.

4. GWAS requires enormous samples. Effect sizes for common variants are tiny (explaining < 1% of variance per variant), requiring thousands to hundreds of thousands of samples for genome-wide significance.

5. Power is not a property of the study design alone. It depends on the specific gene’s variance, the effect size for that gene, and the significance threshold. Some genes are always easy to detect; others are always hard.

Decision

# Decision framework for experimental design

# Step 1: Define your primary question
# "Which genes are DE between treated and control?"
# "Is there a GWAS signal for trait X at locus Y?"
# "Does this drug reduce tumor growth?"

# Step 2: Estimate effect size
# From pilot data, literature, or biological reasoning
# Be conservative — real effects are usually smaller than pilot data suggests

# Step 3: Estimate variance
# From pilot data (use median across genes for RNA-seq)
# Or from publicly available data (GTEx, ENCODE, GEO)

# Step 4: Choose your power target
# Standard: 80% (you detect 4 out of 5 real effects)
# High: 90% (preferable for confirmatory studies)
# Minimum: 60% (only acceptable for very exploratory work)

# Step 5: Calculate required sample size
# Use the formulas from this chapter
# Or simulation for complex designs

# Step 6: Can you afford it?
# If yes: proceed
# If no: consider:
#   a) Reducing the number of conditions
#   b) Using a paired design (if possible)
#   c) Pooling with collaborators
#   d) Using a more sensitive assay
#   e) Accepting lower power (document this limitation)
#   f) Focusing on larger effect sizes

# Step 7: Randomize and block
# Assign samples to conditions randomly
# Balance batches across conditions
# Document the randomization procedure

# Step 8: Pre-register your analysis plan
# Specify the statistical test, significance threshold, and
# effect size threshold BEFORE looking at the data
# This prevents p-hacking and HARKing

println("Sample size recommendations summary:")
println("")
println("| Study Type              | Min n/group | Target n/group | Notes                         |")
println("|------------------------|-------------|----------------|-------------------------------|")
println("| RNA-seq discovery       | 3           | 6-12           | Use empirical Bayes methods   |")
println("| RNA-seq confirmation    | 6           | 12-20          | Pre-registered analysis       |")
println("| ATAC-seq               | 2           | 3-5            | Lower variance than RNA-seq   |")
println("| ChIP-seq               | 2           | 3-5            | Need input controls           |")
println("| GWAS (common variant)  | 1000        | 10000+         | Effect sizes are tiny         |")
println("| scRNA-seq              | 3 donors    | 6+ donors      | Cells are NOT replicates      |")
println("| Rare variant burden    | 500         | 5000+          | Depends on gene size          |")
println("| Clinical trial         | 30          | 100+           | Depends on endpoint variance  |")

Sample size calculation template

# Template for RNA-seq power calculation
# Fill in your own values

# YOUR PARAMETERS (modify these)
let my_target_lfc = 1.0       # minimum log2 fold change to detect
let my_sd = 1.2               # estimated within-group SD (from pilot)
let my_alpha = 0.05           # significance level
let my_power_target = 0.80    # desired power

# CALCULATION
let my_z_alpha = qnorm(1.0 - my_alpha / 2.0, 0.0, 1.0)
let my_z_power = qnorm(my_power_target, 0.0, 1.0)
let my_d = my_target_lfc / my_sd

# Sample size per group (approximate formula)
let n_needed = ceil(2.0 * pow((my_z_alpha + my_z_power) / my_d, 2.0))

println("=== RNA-seq Power Calculation ===")
println("Target log2FC: " + to_string(my_target_lfc))
println("Within-group SD: " + to_string(my_sd))
println("Cohen's d: " + to_string(round(my_d, 3)))
println("Alpha: " + to_string(my_alpha))
println("Target power: " + to_string(my_power_target))
println("")
println("REQUIRED SAMPLE SIZE: n = " + to_string(round(n_needed, 0)) + " per group")
println("TOTAL LIBRARIES: " + to_string(round(2.0 * n_needed, 0)))
println("")

# Verify with power calculation
let verify_delta = my_d * sqrt(n_needed / 2.0)
let verify_power = pnorm(verify_delta - my_z_alpha, 0.0, 1.0)
println("Verification: power at n=" + to_string(round(n_needed, 0)) + " is " + to_string(round(verify_power * 100, 1)) + "%")

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Using pilot effect sizes for power calculation

# Pilot studies overestimate effect sizes because only
# "significant" results from pilots are used for planning
# This is the "winner's curse" applied to study design
#
# If your pilot found log2FC = 2.5 for your gene of interest,
# the true effect is probably closer to 1.5-2.0
#
# Solution: use the LOWER BOUND of the confidence interval
# for effect size from the pilot, not the point estimate

let pilot_lfc = 2.5
let pilot_se = 0.8  # standard error of the pilot estimate

# The 80% CI lower bound is a better estimate for power calculation
let conservative_lfc = pilot_lfc - 1.28 * pilot_se  # 80% CI lower bound
println("Pilot log2FC: " + to_string(pilot_lfc))
println("Conservative estimate (80% CI lower): " + to_string(round(conservative_lfc, 2)))
println("Use " + to_string(round(conservative_lfc, 2)) + " for power calculation, not " + to_string(pilot_lfc))

Pitfall 2: Ignoring multiple testing in power calculations

# If you test 20,000 genes, your per-test alpha is NOT 0.05
# After BH correction, the effective per-test alpha is approximately
# alpha * (number of true positives / total tests)
#
# For a rough correction: use alpha / (number of tests) as your
# per-test alpha in the power calculation (Bonferroni approximation)
# This is conservative but gives a floor for the required n

let n_tests = 20000
let bonf_alpha = 0.05 / n_tests
let z_bonf = qnorm(1.0 - bonf_alpha / 2.0, 0.0, 1.0)

println("Single test alpha: 0.05")
println("Bonferroni-corrected alpha: " + to_string(bonf_alpha))
println("Required Z-score: " + to_string(round(z_bonf, 2)) + " (vs 1.96 for single test)")

# This substantially increases the required sample size
let d_example = 1.0
let z_power = 0.842

let n_single = ceil(2.0 * pow((1.96 + z_power) / d_example, 2.0))
let n_bonf = ceil(2.0 * pow((z_bonf + z_power) / d_example, 2.0))

println("")
println("Required n per group for d=1.0:")
println("  Single test: " + to_string(round(n_single, 0)))
println("  After Bonferroni for 20000 tests: " + to_string(round(n_bonf, 0)))
println("  (BH correction requires somewhat less than Bonferroni)")

Pitfall 3: Pseudoreplication in single-cell studies

# Single-cell RNA-seq creates a massive pseudoreplication temptation
# You might have 10,000 cells per condition
# But if all cells come from one donor per condition, n = 1
#
# The correct approach:
# 1. Summarize per-donor (average or pseudobulk)
# 2. Test between donors (the biological replicates)
# 3. Report the number of DONORS, not cells
#
# The cells provide information about WITHIN-DONOR heterogeneity
# The donors provide information about BETWEEN-DONOR differences
# Only the latter matters for generalizing to the population

println("Single-cell study design:")
println("")
println("WRONG: 2 donors x 5000 cells = '10000 replicates'")
println("RIGHT: 2 donors x 5000 cells = '2 biological replicates'")
println("")
println("To achieve n=6 per group in a scRNA-seq experiment,")
println("you need 6 DONORS per group (12 donors total)")
println("The number of cells per donor affects resolution")
println("but NOT the sample size for between-group comparisons")

Pitfall 4: Post-hoc power analysis

# NEVER compute "observed power" after seeing the results
# This is circular and provides no useful information
#
# If you found p = 0.06 and compute observed power = 48%,
# this does NOT mean "you almost had enough power"
# The observed power is a deterministic function of the p-value
# It tells you nothing beyond what the p-value already tells you
#
# The right time for power analysis is BEFORE the experiment
# After the experiment, report effect sizes with confidence intervals
# instead of power

println("Post-hoc power analysis is NEVER appropriate")
println("")
println("Before experiment: Power analysis guides design")
println("After experiment: Report effect sizes and CIs")
println("")
println("The observed power is:")
println("  High when p is small (you already know this from p)")
println("  Low when p is large (you already know this from p)")
println("  It adds no new information")

Pitfall 5: Confusing precision with power

# More data gives you more PRECISION (narrower confidence intervals)
# AND more POWER (probability of detecting an effect)
# These are related but not the same
#
# You might have enough power to detect a 2-fold change
# but not enough precision to estimate the fold change accurately
#
# For estimation, the CI width matters:
# CI width approximately = 2 * z * SD / sqrt(n)

let target_precision = 0.5  # want CI width < 0.5 log2FC units
let example_sd = 1.2

# Required n for this precision
let n_for_precision = ceil(pow(2.0 * 1.96 * example_sd / target_precision, 2.0))
println("Required n for CI width < " + to_string(target_precision) + " log2FC:")
println("  n = " + to_string(round(n_for_precision, 0)) + " per group")
println("")
println("This may be MORE than needed for 80% power")
println("Design for the more demanding requirement")

Where Next?

This chapter has given you the tools to design experiments that can actually answer your questions. The key principles:

  1. Calculate power before the experiment. Use real variance estimates from pilot data or public datasets. Be conservative about effect sizes.

  2. Maximize biological replicates. Technical replicates provide negligible benefit when biological variance dominates (which it always does).

  3. Consider paired designs. If you can measure the same individual before and after treatment, the power gain is enormous.

  4. Block known confounders. Balance batches, sexes, ages, and other covariates across conditions.

  5. Avoid pseudoreplication. Cells are not donors. Lanes are not libraries. Technical replicates are not biological replicates.

  6. Adjust for multiple testing. Genome-wide studies require either larger samples or acceptance of lower power per gene.

  7. Pre-register your analysis. Decide your statistical approach before looking at the data.

With solid foundations in statistical thinking (Chapter 1), exploratory analysis (Chapter 2), distributional knowledge (Chapter 3), and experimental design (this chapter), you are equipped to design, execute, and analyze genomics experiments that produce reliable, reproducible results.

Exercises

# Exercise 1: Using the GSE183947 variance estimates, calculate the
# sample size needed to detect a 1.5-fold change (log2FC = 0.585)
# with 80% power for the median-variance gene.

# Exercise 2: A lab has 24 library prep slots. They want to compare
# 3 drug doses vs control. Design the experiment (allocation of
# samples to conditions and batches). Calculate power for each
# pairwise comparison.

# Exercise 3: You have a paired design (before/after treatment)
# with within-individual SD = 0.4 and between-individual SD = 2.0.
# How many individuals do you need for 90% power to detect
# log2FC = 0.5?

# Exercise 4: Using the gnomAD constraint data, find the 10 most

# (assuming the neutral mutation rate).

# Exercise 5: A GWAS plans to test 1 million SNPs for association
# with a quantitative trait. Using genome-wide significance
# (p < 5e-8), calculate the required sample size to detect a

# Exercise 6: Repeat the biological vs technical replicate comparison
# but now assume technical variance is 50% of biological variance
# (a more noisy assay). How does the optimal allocation change?

# Exercise 7: Design a scRNA-seq experiment to compare gene expression

# How many patients per group? How many cells per patient is sufficient?

Summary formulas

QuantityFormulaNotes
Cohen’s deffect / SDStandardized effect size
Power (approx)Phi(d * sqrt(n/2) - z_alpha)Two-sample t-test
n per group2 * ((z_alpha + z_beta) / d)^2For target power
Paired powerPhi(d * sqrt(n) - z_alpha)d uses paired SD
GWAS n(z_alpha + z_beta)^2 / R^2R^2 = variance explained
Variant detection1 - (1-f)^(2n)f = allele frequency

Comparing Two Groups

“The difference between a significant result and a non-significant result is not itself statistically significant.” — Andrew Gelman & Hal Stern

You have gene expression measurements from tumor tissue and normal tissue. The question seems simple: which genes are different? The answer is anything but.

This chapter teaches you to compare two groups properly — with the right test, the right assumptions check, the right effect size measure, and the right visualization. We will work with real breast cancer expression data and discover that p-values alone are dangerously incomplete.

The Problem

A breast cancer researcher has RNA-seq expression data from tumor and adjacent normal tissue. For each of ~20,000 genes, she has FPKM values in both conditions. She wants to identify genes that are differentially expressed between tumor and normal.

Her first instinct: run a t-test on every gene, keep the ones with p < 0.05.

This approach has at least five problems. By the end of this chapter, you will understand all of them and know how to do it correctly.

Why It Matters

Comparing two groups is the most common statistical task in biology. Tumor vs normal. Treated vs untreated. Responder vs non-responder. Wild-type vs knockout. Case vs control.

Getting this comparison wrong has real consequences:

  • False positives waste years of follow-up experiments on genes that are not truly different
  • False negatives miss real biological signals — the drug target that was actually there
  • Ignoring effect size means you chase genes with tiny differences that are statistically significant only because of large sample size
  • Ignoring assumptions means the p-values you report are not calibrated — they do not mean what you think they mean

The t-test was invented in 1908 by William Sealy Gosset (publishing under the pseudonym “Student”) while working at the Guinness Brewery. He needed to compare small samples of barley yields. Over a century later, biologists still misuse his test daily.

The Data

We use GEO dataset GSE183947, which contains FPKM expression values from breast cancer (tumor) and adjacent normal tissue samples. This is a real RNA-seq dataset with paired tumor-normal samples.

Downloading the Data

# Download the breast cancer expression dataset
let url = "https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/series/GSE183nnn/GSE183947/suppl/GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz"
let raw_file = download(url, "GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz")
println("Downloaded: " + raw_file)

Loading and Inspecting

# Load the expression matrix
let expr = read_csv("GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz")

# Basic dimensions
println("Rows: " + to_string(len(expr)))
println("Columns: " + to_string(ncols(expr)))
println("Column names:")
let cols = columns(expr)
cols |> each(|c| println("  " + c))

# Preview the first few rows
take(expr, 5)

The data has genes in rows and samples in columns. Tumor samples and normal samples are identified by their column names. We need to separate them.

Preparing Tumor vs Normal Groups

# Identify tumor and normal sample columns
# In GSE183947, tumor samples contain "tumor" and normal contain "normal"
let all_cols = columns(expr)

# Separate sample columns (skip the gene ID column)
let tumor_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "tumor"))
let normal_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "normal"))

println("Tumor samples: " + to_string(len(tumor_cols)))
println("Normal samples: " + to_string(len(normal_cols)))

# Extract expression values for a single gene to demonstrate
# Let us start with TP53 — a well-known tumor suppressor
let tp53_row = expr |> filter_by("gene", |g| contains(g, "TP53"))

let tp53_tumor = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(tp53_row, c)) |> flatten()
let tp53_normal = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(tp53_row, c)) |> flatten()

println("TP53 tumor values: " + to_string(tp53_tumor))
println("TP53 normal values: " + to_string(tp53_normal))

Exploration

Before running any test, look at your data. This is not optional. Skipping exploration is the single most common source of wrong conclusions in genomics.

Summary Statistics

# Descriptive statistics for TP53 in both groups
println("=== TP53 Expression ===")
println("Tumor:")
println("  n     = " + to_string(len(tp53_tumor)))
println("  mean  = " + to_string(round(mean(tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("  median= " + to_string(round(median(tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("  sd    = " + to_string(round(stdev(tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("  min   = " + to_string(round(min(tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("  max   = " + to_string(round(max(tp53_tumor), 3)))

println("Normal:")
println("  n     = " + to_string(len(tp53_normal)))
println("  mean  = " + to_string(round(mean(tp53_normal), 3)))
println("  median= " + to_string(round(median(tp53_normal), 3)))
println("  sd    = " + to_string(round(stdev(tp53_normal), 3)))
println("  min   = " + to_string(round(min(tp53_normal), 3)))
println("  max   = " + to_string(round(max(tp53_normal), 3)))

Already you can see something important: are the standard deviations similar? If one group is much more variable than the other, we need Welch’s t-test, not Student’s t-test.

Visualizing the Distributions

# Histogram of TP53 expression in both groups
histogram(tp53_tumor, "TP53 Tumor Expression (FPKM)")
  |> save_plot("tp53_tumor_hist.svg")

histogram(tp53_normal, "TP53 Normal Expression (FPKM)")
  |> save_plot("tp53_normal_hist.svg")

# Side-by-side boxplot
let tp53_data = table(
  "expression", concat(tp53_tumor, tp53_normal),
  "group", concat(
    tp53_tumor |> map(|_| "Tumor"),
    tp53_normal |> map(|_| "Normal")
  )
)

boxplot(tp53_data, "group", "expression")
  |> save_plot("tp53_boxplot.svg")

The Log-Transform Question

Gene expression data is almost always right-skewed — a few genes have very high expression, most are moderate or low. This violates the normality assumption of the t-test.

# Raw expression is typically right-skewed
# Check skewness by comparing mean vs median
let skew_ratio = mean(tp53_tumor) / median(tp53_tumor)
println("Mean/median ratio (tumor): " + to_string(round(skew_ratio, 3)))
# If ratio >> 1, the data is right-skewed

The standard solution is to log-transform:

# Log2-transform with pseudocount to avoid log(0)
let log_tp53_tumor = tp53_tumor |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
let log_tp53_normal = tp53_normal |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

println("Log2 tumor mean:   " + to_string(round(mean(log_tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("Log2 tumor median: " + to_string(round(median(log_tp53_tumor), 3)))
println("Log2 normal mean:  " + to_string(round(mean(log_tp53_normal), 3)))
println("Log2 normal median:" + to_string(round(median(log_tp53_normal), 3)))

# QQ plot to check normality after log-transform
qq_plot(log_tp53_tumor, "TP53 Tumor (log2)")
  |> save_plot("tp53_tumor_qq.svg")

qq_plot(log_tp53_normal, "TP53 Normal (log2)")
  |> save_plot("tp53_normal_qq.svg")

A QQ plot plots the quantiles of your data against the quantiles of a normal distribution. If the points fall on a straight line, the data is approximately normal. Deviations at the tails indicate heavy or light tails.

Rule of thumb: Log-transformed expression data is usually close enough to normal for the t-test. Always check with a QQ plot.

The Analysis

Step 1: Student’s t-Test — The Classic

Student’s t-test assumes:

  1. Both groups are drawn from normal distributions
  2. Both groups have equal variance (homoscedasticity)
  3. Observations are independent
# Student's t-test on log-transformed TP53
let result = ttest(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)
println("=== Student's t-test (TP53 log2-FPKM) ===")
println("t-statistic: " + to_string(round(result.statistic, 4)))
println("p-value:     " + to_string(result.p_value))
println("df:          " + to_string(round(result.df, 1)))

But wait — did we check the equal variance assumption?

# Check variance ratio (F-ratio)
let var_tumor = variance(log_tp53_tumor)
let var_normal = variance(log_tp53_normal)
let f_ratio = var_tumor / var_normal
println("Variance tumor:  " + to_string(round(var_tumor, 4)))
println("Variance normal: " + to_string(round(var_normal, 4)))
println("F-ratio:         " + to_string(round(f_ratio, 4)))
# If F-ratio is far from 1 (say > 2 or < 0.5), variances are unequal

Step 2: Welch’s t-Test — The Safer Choice

Welch’s t-test does not assume equal variance. It adjusts the degrees of freedom based on the sample variances. In practice, Welch’s test is almost always preferred because:

  • When variances are equal, Welch’s gives nearly identical results to Student’s
  • When variances are unequal, Student’s gives wrong p-values but Welch’s is correct
  • There is almost no cost to using Welch’s and a real cost to using Student’s incorrectly
# Welch's t-test (default in most modern software)
# In BioLang, ttest performs Welch's by default
let welch = ttest(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)
println("=== Welch's t-test (TP53 log2-FPKM) ===")
println("t-statistic: " + to_string(round(welch.statistic, 4)))
println("p-value:     " + to_string(welch.p_value))
println("df:          " + to_string(round(welch.df, 1)))

Key insight: The degrees of freedom in Welch’s test are often not an integer. This is correct — it is the Welch-Satterthwaite approximation. If you see df = 14.7 instead of df = 18, that is Welch’s adjustment working.

Step 3: When to Use Paired Tests

If each tumor sample has a matched normal sample from the same patient, you should use a paired t-test. Pairing removes patient-to-patient variability and dramatically increases statistical power.

# If samples are paired (tumor-normal from same patient):
# The paired test operates on DIFFERENCES within each pair
let differences = zip(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)
  |> map(|pair| pair[0] - pair[1])

println("Mean of differences: " + to_string(round(mean(differences), 4)))
println("SD of differences:   " + to_string(round(stdev(differences), 4)))

# The paired t-test tests whether the mean difference is zero
let paired_result = ttest_paired(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)
println("=== Paired t-test (TP53 log2-FPKM) ===")
println("t-statistic: " + to_string(round(paired_result.statistic, 4)))
println("p-value:     " + to_string(paired_result.p_value))

When to pair: If the samples are naturally matched (same patient, same litter, same batch processed together), ALWAYS use the paired test. The unpaired test ignores this information and throws away statistical power.

When NOT to pair: If the samples are independent (different patients, no natural matching), the paired test is inappropriate and can give wrong results.

Step 4: Effect Size — Cohen’s d

A p-value tells you whether a difference is statistically distinguishable from zero. It does NOT tell you how large the difference is. With a large enough sample size, even a biologically meaningless 0.01-FPKM difference becomes “statistically significant.”

Cohen’s d measures effect size in units of standard deviation:

# Cohen's d for independent samples
# d = (mean1 - mean2) / pooled_sd
let mean_diff = mean(log_tp53_tumor) - mean(log_tp53_normal)
let n1 = to_float(len(log_tp53_tumor))
let n2 = to_float(len(log_tp53_normal))
let pooled_var = ((n1 - 1.0) * variance(log_tp53_tumor) + (n2 - 1.0) * variance(log_tp53_normal)) / (n1 + n2 - 2.0)
let pooled_sd = sqrt(pooled_var)
let cohens_d = mean_diff / pooled_sd

println("=== Effect Size ===")
println("Mean difference (log2): " + to_string(round(mean_diff, 4)))
println("Pooled SD:              " + to_string(round(pooled_sd, 4)))
println("Cohen's d:              " + to_string(round(cohens_d, 4)))

Interpreting Cohen’s d:

  • |d| < 0.2: negligible effect
  • |d| ~ 0.2: small effect
  • |d| ~ 0.5: medium effect
  • |d| ~ 0.8: large effect
  • |d| > 1.0: very large effect
# Effect size interpretation
let d_abs = abs(cohens_d)
let interpretation = if d_abs < 0.2 then "negligible"
  else if d_abs < 0.5 then "small"
  else if d_abs < 0.8 then "medium"
  else "large"

println("Effect size interpretation: " + interpretation)

Critical point: A gene with p = 0.001 and d = 0.1 is probably not biologically interesting. A gene with p = 0.06 and d = 1.2 probably is. Report both.

Step 5: Confidence Intervals

A confidence interval gives you a range of plausible values for the true difference. Unlike a p-value, it tells you about the magnitude of the effect.

# 95% confidence interval for the difference in means
let se_diff = sqrt(variance(log_tp53_tumor) / n1 + variance(log_tp53_normal) / n2)
let t_crit = qnorm(0.975)  # approximate for large samples
let ci_lower = mean_diff - t_crit * se_diff
let ci_upper = mean_diff + t_crit * se_diff

println("=== 95% Confidence Interval ===")
println("Difference in means (log2): " + to_string(round(mean_diff, 4)))
println("SE of difference:           " + to_string(round(se_diff, 4)))
println("95% CI: [" + to_string(round(ci_lower, 4)) + ", " + to_string(round(ci_upper, 4)) + "]")

If the confidence interval contains zero, the difference is not statistically significant at the 0.05 level. But notice how much MORE information the CI provides compared to a binary yes/no p-value.

Step 6: The Fold Change

In genomics, the fold change is the ratio of expression between conditions. On log2-transformed data, the fold change is simply the difference:

# Log2 fold change
let log2fc = mean(log_tp53_tumor) - mean(log_tp53_normal)
println("log2 fold change: " + to_string(round(log2fc, 4)))

# Convert back to fold change
let fc = pow(2.0, abs(log2fc))
let direction = if log2fc > 0.0 then "upregulated in tumor" else "downregulated in tumor"
println("Fold change: " + to_string(round(fc, 2)) + "x " + direction)

Step 7: Genome-Wide Analysis

Now let us scale up to all genes. This is where things get interesting.

# Run t-tests for every gene across the expression matrix
let gene_col = columns(expr) |> take(1)  # first column is gene ID
let genes = col_values(expr, gene_col[0])

# Build results table gene by gene
let results = genes |> map(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col[0], |g| g == gene)

  # Extract tumor and normal values
  let tumor_vals = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let normal_vals = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()

  # Log2 transform
  let log_tumor = tumor_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let log_normal = normal_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

  # Calculate statistics
  let log2fc = mean(log_tumor) - mean(log_normal)
  let test_result = ttest(log_tumor, log_normal)

  # Cohen's d
  let n_t = to_float(len(log_tumor))
  let n_n = to_float(len(log_normal))
  let pvar = ((n_t - 1.0) * variance(log_tumor) + (n_n - 1.0) * variance(log_normal)) / (n_t + n_n - 2.0)
  let d = if pvar > 0.0 then log2fc / sqrt(pvar) else 0.0

  record(
    "gene", gene,
    "log2fc", round(log2fc, 4),
    "t_stat", round(test_result.statistic, 4),
    "p_value", test_result.p_value,
    "cohens_d", round(d, 4),
    "mean_tumor", round(mean(log_tumor), 4),
    "mean_normal", round(mean(log_normal), 4)
  )
}) |> to_list()

let results_table = table(results)
println("Tested " + to_string(len(results_table)) + " genes")

# Sort by p-value to see top hits
let top_genes = results_table
  |> arrange("p_value")
  |> take(20)

println("=== Top 20 Genes by P-value ===")
top_genes

Step 8: The Volcano Plot

The volcano plot is the signature visualization of differential expression analysis. It plots log2 fold change (x-axis) against -log10 p-value (y-axis). Genes that are both statistically significant AND have large effect size appear in the upper corners.

# Create the volcano plot
let volcano_data = results_table
  |> mutate("neg_log10_p", |row| -log10(row.p_value + 1e-300))

volcano(volcano_data, "log2fc", "neg_log10_p")
  |> save_plot("breast_cancer_volcano.svg")

# Annotated volcano with significance thresholds
# Genes with |log2FC| > 1 AND p < 0.05 are "significant"
let sig_up = results_table
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| fc > 1.0)
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.05)

let sig_down = results_table
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| fc < -1.0)
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.05)

println("Upregulated (FC>2, p<0.05):   " + to_string(len(sig_up)))
println("Downregulated (FC<0.5, p<0.05): " + to_string(len(sig_down)))
println("Not significant:                " + to_string(
  len(results_table) - len(sig_up) - len(sig_down)
))

Step 9: Mann-Whitney U (Wilcoxon Rank-Sum) as Alternative

When the normality assumption is questionable, the Wilcoxon rank-sum test is a non-parametric alternative. It tests whether one group tends to have larger values than the other, without assuming any particular distribution shape.

# Wilcoxon rank-sum test on TP53
let wilcox_result = wilcoxon(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)
println("=== Wilcoxon Rank-Sum Test (TP53 log2-FPKM) ===")
println("W statistic: " + to_string(wilcox_result.statistic))
println("p-value:     " + to_string(wilcox_result.p_value))

Step 10: Head-to-Head — t-Test vs Wilcoxon

This is the real question: when do parametric and non-parametric tests disagree?

# Compare t-test and Wilcoxon across all genes
let comparison = genes |> map(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col[0], |g| g == gene)
  let tumor_vals = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let normal_vals = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()

  let log_tumor = tumor_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let log_normal = normal_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

  let t_result = ttest(log_tumor, log_normal)
  let w_result = wilcoxon(log_tumor, log_normal)

  record(
    "gene", gene,
    "p_ttest", t_result.p_value,
    "p_wilcoxon", w_result.p_value
  )
}) |> to_list()

let comp_table = table(comparison)

# Scatter plot: t-test p-values vs Wilcoxon p-values
let comp_log = comp_table
  |> mutate("log_p_t", |row| -log10(row.p_ttest + 1e-300))
  |> mutate("log_p_w", |row| -log10(row.p_wilcoxon + 1e-300))

scatter(comp_log, "log_p_t", "log_p_w")
  |> save_plot("ttest_vs_wilcoxon.svg")

# Find genes where the tests disagree
# Significant by t-test but not by Wilcoxon
let t_only = comp_table
  |> filter_by("p_ttest", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("p_wilcoxon", |p| p >= 0.05)

# Significant by Wilcoxon but not by t-test
let w_only = comp_table
  |> filter_by("p_wilcoxon", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("p_ttest", |p| p >= 0.05)

println("=== Disagreements ===")
println("Significant by t-test only:   " + to_string(len(t_only)))
println("Significant by Wilcoxon only: " + to_string(len(w_only)))
println("Agree on significance:        " + to_string(
  len(comp_table) - len(t_only) - len(w_only)
))

Why do they disagree?

The t-test is sensitive to outliers because it uses means and variances. A single extreme value can pull the mean and inflate the variance, changing the p-value substantially.

The Wilcoxon test uses ranks instead of raw values. An outlier that is 10x larger than the next value gets rank n regardless — its magnitude does not matter. This makes it robust to outliers but less powerful when the data truly is normal.

# Examine a gene where they disagree
let disagreement_genes = col_values(t_only, "gene")
if len(disagreement_genes) > 0 then {
  let check_gene = disagreement_genes[0]
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col[0], |g| g == check_gene)
  let tumor_vals = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let normal_vals = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()

  let log_tumor = tumor_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let log_normal = normal_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

  println("Gene: " + check_gene)
  println("Tumor values (log2): " + to_string(log_tumor))
  println("Normal values (log2): " + to_string(log_normal))

  # Check for outliers — values more than 3 SDs from mean
  let t_mean = mean(log_tumor)
  let t_sd = stdev(log_tumor)
  let outliers = log_tumor |> filter(|x| abs(x - t_mean) > 3.0 * t_sd)
  println("Outliers in tumor: " + to_string(len(outliers)))

  # Box plot to visualize
  let check_data = table(
    "expression", concat(log_tumor, log_normal),
    "group", concat(
      log_tumor |> map(|_| "Tumor"),
      log_normal |> map(|_| "Normal")
    )
  )
  boxplot(check_data, "group", "expression")
    |> save_plot("disagreement_gene_boxplot.svg")
}

Step 11: The One-Sample t-Test

Sometimes you want to test whether a single group differs from a known value. For instance: is the average log2 fold change across a pathway significantly different from zero?

# One-sample t-test: are the fold changes in a gene set different from zero?
# Let us test a set of known breast cancer genes
let bc_genes = ["BRCA1", "BRCA2", "TP53", "ERBB2", "ESR1", "PGR", "MKI67", "CDH1"]

let bc_fcs = bc_genes |> map(|gene| {
  let row = results_table |> filter_by("gene", |g| contains(g, gene))
  if len(row) > 0 then col_values(row, "log2fc")[0] else 0.0
}) |> filter(|x| x != 0.0)

println("Fold changes of BC genes: " + to_string(bc_fcs))
println("Mean log2FC: " + to_string(round(mean(bc_fcs), 4)))

# Test: is the mean FC significantly different from 0?
let one_sample = ttest_one(bc_fcs, 0.0)
println("=== One-sample t-test ===")
println("t-statistic: " + to_string(round(one_sample.statistic, 4)))
println("p-value:     " + to_string(one_sample.p_value))

Interpretation

What the P-value Actually Means

The p-value is the probability of observing data as extreme as (or more extreme than) what was observed, assuming the null hypothesis is true. It is NOT:

  • The probability that the null hypothesis is true
  • The probability that you made an error
  • The probability that the result will replicate

A p-value of 0.03 means: “If there were truly no difference between tumor and normal, data this extreme would occur about 3% of the time by chance.”

The Complete Report for a Gene

Never report just a p-value. A complete comparison includes:

# Complete report for TP53
let report_gene = "TP53"
let row = results_table |> filter_by("gene", |g| contains(g, report_gene))

println("=== Complete Report: " + report_gene + " ===")
println("Mean expression (log2 FPKM):")
println("  Tumor:  " + to_string(col_values(row, "mean_tumor")[0]))
println("  Normal: " + to_string(col_values(row, "mean_normal")[0]))
println("Log2 fold change: " + to_string(col_values(row, "log2fc")[0]))
println("Fold change: " + to_string(round(pow(2.0, abs(col_values(row, "log2fc")[0])), 2)) + "x")
println("Direction: " + if col_values(row, "log2fc")[0] > 0.0 then "up in tumor" else "down in tumor")
println("t-statistic: " + to_string(col_values(row, "t_stat")[0]))
println("p-value: " + to_string(col_values(row, "p_value")[0]))
println("Cohen's d: " + to_string(col_values(row, "cohens_d")[0]))
println("Note: p-value is NOT corrected for multiple testing (see Chapter 8)")

When the t-Test Finds Significance but the Biology Does Not

# Find genes with tiny fold changes but "significant" p-values
let stat_only = results_table
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.01)
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| abs(fc) < 0.3)
  |> arrange("p_value")

println("=== Statistically Significant but Biologically Questionable ===")
println("Genes with p < 0.01 but |log2FC| < 0.3:")
println("(These are less than 1.23-fold changes)")
take(stat_only, 10)

These genes are statistically different but the difference is so small it is unlikely to be biologically meaningful. This is exactly why effect size matters.

When Biology Happens Without Statistical Significance

# Find genes with large fold changes but non-significant p-values
let bio_only = results_table
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p > 0.05)
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| abs(fc) > 2.0)
  |> arrange("log2fc")

println("=== Biologically Large but Not Statistically Significant ===")
println("Genes with p > 0.05 but |log2FC| > 2 (4-fold change):")
take(bio_only, 10)

These genes have large fold changes but high variance or small sample sizes prevent the difference from being statistically significant. They may be worth investigating with more samples.

Decision

What Test Should You Use?

# Decision framework encoded in code:

# 1. Are the samples paired (same patient)?
let paired = true  # set based on experimental design

# 2. Check normality of the data (or log-data)
let check_normality = true  # QQ plot looked reasonable

# 3. Choose the test
let test_choice = if paired then {
  if check_normality then "paired t-test"
  else "Wilcoxon signed-rank"
} else {
  if check_normality then "Welch's t-test"
  else "Wilcoxon rank-sum"
}

println("Recommended test: " + test_choice)

Summary Table of All Methods

# Build a decision summary
let summary_table = table(
  "scenario", [
    "Paired, normal data",
    "Paired, non-normal data",
    "Unpaired, normal data",
    "Unpaired, non-normal data",
    "Unpaired, unequal variance"
  ],
  "test", [
    "Paired t-test",
    "Wilcoxon signed-rank",
    "Student's t-test",
    "Wilcoxon rank-sum",
    "Welch's t-test"
  ],
  "biolang_function", [
    "ttest_paired(x, y)",
    "wilcoxon(x, y)",
    "ttest(x, y)",
    "wilcoxon(x, y)",
    "ttest(x, y)"
  ],
  "note", [
    "Most powerful when pairing exists",
    "Uses ranks, robust to outliers",
    "Assumes equal variance",
    "No distribution assumption",
    "Default — safe with unequal variance"
  ]
)

summary_table

The Recommended Workflow

# Complete workflow for comparing two groups in genomics:

# 1. Log-transform expression data
# 2. Check normality with QQ plots
# 3. Check variance equality (compare SDs)
# 4. Use paired test if samples are matched
# 5. Use Welch's t-test as default (not Student's)
# 6. Always compute Cohen's d
# 7. Always compute confidence intervals
# 8. Always make a volcano plot
# 9. Do NOT interpret raw p-values when testing many genes
#    (see Chapter 8 for multiple testing correction)

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Using Student’s t-Test When Variances Are Unequal

# Simulate unequal variances
let group_a = rnorm(30, 5.0, 1.0)   # mean=5, sd=1
let group_b = rnorm(30, 5.5, 3.0)   # mean=5.5, sd=3

# Student's t-test ASSUMES equal variance
# Welch's does not
let t_student = ttest(group_a, group_b)
println("Welch's p-value: " + to_string(t_student.p_value))

# With unequal n AND unequal variance, Student's can be seriously wrong
let small_a = rnorm(10, 5.0, 1.0)
let large_b = rnorm(50, 5.5, 3.0)
let t_wrong = ttest(small_a, large_b)
println("Unequal n + unequal var: p = " + to_string(t_wrong.p_value))

Rule: Just use Welch’s t-test. Always. There is no good reason to use Student’s t-test in practice.

Pitfall 2: Testing Without Log-Transforming

# Demonstrate the danger of testing raw FPKM values
# Raw expression is heavily right-skewed
let raw_result = ttest(tp53_tumor, tp53_normal)
let log_result = ttest(log_tp53_tumor, log_tp53_normal)

println("=== Raw vs Log-transformed ===")
println("Raw FPKM t-test p-value: " + to_string(raw_result.p_value))
println("Log2 FPKM t-test p-value: " + to_string(log_result.p_value))
println("These can differ substantially!")

Pitfall 3: Confusing Statistical and Biological Significance

A gene with p = 1e-10 and fold change = 1.05 is statistically significant but biologically irrelevant. With enough samples, ANY non-zero difference becomes significant. Always report and filter by effect size.

Pitfall 4: Ignoring Multiple Testing

# Running 20,000 t-tests without correction
let n_tests = len(results_table)
let n_sig_raw = results_table
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> len()

let expected_false_pos = to_float(n_tests) * 0.05

println("=== The Multiple Testing Problem ===")
println("Total genes tested: " + to_string(n_tests))
println("Significant at p<0.05: " + to_string(n_sig_raw))
println("Expected false positives by chance: " + to_string(round(expected_false_pos, 0)))
println("See Chapter 8 for correction methods!")

Pitfall 5: Applying Paired Test to Unpaired Data (or Vice Versa)

# Paired test on unpaired data gives WRONG results
# If samples are not truly paired, the differences are meaningless

# Simulate: generate two independent groups
let independent_a = rnorm(20, 10.0, 2.0)
let independent_b = rnorm(20, 11.0, 2.0)

# Wrong: treating independent data as paired
let wrong_paired = ttest_paired(independent_a, independent_b)

# Correct: unpaired test
let correct_unpaired = ttest(independent_a, independent_b)

println("Wrong (paired on unpaired data): p = " + to_string(wrong_paired.p_value))
println("Correct (unpaired test):         p = " + to_string(correct_unpaired.p_value))

Pitfall 6: Cherry-Picking Genes

If you look at a gene because it has a large fold change and then test it, you are not doing an honest hypothesis test. You are confirming what you already saw in the data. This inflates false positive rates. Either test all genes (and correct for multiple testing) or have a pre-specified hypothesis.

Pitfall 7: The Pseudocount Matters

# The choice of pseudocount affects log2 fold change for lowly expressed genes
let low_expr = [0.0, 0.0, 0.1, 0.0, 0.2]

let log_pc1 = low_expr |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
let log_pc01 = low_expr |> map(|x| log2(x + 0.1))
let log_pc001 = low_expr |> map(|x| log2(x + 0.01))

println("Pseudocount = 1:    " + to_string(log_pc1))
println("Pseudocount = 0.1:  " + to_string(log_pc01))
println("Pseudocount = 0.01: " + to_string(log_pc001))
println("The pseudocount dramatically affects near-zero genes!")

Putting It All Together

Here is the complete analysis pipeline for comparing tumor vs normal expression:

# ============================================================
# Complete Two-Group Comparison Pipeline
# ============================================================

# 1. Load data
let url = "https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/series/GSE183nnn/GSE183947/suppl/GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz"
let expr = download(url, "GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz") |> read_csv()

# 2. Identify groups
let all_cols = columns(expr)
let tumor_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "tumor"))
let normal_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "normal"))
let gene_col = all_cols[0]

# 3. Genome-wide comparison
let results = col_values(expr, gene_col) |> map(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col, |g| g == gene)

  let tumor_vals = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let normal_vals = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()

  # Log-transform
  let lt = tumor_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let ln = normal_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

  # Test and effect size
  let log2fc = mean(lt) - mean(ln)
  let t_res = ttest(lt, ln)
  let w_res = wilcoxon(lt, ln)

  let nt = to_float(len(lt))
  let nn = to_float(len(ln))
  let pv = ((nt - 1.0) * variance(lt) + (nn - 1.0) * variance(ln)) / (nt + nn - 2.0)
  let d = if pv > 0.0 then log2fc / sqrt(pv) else 0.0

  record(
    "gene", gene,
    "log2fc", round(log2fc, 4),
    "p_ttest", t_res.p_value,
    "p_wilcoxon", w_res.p_value,
    "cohens_d", round(d, 4)
  )
}) |> to_list() |> table()

# 4. Volcano plot
let plot_data = results
  |> mutate("neg_log10_p", |row| -log10(row.p_ttest + 1e-300))

volcano(plot_data, "log2fc", "neg_log10_p")
  |> save_plot("complete_volcano.svg")

# 5. Summary
let sig = results
  |> filter_by("p_ttest", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| abs(fc) > 1.0)

println("=== Final Summary ===")
println("Total genes tested: " + to_string(len(results)))
println("Significant (p<0.05, |FC|>2): " + to_string(len(sig)))
println("WARNING: p-values are NOT adjusted for multiple testing")
println("See Chapter 8 before reporting these results!")

Where Next?

This chapter covered comparing two groups. But what happens when you have three, four, or five groups? You cannot simply run pairwise t-tests — that creates a multiple testing problem within a single comparison. Chapter 6 covers multi-group comparisons with ANOVA and Kruskal-Wallis.

We also noted repeatedly that testing 20,000 genes requires correction for multiple testing. Chapter 8 covers this critical topic — Bonferroni, Benjamini-Hochberg, and why the choice matters enormously.

And if your data is not normally distributed even after log-transformation, Chapter 7 covers non-parametric methods in detail.

Key Takeaways

  1. Use Welch’s t-test by default, not Student’s
  2. Log-transform expression data before testing
  3. Always compute effect size (Cohen’s d) alongside p-values
  4. Always compute confidence intervals — they tell you more than p-values
  5. Visualize with volcano plots — they combine fold change and significance
  6. Check your assumptions — QQ plots for normality, variance comparison
  7. Use paired tests when samples are naturally paired
  8. Consider Wilcoxon when normality is questionable
  9. Never interpret raw p-values from genome-wide tests (Chapter 8)
  10. Report the complete picture: mean, SD, fold change, CI, p-value, effect size

Exercises

  1. Download GSE183947 and identify the 10 most upregulated and 10 most downregulated genes. Report all six statistics (mean per group, log2FC, p-value, Cohen’s d, 95% CI).

  2. For the gene ERBB2 (HER2), compare the t-test result on raw FPKM vs log2-transformed FPKM. Which is more appropriate and why?

  3. Pick 5 genes where the t-test and Wilcoxon disagree. Make boxplots for each. What pattern do you see? (Hint: look for outliers.)

  4. Calculate Cohen’s d for all 20,000 genes. What percentage have |d| > 0.8? How does this compare to the percentage with p < 0.05?

  5. Simulate paired data: generate 20 pairs where each “normal” value is drawn from N(10, 2) and each “tumor” value is the paired normal value plus a random effect from N(1, 1). Run both paired and unpaired t-tests. Which is more powerful? Why?

Summary Table

ConceptKey Point
Student’s t-testAssumes equal variance — almost never use
Welch’s t-testDefault choice — handles unequal variance
Paired t-testUse when samples are matched — much more powerful
Wilcoxon rank-sumNon-parametric alternative — robust to outliers
Cohen’s dEffect size in SD units — report alongside p-value
Confidence intervalRange of plausible effect sizes — more informative than p-value
Log-transformationEssential for expression data — makes it approximately normal
Volcano plotCombines fold change and significance — the signature genomics plot
Multiple testingTesting 20,000 genes at p<0.05 yields ~1,000 false positives

Next: Chapter 6 — Multi-Group Comparisons

Multi-Group Comparisons

“If you torture the data long enough, it will confess to anything.” — Ronald Coase

You have expression data across five cancer subtypes. Or across 54 human tissues. Or across four treatment doses. The temptation is to run all pairwise t-tests and highlight the significant ones. This chapter explains why that approach is wrong and what to do instead.

The Problem

A researcher is studying tissue-specific gene expression using the GTEx (Genotype-Tissue Expression) dataset. She wants to know whether housekeeping genes like GAPDH truly have constant expression across tissues, and whether tissue-specific genes like albumin (ALB) show the expected patterns. She has median TPM values across 54 tissues.

Her first idea: compare every pair of tissues with a t-test. For 54 tissues, that is 54 * 53 / 2 = 1,431 pairwise comparisons. At alpha = 0.05, she expects about 72 false positives even if there is no real difference anywhere. And she is doing this for every gene.

She needs ANOVA.

Why It Matters

Multi-group comparisons arise constantly in biology:

  • Cancer subtypes: Is gene X differentially expressed across Luminal A, Luminal B, HER2+, Basal, and Normal-like breast cancer?
  • Tissue types: Does a drug candidate affect expression equally across liver, kidney, heart, and brain?
  • Dose responses: Does the treatment effect change across 0, 1, 5, 10, and 50 mg/kg?
  • Developmental stages: How does gene expression change across embryonic, neonatal, juvenile, and adult stages?
  • Species comparisons: Is a pathway conserved across human, mouse, rat, and zebrafish?

Running pairwise t-tests inflates the false positive rate. With k groups, you have k*(k-1)/2 pairs. The probability of at least one false positive is:

# Multiple pairwise comparisons inflate false positives
# Probability of at least one false positive with alpha=0.05

let n_groups = [3, 5, 10, 20, 54]
n_groups |> each(|k| {
  let n_pairs = k * (k - 1) / 2
  let p_no_fp = pow(1.0 - 0.05, to_float(n_pairs))
  let p_at_least_one = 1.0 - p_no_fp
  println("k=" + to_string(k) + " groups: "
    + to_string(n_pairs) + " pairs, "
    + "P(>=1 false positive) = "
    + to_string(round(p_at_least_one * 100.0, 1)) + "%")
})

With 5 groups: 10 pairs, 40% chance of a false positive. With 10 groups: 45 pairs, 90% chance. With 54 tissues: 1,431 pairs, virtually certain to have many false positives.

ANOVA solves this by asking a single question: “Is there ANY difference among the groups?” — with a single test at a single alpha level.

The Data

We use the GTEx v8 gene median TPM dataset, which contains median expression values (in TPM) for every gene across 54 human tissues.

Downloading the Data

# Download GTEx median TPM by tissue
let url = "https://storage.googleapis.com/adult-gtex/bulk-gex/v8/rna-seq/GTEx_Analysis_2017-06-05_v8_RNASeQCv1.1.9_gene_median_tpm.gct.gz"
let raw_file = download(url, "GTEx_v8_median_tpm.gct.gz")
println("Downloaded: " + raw_file)

Loading and Inspecting

GCT format has two header lines before the data matrix. We need to handle this:

# Read the GCT file (tab-separated with header rows)
# GCT files have 2 header lines, then column names, then data
let gtex = read_tsv("GTEx_v8_median_tpm.gct.gz")

println("Rows (genes): " + to_string(len(gtex)))
println("Columns: " + to_string(ncols(gtex)))

# Preview column names — these are tissue names
let cols = columns(gtex)
println("First 10 columns:")
cols |> take(10) |> each(|c| println("  " + c))

# List all tissue types
# First two columns are typically Name (gene ID) and Description (gene symbol)
let tissue_cols = cols |> filter(|c| c != "Name" and c != "Description")
println("Number of tissues: " + to_string(len(tissue_cols)))
println("Tissues:")
tissue_cols |> enumerate() |> each(|pair| {
  println("  " + to_string(pair[0] + 1) + ". " + pair[1])
})

Understanding TPM

TPM (Transcripts Per Million) is a normalized expression measure. Unlike raw counts, TPM values are comparable across samples because they account for gene length and sequencing depth.

# Verify TPM normalization: should sum to ~1,000,000 per tissue
# (approximately, since these are median values across samples)
let first_tissue = tissue_cols[0]
