FDR is the expected proportion of false positives among all rejected hypotheses. Instead of controlling the probability of ANY false positive (FWER), FDR controls the RATE of false positives among your discoveries.

This is a fundamentally different question:

  • FWER: “What is the probability I made even one mistake?”
  • FDR: “Among the genes I call significant, what fraction are mistakes?”

For genomics, FDR is almost always more appropriate. If you call 500 genes significant at FDR = 0.05, you expect about 25 of them (5%) to be false positives. That is a tolerable error rate for most applications.

# Benjamini-Hochberg (BH) correction
let bh_pvals = p_adjust(pvals, "bh")

let n_bh_sig = bh_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
println("=== Benjamini-Hochberg (FDR) Correction ===")
println("Significant at FDR < 0.05: " + to_string(n_bh_sig) + " genes")
println("Significant at FDR < 0.01: " + to_string(
  bh_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len()
) + " genes")
println("Significant at FDR < 0.10: " + to_string(
  bh_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.10) |> len()
) + " genes")

# Compare all methods side by side
println("=== Comparison of Correction Methods ===")
println("Method          | Significant at 0.05 | Controls")
println("----------------|--------------------|---------")
println("None (raw)      | " + to_string(pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len())
  + "               | Nothing")
println("Bonferroni      | " + to_string(n_bonf_sig)
  + "               | FWER")
println("Holm            | " + to_string(n_holm_sig)
  + "               | FWER")
println("BH (FDR)        | " + to_string(n_bh_sig)
  + "               | FDR")

How BH works:

  1. Sort all m p-values from smallest to largest: p(1), p(2), …, p(m)
  2. For each p(i), compute the BH threshold: i/m * alpha
  3. Find the largest i where p(i) <= i/m * alpha
  4. Reject all hypotheses with p-values <= p(i)
# Demonstrate the BH procedure step by step
let sorted_pvals = pvals |> sort()
let m = len(sorted_pvals)
let alpha = 0.05

# Find the BH threshold
let bh_thresholds = sorted_pvals |> enumerate() |> map(|pair| {
  let i = pair[0] + 1  # 1-indexed rank
  let p = pair[1]
  let threshold = to_float(i) / to_float(m) * alpha
  record("rank", i, "p_value", p, "threshold", threshold,
         "reject", p <= threshold)
})

# Show the first 20 and last few before the cutoff
println("=== BH Procedure (first 20 genes) ===")
println("Rank | P-value      | Threshold    | Reject?")
bh_thresholds |> take(20) |> each(|row| {
  println(to_string(row.rank) + "    | "
    + to_string(row.p_value) + " | "
    + to_string(round(row.threshold, 8)) + " | "
    + to_string(row.reject))
})

Step 4: BY Correction for Correlated Tests

The BH procedure assumes independence between tests. But genes are correlated — co-regulated genes, pathway members, and genomic neighbors often have correlated expression. The Benjamini-Yekutieli (BY) correction handles arbitrary dependence structure.

# BY correction (for correlated tests)
let by_pvals = p_adjust(pvals, "by")

let n_by_sig = by_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
println("=== Benjamini-Yekutieli (BY) Correction ===")
println("Significant at FDR < 0.05: " + to_string(n_by_sig) + " genes")
println("")
println("Comparison:")
println("  BH (assumes independence): " + to_string(n_bh_sig) + " genes")
println("  BY (allows correlation):   " + to_string(n_by_sig) + " genes")
println("  BY is more conservative than BH")

In practice, BH works well even with moderate correlation. BY is overly conservative for most genomics applications. Use BY only when you have strong prior reason to believe tests are heavily correlated.

Step 5: Independent Filtering — Improving Power

Here is a counterintuitive fact: you can INCREASE your power to detect true differences by REMOVING genes BEFORE testing. Specifically, removing genes with very low expression (low mean, low variance) removes true nulls that add noise to the multiple testing correction without contributing any signal.

# Independent filtering: remove low-expression genes before testing

# Step 1: compute mean expression across all samples
let mean_expr = genes |> map(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col, |g| g == gene)
  let all_vals = concat(tumor_cols, normal_cols) |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let log_mean = mean(all_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0)))
  record("gene", gene, "mean_expr", log_mean)
}) |> to_list() |> table()

# Step 2: filter out lowly expressed genes (mean log2 FPKM < 1)
let expressed = mean_expr
  |> filter_by("mean_expr", |m| m >= 1.0)

let n_expressed = len(expressed)
let n_filtered = len(mean_expr) - n_expressed

println("=== Independent Filtering ===")
println("Total genes: " + to_string(len(mean_expr)))
println("Expressed (mean log2 >= 1): " + to_string(n_expressed))
println("Filtered out: " + to_string(n_filtered))

# Re-run BH on only expressed genes
let expressed_genes = col_values(expressed, "gene")
let expressed_results = results
  |> filter_by("gene", |g| expressed_genes |> contains(g))

let expressed_pvals = col_values(expressed_results, "p_value")
let expressed_bh = p_adjust(expressed_pvals, "bh")

let n_expressed_sig = expressed_bh |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()

println("=== Effect of Independent Filtering on BH ===")
println("BH on ALL genes:       " + to_string(n_bh_sig) + " significant")
println("BH on expressed genes: " + to_string(n_expressed_sig) + " significant")
println("Filtering INCREASED discoveries by: " + to_string(n_expressed_sig - n_bh_sig))
println("")
println("This works because:")
println("  - Low-expression genes are rarely truly differential")
println("  - Removing them reduces the number of tests m")
println("  - The BH threshold i/m * alpha becomes larger (less strict)")
println("  - True positives that were borderline now pass the threshold")

Important: The filtering criterion must be INDEPENDENT of the test statistic. You cannot filter by p-value first and then re-correct — that is circular. Mean expression is independent of the difference between groups, so it is a valid filter.

Step 6: Visualizing the Correction Effect

# Scatter: raw p-value vs BH-adjusted p-value
let pval_comparison = table(
  "raw_p", pvals |> map(|p| -log10(p + 1e-300)),
  "bh_p", bh_pvals |> map(|p| -log10(p + 1e-300))
)

scatter(pval_comparison, "raw_p", "bh_p")
  |> save_plot("raw_vs_bh_pvalues.svg")

# Volcano plots: raw vs corrected
# Raw p-values
let volcano_raw = results
  |> mutate("neg_log10_p", |row| -log10(row.p_value + 1e-300))

volcano(volcano_raw, "log2fc", "neg_log10_p")
  |> save_plot("volcano_raw.svg")

# BH-adjusted — add adjusted p-values to results
let results_with_bh = results
  |> mutate("bh_p", |row| {
    let idx = col_values(results, "gene") |> enumerate()
      |> filter(|pair| pair[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then bh_pvals[idx[0][0]] else 1.0
  })
  |> mutate("neg_log10_bh", |row| -log10(row.bh_p + 1e-300))

volcano(results_with_bh, "log2fc", "neg_log10_bh")
  |> save_plot("volcano_bh.svg")

# Show genes that change status after correction
let sig_raw_only = results_with_bh
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("bh_p", |p| p >= 0.05)

let sig_both = results_with_bh
  |> filter_by("p_value", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("bh_p", |p| p < 0.05)

println("=== Correction Impact ===")
println("Significant by raw p < 0.05 only: " + to_string(len(sig_raw_only)))
println("Significant by both raw and BH:   " + to_string(len(sig_both)))
println("These " + to_string(len(sig_raw_only)) + " genes were likely false positives")

Step 7: The Pi-Zero Concept

Pi-zero (pi_0) is the estimated proportion of true null hypotheses. If you test 20,000 genes and 15,000 are truly not different (pi_0 = 0.75), then 5,000 are truly different. Estimating pi_0 tells you how much signal is in your data.

# Estimating pi_0 from the p-value histogram
# The flat region of the p-value histogram estimates pi_0

# Under the null, p-values are uniform
# Under the alternative, p-values are concentrated near 0
# The height of the flat region (say p > 0.5) estimates pi_0

let n_total = len(pvals)
let n_above_05 = pvals |> filter(|p| p > 0.5) |> len()

# If p-values were uniform, half would be above 0.5
# The fraction above 0.5 estimates pi_0
let pi0_estimate = to_float(n_above_05) / to_float(n_total) * 2.0

println("=== Estimating pi_0 (proportion of true nulls) ===")
println("Total genes: " + to_string(n_total))
println("P-values > 0.5: " + to_string(n_above_05))
println("Estimated pi_0: " + to_string(round(pi0_estimate, 3)))
println("Estimated true positives: " + to_string(round(to_float(n_total) * (1.0 - pi0_estimate), 0)))
println("Estimated true nulls: " + to_string(round(to_float(n_total) * pi0_estimate, 0)))

# Use different cutoffs to verify pi_0 estimate stability
let cutoffs = [0.3, 0.4, 0.5, 0.6, 0.7, 0.8, 0.9]
println("Pi_0 estimates at different cutoffs:")
cutoffs |> each(|lambda| {
  let n_above = pvals |> filter(|p| p > lambda) |> len()
  let pi0 = to_float(n_above) / (to_float(n_total) * (1.0 - lambda))
  println("  lambda = " + to_string(lambda) + ": pi_0 = " + to_string(round(pi0, 3)))
})

Step 8: Understanding What FDR = 0.05 Really Means

This is the most commonly misunderstood concept in genomics. When you set FDR = 0.05 and find 500 significant genes:

  • It does NOT mean each gene has a 5% probability of being a false positive
  • It DOES mean that among your 500 genes, approximately 25 (5%) are expected to be false positives
  • It does NOT guarantee exactly 25 false positives — it is an expected value
# What FDR = 0.05 means in practice

let fdr_thresholds = [0.01, 0.05, 0.10, 0.20]
println("=== FDR Interpretation ===")
fdr_thresholds |> each(|fdr| {
  let n_sig = bh_pvals |> filter(|p| p < fdr) |> len()
  let expected_fp = round(to_float(n_sig) * fdr, 1)
  let expected_tp = round(to_float(n_sig) * (1.0 - fdr), 1)

  println("FDR < " + to_string(fdr) + ":")
  println("  Significant genes: " + to_string(n_sig))
  println("  Expected false positives: " + to_string(expected_fp)
    + " (" + to_string(round(fdr * 100.0, 0)) + "% of discoveries)")
  println("  Expected true positives: " + to_string(expected_tp))
  println("")
})

# The q-value: gene-specific FDR
# The BH-adjusted p-value for a gene IS its q-value
# q = 0.03 means: if you call all genes with q <= 0.03 significant,
# 3% of those calls are expected to be false

# Example genes
let example_genes = ["TP53", "BRCA1", "GAPDH", "ERBB2", "ESR1",
                     "MKI67", "CDH1", "PGR"]

println("=== Q-values for Known Genes ===")
example_genes |> each(|gene| {
  let row = results_with_bh
    |> filter_by("gene", |g| contains(g, gene))
  if len(row) > 0 then {
    let raw_p = col_values(row, "p_value")[0]
    let bh_q = col_values(row, "bh_p")[0]
    let fc = col_values(row, "log2fc")[0]
    println(gene + ": raw p = " + to_string(raw_p)
      + ", q-value = " + to_string(round(bh_q, 6))
      + ", log2FC = " + to_string(fc))
  }
})

Step 9: P-Value Histogram Diagnostics

The shape of the p-value histogram tells you about your data quality:

# Different p-value histogram shapes and what they mean

# Shape 1: Spike at 0 + flat = good data with real signal
println("Shape 1: Spike near 0 + flat elsewhere")
println("  Interpretation: True signal exists")
println("  Action: Apply BH correction and proceed")
println("")

# Shape 2: Completely flat = no signal (all null)
println("Shape 2: Completely uniform/flat")
println("  Interpretation: No genes are differentially expressed")
println("  Action: No correction needed — nothing is significant")
println("")

# Shape 3: Spike at 0 AND spike at 1
println("Shape 3: Spikes at both 0 and 1")
println("  Interpretation: Anti-conservative p-values near 1")
println("  Possible cause: Discrete test statistics, low counts")
println("  Action: Use a different test or filter low-count genes")
println("")

# Shape 4: U-shaped (high at both ends)
println("Shape 4: U-shaped distribution")
println("  Interpretation: Batch effects or confounders")
println("  Action: Check for technical artifacts BEFORE testing")
println("")

# Shape 5: Bump in the middle
println("Shape 5: Bump around 0.5")
println("  Interpretation: Correlation structure among tests")
println("  Action: Consider BY correction or decorrelation")

# Quantify the shape of our actual p-value distribution
let n_bins = 20
let bin_width = 1.0 / to_float(n_bins)

let bin_counts = range(0, n_bins) |> map(|i| {
  let lower = to_float(i) * bin_width
  let upper = lower + bin_width
  let count = pvals |> filter(|p| p >= lower and p < upper) |> len()
  record("bin", to_string(round(lower, 2)) + "-" + to_string(round(upper, 2)),
         "count", count,
         "expected", round(to_float(n_total) / to_float(n_bins), 0))
})

let bin_table = table(bin_counts |> to_list())
println("=== P-value Histogram (20 bins) ===")
bin_table

Step 10: Applying Corrections Genome-Wide

# Complete corrected results for all genes
let all_pvals = col_values(results, "p_value")

let bonf = p_adjust(all_pvals, "bonferroni")
let holm = p_adjust(all_pvals, "holm")
let bh = p_adjust(all_pvals, "bh")
let by = p_adjust(all_pvals, "by")

# Summary across all methods
let summary = table(
  "method", ["None", "Bonferroni", "Holm", "BH", "BY"],
  "controls", ["Nothing", "FWER", "FWER", "FDR", "FDR (correlated)"],
  "sig_005", [
    all_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len(),
    bonf |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len(),
    holm |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len(),
    bh |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len(),
    by |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
  ],
  "sig_001", [
    all_pvals |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len(),
    bonf |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len(),
    holm |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len(),
    bh |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len(),
    by |> filter(|p| p < 0.01) |> len()
  ]
)

println("=== Correction Method Comparison ===")
summary

# Write complete results to file
let final_results = results
  |> mutate("p_bonferroni", |row| {
    let idx = genes |> enumerate() |> filter(|p| p[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then bonf[idx[0][0]] else 1.0
  })
  |> mutate("p_holm", |row| {
    let idx = genes |> enumerate() |> filter(|p| p[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then holm[idx[0][0]] else 1.0
  })
  |> mutate("p_bh", |row| {
    let idx = genes |> enumerate() |> filter(|p| p[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then bh[idx[0][0]] else 1.0
  })
  |> mutate("p_by", |row| {
    let idx = genes |> enumerate() |> filter(|p| p[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then by[idx[0][0]] else 1.0
  })

# Sort by BH-adjusted p-value
let final_sorted = final_results |> arrange("p_bh")

write_tsv(final_sorted, "differential_expression_results.csv")
println("Results written to differential_expression_results.csv")

Interpretation

When to Use Which Method

# Decision framework for multiple testing correction

let decision_table = table(
  "situation", [
    "GWAS (must avoid any false positive)",
    "Differential expression (tolerate some FP)",
    "Exploratory screen (maximize discoveries)",
    "Correlated tests (pathway genes)",
    "Follow-up study (few candidate genes)",
    "Publication requirement"
  ],
  "recommended", [
    "Bonferroni (FWER < 5e-8 by convention)",
    "BH (FDR < 0.05)",
    "BH (FDR < 0.10 or 0.20)",
    "BY (FDR for dependent tests)",
    "Bonferroni (small m makes it reasonable)",
    "BH is standard; report raw and adjusted"
  ],
  "why", [
    "False positives trigger expensive follow-up",
    "5% FP rate is acceptable for gene lists",
    "Cast a wide net, validate later",
    "BH assumes independence, BY does not",
    "With m < 50, Bonferroni is not too strict",
    "Reviewers expect FDR control"
  ]
)

decision_table

The GWAS Threshold

In genome-wide association studies, the convention is p < 5e-8, which corresponds to a Bonferroni correction for approximately 1 million independent tests (the effective number of independent SNPs in the genome after accounting for linkage disequilibrium).

# GWAS significance threshold
let gwas_threshold = 5e-8
let n_snps = 1000000

# This is approximately Bonferroni for 1M tests
let bonf_threshold = 0.05 / to_float(n_snps)
println("GWAS convention: p < 5e-8")
println("Bonferroni for 1M tests: p < " + to_string(bonf_threshold))
println("These are approximately equal")
println("")
println("This threshold has served the GWAS community well")
println("because false positives are very costly (replication studies)")

Reporting Results

# How to properly report multiple testing results

println("=== Reporting Checklist ===")
println("1. State the total number of tests performed")
println("2. State the correction method used (BH for FDR, etc.)")
println("3. Report BOTH raw and adjusted p-values")
println("4. State the significance threshold (e.g., FDR < 0.05)")
println("5. Report the number of significant results")
println("6. Show the p-value histogram as a supplementary figure")
println("7. If you filtered before testing, describe the filter")
println("8. Report effect sizes (log2FC, Cohen's d) alongside p-values")
println("9. Include a volcano plot (log2FC vs -log10 adjusted p)")
println("10. Acknowledge the expected number of false discoveries")

# Example results paragraph (fill in your numbers):
let n_tested = len(results)
let n_sig_bh = bh |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
let n_up = final_sorted
  |> filter_by("p_bh", |p| p < 0.05)
  |> filter_by("log2fc", |fc| fc > 0.0)
  |> len()
let n_down = n_sig_bh - n_up

println("Example results text:")
println("'We tested " + to_string(n_tested) + " genes for differential")
println("expression between tumor and normal tissue using Welch's t-test")
println("on log2-transformed FPKM values. P-values were corrected for")
println("multiple testing using the Benjamini-Hochberg procedure to")
println("control the false discovery rate. At FDR < 0.05, we identified")
println(to_string(n_sig_bh) + " differentially expressed genes")
println("(" + to_string(n_up) + " upregulated, " + to_string(n_down)
  + " downregulated in tumor).'")

Decision

The Complete Multiple Testing Pipeline

# ============================================================
# Complete Multiple Testing Correction Pipeline
# ============================================================

# 1. Load and prepare data
let url = "https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/series/GSE183nnn/GSE183947/suppl/GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz"
let expr = download(url, "GSE183947_fpkm.csv.gz") |> read_csv()

let all_cols = columns(expr)
let tumor_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "tumor"))
let normal_cols = all_cols |> filter(|c| contains(c, "normal"))
let gene_col = all_cols[0]
let genes = col_values(expr, gene_col)

# 2. Pre-filter: remove lowly expressed genes (INDEPENDENT of test)
let keep_genes = genes |> filter(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col, |g| g == gene)
  let all_vals = concat(tumor_cols, normal_cols)
    |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
  let log_mean = mean(all_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0)))
  log_mean >= 1.0
})

println("Genes before filter: " + to_string(len(genes)))
println("Genes after filter:  " + to_string(len(keep_genes)))

# 3. Test filtered genes
let filtered_results = keep_genes |> map(|gene| {
  let row = expr |> filter_by(gene_col, |g| g == gene)
  let lt = tumor_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
    |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let ln = normal_cols |> map(|c| col_values(row, c)) |> flatten()
    |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))
  let fc = mean(lt) - mean(ln)
  let t = ttest(lt, ln)
  record("gene", gene, "log2fc", round(fc, 4), "p_value", t.p_value)
}) |> to_list() |> table()

# 4. Correct for multiple testing
let raw_p = col_values(filtered_results, "p_value")
let adj_p = p_adjust(raw_p, "bh")

# 5. Inspect p-value histogram
histogram(raw_p, "P-value Distribution (filtered genes)")
  |> save_plot("pval_hist_filtered.svg")

# 6. Apply threshold and report
let n_sig = adj_p |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
let expected_fp = round(to_float(n_sig) * 0.05, 0)

println("=== Final Results ===")
println("Genes tested: " + to_string(len(keep_genes)))
println("Significant (FDR < 0.05): " + to_string(n_sig))
println("Expected false discoveries: ~" + to_string(expected_fp))

# 7. Final volcano plot
let final_data = filtered_results
  |> mutate("neg_log10_fdr", |row| {
    let idx = col_values(filtered_results, "gene") |> enumerate()
      |> filter(|p| p[1] == row.gene)
    if len(idx) > 0 then -log10(adj_p[idx[0][0]] + 1e-300) else 0.0
  })

volcano(final_data, "log2fc", "neg_log10_fdr")
  |> save_plot("final_volcano.svg")

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Not Correcting at All

# The most common mistake: reporting raw p-values from genome-wide tests
println("If you test 20,000 genes and report raw p < 0.05:")
println("  You WILL have hundreds of false positives")
println("  Reviewers WILL reject your paper")
println("  The results CANNOT be trusted")
println("")
println("ALWAYS correct for multiple testing in genome-wide analyses")

Pitfall 2: Applying Bonferroni When BH Would Be Better

# Bonferroni is appropriate when:
# - You have few tests (< 50)
# - False positives are very costly (drug targets, clinical decisions)
# - You need a simple, universally accepted method

# Bonferroni is NOT appropriate when:
# - You have thousands of tests (genomics)
# - You can tolerate some false positives
# - You want to maximize discoveries for follow-up

println("With 20,000 genes:")
println("  Bonferroni threshold: p < " + to_string(0.05 / 20000.0))
println("  BH can identify 10x more genes at the same overall error rate")
println("  Use BH for discovery, Bonferroni for validation")

Pitfall 3: Filtering AFTER Testing

# WRONG: test all genes, then filter by expression level
# This biases the correction because you counted tests you then excluded

# RIGHT: filter by expression BEFORE testing
# The filter must be INDEPENDENT of the test statistic

# Common independent filters:
println("Valid independent filters:")
println("  - Mean expression across all samples")
println("  - Variance across all samples")
println("  - Gene length")
println("  - GC content")
println("")
println("INVALID filters (dependent on test):")
println("  - P-value (circular!)")
println("  - Fold change (correlated with test statistic)")
println("  - Expression in only one group")

Pitfall 4: Confusing FDR with Per-Gene Error Probability

# A common misinterpretation:
# "This gene has FDR = 0.03, so there's a 3% chance it's a false positive"
# WRONG!

# The correct interpretation:
# "If I call all genes with FDR <= 0.03 significant,
#  3% of those genes are expected to be false positives"

# FDR is a PROPERTY OF THE LIST, not of individual genes
println("FDR = 0.05 means:")
println("  Among ALL genes you call significant at this threshold,")
println("  5% are expected to be false discoveries.")
println("")
println("It does NOT mean:")
println("  Each individual gene has a 5% chance of being false.")

Pitfall 5: Multiple Testing Across Multiple Analyses

# You run DE analysis, then pathway analysis, then GO enrichment,
# then network analysis on the same dataset.
# Each analysis has its own multiple testing correction.
# But you are also testing multiple ANALYSES!

println("The meta-analysis problem:")
println("  1. Differential expression: 20,000 tests (BH corrected)")
println("  2. Pathway enrichment: 300 pathways (BH corrected)")
println("  3. GO enrichment: 5,000 terms (BH corrected)")
println("  4. Motif enrichment: 800 motifs (BH corrected)")
println("")
println("Each is individually corrected, but you ran 4 analyses.")
println("If each has a 5% FDR, the combined FDR is higher.")
println("")
println("In practice, this is usually acknowledged in the discussion")
println("rather than formally corrected. Pre-registration helps.")

Pitfall 6: The Replication Trap

# "We found 500 genes at FDR < 0.05 in dataset 1.
#  We then tested just these 500 genes in dataset 2.
#  We corrected for 500 tests (not 20,000)."
#
# This is actually CORRECT and is the standard approach.
# The replication study tests a pre-specified set of hypotheses.
# The multiple testing burden is only for the number of hypotheses tested.

println("Replication analysis:")
println("  Discovery: test 20,000 genes, correct for 20,000")
println("  Replication: test only the significant ones, correct for THOSE")
println("  This is valid because the hypotheses were pre-specified")

Pitfall 7: Thresholding-Induced Artifacts

# Using a hard FDR threshold creates artificial boundaries
# Gene A with FDR = 0.049 is "significant"
# Gene B with FDR = 0.051 is "not significant"
# But they are essentially identical

# Better approaches:
println("Instead of binary significant/not-significant:")
println("  1. Report the full ranked list with FDR values")
println("  2. Use gene set enrichment (which avoids thresholds)")
println("  3. Report results at multiple FDR thresholds")
println("  4. Focus on effect size (fold change) alongside FDR")
println("  5. Use volcano plots that show the continuous relationship")

Putting It All Together

# ============================================================
# Summary: The Multiple Testing Decision Tree
# ============================================================

println("1. Run your tests on all features (genes, variants, etc.)")
println("2. Plot the p-value histogram")
println("   - Spike at 0 + flat = good signal, proceed")
println("   - Flat = no signal, stop")
println("   - U-shaped = batch effects, fix first")
println("")
println("3. Consider independent filtering (remove noise features)")
println("   - Filter by mean expression, variance, or coverage")
println("   - Filter must be INDEPENDENT of the test")
println("")
println("4. Choose correction method:")
println("   - Few tests (< 50): Bonferroni")
println("   - Genomics (discovery): BH (FDR)")
println("   - Correlated tests: BY")
println("   - GWAS: Bonferroni at 5e-8 (convention)")
println("")
println("5. Report:")
println("   - Number of tests")
println("   - Correction method")
println("   - Both raw and adjusted p-values")
println("   - Number of discoveries at chosen FDR")
println("   - Expected false discoveries")
println("   - Effect sizes alongside p-values")
println("   - P-value histogram (supplementary)")
println("   - Volcano plot (main figure)")

Where Next?

This chapter covered the correction side of hypothesis testing. But the underlying tests (t-test, ANOVA, Wilcoxon) are all about comparing groups. Chapter 9 moves to correlation and regression — modeling relationships between continuous variables, which is the foundation for more complex analyses.

Chapters 10-12 build on regression to handle the specific challenges of genomics data: generalized linear models for count data (Chapter 10), survival analysis for time-to-event data (Chapter 11), and batch effects and confounders (Chapter 12).

Key Takeaways

  1. Testing 20,000 genes at p < 0.05 gives ~1,000 false positives by chance
  2. FWER controls the probability of ANY false positive (Bonferroni, Holm)
  3. FDR controls the RATE of false positives among discoveries (BH, BY)
  4. Bonferroni is too conservative for genomics — use BH instead
  5. BH is the workhorse of genomics multiple testing correction
  6. Independent filtering improves power without inflating false positives
  7. The p-value histogram is the key diagnostic — always plot it
  8. Pi-zero estimates the fraction of true nulls in your data
  9. FDR is a property of the list, not of individual genes
  10. Report both raw and adjusted p-values alongside effect sizes

Exercises

  1. Download GSE183947 and run t-tests on all genes. Compare the number of significant genes using Bonferroni, Holm, BH, and BY at alpha = 0.05. Make a table comparing the methods.

  2. Plot the p-value histogram with 50 bins. Estimate pi_0 using the flat region above 0.5. How many genes do you estimate are truly differentially expressed?

  3. Apply independent filtering with three different mean expression thresholds (log2 FPKM > 0, > 1, > 2). How does each threshold affect the number of BH discoveries? Is there a sweet spot?

  4. For the top 20 genes by BH-adjusted p-value, report: gene name, log2FC, raw p-value, BH q-value, Bonferroni-adjusted p-value. How many are significant by both methods?

  5. Simulate: generate 20,000 p-values where 19,000 are from Uniform(0,1) and 1,000 are from Beta(0.1, 1) (enriched near 0). Apply BH at FDR = 0.05. How many true positives and false positives do you find? What is the actual FDR?

Summary Table

ConceptKey Point
Multiple testingTesting many hypotheses inflates false positives
FWERProbability of any false positive — controlled by Bonferroni
FDRExpected false positive rate among discoveries — controlled by BH
BonferroniDivide alpha by m — simple but very conservative
HolmStep-down Bonferroni — always at least as powerful
Benjamini-HochbergFDR control — the standard in genomics
BYFDR for correlated tests — more conservative than BH
Independent filteringRemove noise features to improve BH power
P-value histogramKey diagnostic — check shape before correction
Pi-zeroProportion of true nulls — estimated from flat region
Q-valueBH-adjusted p-value — gene-specific FDR

Next: Chapter 9 — Correlation & Regression

Correlation & Regression

“Correlation does not imply causation, but it does waggle its eyebrows suggestively and gesture furtively while mouthing ‘look over there.’” — Randall Munroe

The Problem

You have RNA-seq expression data and clinical outcomes for over a thousand breast cancer patients from TCGA. A collaborator asks: “Does ESR1 expression predict survival? What about ERBB2? Can we build a model that uses expression of a handful of genes to predict patient outcomes?”

These are correlation and regression questions. Before you can answer them, you need to understand what correlation actually measures, when it misleads, and how regression lets you disentangle the effects of multiple variables acting simultaneously.

This chapter takes you from pairwise correlation to multiple regression, using real TCGA breast cancer data throughout.

Why It Matters

Clinical Decisions Depend on Associations

Breast cancer treatment has been revolutionized by molecular markers. The estrogen receptor (ER), progesterone receptor (PR), and HER2 (ERBB2) status determine treatment strategy. These markers were discovered through correlation studies: researchers noticed that expression of certain genes correlated with treatment response and survival.

But not every correlation is a useful biomarker. Thousands of genes correlate with survival in breast cancer — most because they correlate with subtype, which correlates with survival. The challenge is distinguishing direct, useful associations from indirect, confounded ones.

The Stakes of Getting It Wrong

If you report a “survival gene” that is actually correlated with survival only because it is a marker of ER status, you have not found a new biomarker — you have rediscovered ER status with extra steps. Worse, if you build a model that ignores confounders, it will fail when applied to a different cohort.

Regression Gives You Conditional Associations

Simple correlation tells you whether two variables move together. Regression tells you whether they move together after accounting for other variables. This is the tool that lets you ask: “Does gene X predict survival after controlling for age, stage, and subtype?”

The Data

We use two TCGA BRCA datasets from the Genomic Data Commons (GDC):

  1. Expression data: FPKM-normalized RNA-seq expression for ~20,000 genes across ~1,200 samples.
  2. Clinical phenotype data: Patient demographics, tumor characteristics, survival outcomes.
# Download TCGA BRCA expression data (FPKM)
let expr_url = "https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.htseq_fpkm.tsv.gz"
let expr_file = download(expr_url, "tcga_brca_fpkm.tsv.gz")

# Download clinical phenotype data
let clin_url = "https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.GDC_phenotype.tsv.gz"
let clin_file = download(clin_url, "tcga_brca_phenotype.tsv.gz")

Loading and Inspecting the Expression Matrix

# Read the expression matrix
let expr = read_tsv(expr_file)
println("Expression matrix dimensions:")
println("  Rows (genes): " + to_string(len(expr)))
println("  Columns (samples): " + to_string(ncols(expr)))

# Preview the first few rows and columns
head(expr, 5)

The expression matrix has genes as rows and samples as columns. Each value is the FPKM (Fragments Per Kilobase of transcript per Million mapped fragments) for that gene in that sample.

# Check column names — first column is gene ID, rest are sample barcodes
let columns = columns(expr)
println("First 5 columns:")
println(slice(columns, 0, 5))

Loading Clinical Data

# Read clinical phenotype data
let clinical = read_tsv(clin_file)
println("Clinical data dimensions:")
println("  Patients: " + to_string(len(clinical)))
println("  Variables: " + to_string(ncols(clinical)))

# What clinical variables do we have?
println("Clinical columns:")
println(columns(clinical))

The clinical data contains dozens of variables. The ones we care about most:

  • submitter_id.samples — The sample barcode (links to expression columns)
  • age_at_initial_pathologic_diagnosis — Patient age
  • pathologic_stage — Tumor stage (Stage I through Stage IV)
  • OS — Overall survival status (0 = alive/censored, 1 = dead)
  • OS.time — Overall survival time in days
  • breast_carcinoma_estrogen_receptor_status — ER status
  • breast_carcinoma_progesterone_receptor_status — PR status
  • lab_proc_her2_neu_immunohistochemistry_receptor_status — HER2 status
# Summary of key clinical variables
let age = col_values(clinical, "age_at_initial_pathologic_diagnosis")
let stage = col_values(clinical, "pathologic_stage")
let os_status = col_values(clinical, "OS")
let os_time = col_values(clinical, "OS.time")

println("Age summary:")
let age_numeric = age |> filter(|x| !is_none(x)) |> map(|x| to_float(x))
println("  Mean: " + to_string(round(mean(age_numeric), 1)))
println("  Median: " + to_string(median(age_numeric)))
println("  SD: " + to_string(round(stdev(age_numeric), 1)))
println("  Range: " + to_string(min(age_numeric)) + " - " + to_string(max(age_numeric)))

Preparing a Joint Dataset

The expression matrix has samples as columns — we need to extract specific genes and join them with clinical data.

# Extract expression for key genes
# ESR1 (estrogen receptor), ERBB2 (HER2), TP53, MKI67 (Ki-67 proliferation marker)
# PGR (progesterone receptor), BRCA1

let genes_of_interest = ["ESR1", "ERBB2", "TP53", "MKI67", "PGR", "BRCA1"]

# Filter expression matrix for our genes of interest
let gene_col = columns(expr) |> head(1)
let expr_subset = expr |> filter_by(gene_col, |g| contains(genes_of_interest, g))

println("Extracted expression for " + to_string(len(expr_subset)) + " genes")
head(expr_subset, 6)

# Transpose so genes become columns and samples become rows
let expr_t = transpose(expr_subset)

# Merge expression with clinical data on sample ID
let merged = inner_join(expr_t, clinical, "sample_id", "submitter_id.samples")

println("Merged dataset: " + to_string(len(merged)) + " patients with both expression and clinical data")

# Drop rows with missing survival data
let analysis_data = merged |> drop_null("OS") |> drop_null("OS.time")
println("After removing missing survival: " + to_string(len(analysis_data)) + " patients")

Exploration

Before computing any correlations, look at the data.

Distribution of Expression Values

# FPKM values are right-skewed — check ESR1
let esr1_vals = col_values(analysis_data, "ESR1") |> map(|x| to_float(x))

println("ESR1 FPKM summary:")
println(summary(esr1_vals))

histogram(esr1_vals, "ESR1 FPKM Distribution") |> save_plot("esr1_fpkm_raw.svg")

The histogram will show a dramatically right-skewed distribution. Most samples have moderate ESR1 expression, but some ER-positive tumors have very high values. This is typical of gene expression data and matters for correlation.

# Log-transform for more normal distribution
# Add small constant to avoid log(0)
let esr1_log = esr1_vals |> map(|x| log2(x + 1.0))

println("log2(ESR1 + 1) summary:")
println(summary(esr1_log))

histogram(esr1_log, "ESR1 log2(FPKM+1) Distribution") |> save_plot("esr1_fpkm_log.svg")

The log-transformed distribution is much closer to normal. This is important because Pearson correlation assumes linear relationships, which are more likely after log transformation of expression data.

Pairwise Scatter Plots

# ESR1 vs PGR — these should be strongly correlated (both ER pathway)
let pgr_log = col_values(analysis_data, "PGR") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))

scatter(esr1_log, pgr_log, "ESR1 vs PGR (log2 FPKM)")
    |> save_plot("esr1_pgr_scatter.svg")

# ESR1 vs ERBB2 — these should be negatively correlated
# (ER+ and HER2+ are largely distinct subtypes)
let erbb2_log = col_values(analysis_data, "ERBB2") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))

scatter(esr1_log, erbb2_log, "ESR1 vs ERBB2 (log2 FPKM)")
    |> save_plot("esr1_erbb2_scatter.svg")

# MKI67 (proliferation) vs survival time
let mki67_log = col_values(analysis_data, "MKI67") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let surv_time = col_values(analysis_data, "OS.time") |> map(|x| to_float(x))

scatter(mki67_log, surv_time, "Ki-67 vs Overall Survival Time")
    |> save_plot("ki67_survival_scatter.svg")

The Analysis

Part 1: Pearson Correlation

Pearson correlation (r) measures the strength and direction of the linear relationship between two continuous variables. It ranges from -1 (perfect negative linear relationship) to +1 (perfect positive linear relationship). Zero means no linear relationship.

The formula:

r = sum((x_i - mean_x)(y_i - mean_y)) / sqrt(sum((x_i - mean_x)^2) * sum((y_i - mean_y)^2))

Assumptions:

  1. Both variables are continuous
  2. The relationship is linear
  3. Both variables are approximately normally distributed (for inference)
  4. No extreme outliers (they inflate or deflate r dramatically)
# Pearson correlation between ESR1 and PGR
let r_esr1_pgr = cor(esr1_log, pgr_log)
println("Pearson r (ESR1, PGR): " + to_string(round(r_esr1_pgr, 4)))

Expected result: r around 0.65-0.75. ESR1 and PGR are co-regulated — the estrogen receptor drives PGR transcription. This is a biologically meaningful positive correlation.

# ESR1 vs ERBB2
let r_esr1_erbb2 = cor(esr1_log, erbb2_log)
println("Pearson r (ESR1, ERBB2): " + to_string(round(r_esr1_erbb2, 4)))

Expected: r around -0.20 to -0.35. The negative correlation reflects the biological reality that ER+ and HER2+ are largely distinct subtypes, though the correlation is moderate because some tumors are double-positive or double-negative.

# Full correlation matrix for all genes of interest
let gene_names = ["ESR1", "ERBB2", "TP53", "MKI67", "PGR", "BRCA1"]
let gene_data = gene_names |> map(|g|
    col_values(analysis_data, g) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
)

let gene_matrix = matrix(gene_data)
let cor_mat = cor_matrix(gene_matrix)

println("Gene-gene correlation matrix:")
println(cor_mat)

heatmap(cor_mat, gene_names, gene_names, "Gene Expression Correlation Matrix")
    |> save_plot("gene_correlation_heatmap.svg")

Interpreting the Correlation Matrix

The heatmap reveals the structure of breast cancer biology:

  • ESR1-PGR: Strong positive. Co-regulated by estrogen signaling.
  • ESR1-ERBB2: Negative. Different subtypes.
  • MKI67-ESR1: Negative. High-proliferation tumors tend to be ER-negative.
  • MKI67-ERBB2: Positive. HER2+ tumors tend to be more proliferative.
  • TP53-MKI67: Positive. TP53 mutations associate with high proliferation.
  • BRCA1-ESR1: Negative. BRCA1-related tumors are often triple-negative.

Each of these correlations has a biological explanation. But correlation alone does not tell you which is cause and which is effect. ESR1 drives PGR transcription (causal), but ESR1 and ERBB2 are negatively correlated because they mark different cell lineages (not causal — subtype is the common cause).

Part 2: Correlation Is Not Causation

This point deserves a detailed treatment because genomics is full of misleading correlations.

Example 1: The Subtype Confounder

# Separate ER-positive and ER-negative patients
let er_status = col_values(analysis_data, "breast_carcinoma_estrogen_receptor_status")

let er_pos_idx = er_status |> map(|x| x == "Positive")
let er_neg_idx = er_status |> map(|x| x == "Negative")

# Correlation between MKI67 and ERBB2 in ALL patients
let mki67_all = col_values(analysis_data, "MKI67") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let erbb2_all = col_values(analysis_data, "ERBB2") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let r_all = cor(mki67_all, erbb2_all)
println("MKI67-ERBB2 correlation (all patients): " + to_string(round(r_all, 4)))

# Now separate by ER status
let data_er_pos = analysis_data |> filter_by("breast_carcinoma_estrogen_receptor_status", |x| x == "Positive")
let data_er_neg = analysis_data |> filter_by("breast_carcinoma_estrogen_receptor_status", |x| x == "Negative")

let mki67_pos = col_values(data_er_pos, "MKI67") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let erbb2_pos = col_values(data_er_pos, "ERBB2") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let r_pos = cor(mki67_pos, erbb2_pos)

let mki67_neg = col_values(data_er_neg, "MKI67") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let erbb2_neg = col_values(data_er_neg, "ERBB2") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let r_neg = cor(mki67_neg, erbb2_neg)

println("MKI67-ERBB2 correlation (ER+): " + to_string(round(r_pos, 4)))
println("MKI67-ERBB2 correlation (ER-): " + to_string(round(r_neg, 4)))

This demonstrates a critical phenomenon. The overall correlation between MKI67 and ERBB2 may be positive, but within each ER subgroup, the correlation can be much weaker or even reversed. The overall correlation was driven by ER status acting as a confounder — ER-negative tumors tend to have both higher MKI67 and higher ERBB2 on average, creating an apparent positive correlation.

This is Simpson’s paradox in action: the trend in the combined data does not hold within the subgroups.

Example 2: Batch Effects Create Spurious Correlation

# Suppose two genes are uncorrelated biologically but both are affected
# by library size. Samples processed in batch A have larger libraries.
# Both genes appear "upregulated" in batch A — creating a correlation
# that is purely technical.

# Simulate this to demonstrate the principle
let batch_a_lib = rnorm(50, 100.0, 10.0)  # larger libraries
let batch_b_lib = rnorm(50, 60.0, 10.0)   # smaller libraries
let all_lib = concat(batch_a_lib, batch_b_lib)

# Gene X: no real difference, but expression scales with library size
let gene_x = all_lib |> map(|lib| lib * 0.5 + rnorm(1, 0.0, 5.0) |> head(1))
let gene_x_flat = gene_x |> flatten()

# Gene Y: also no real difference, also scales with library size
let gene_y = all_lib |> map(|lib| lib * 0.3 + rnorm(1, 0.0, 5.0) |> head(1))
let gene_y_flat = gene_y |> flatten()

let r_spurious = cor(gene_x_flat, gene_y_flat)
println("Spurious correlation from batch effect: " + to_string(round(r_spurious, 4)))
# This will be strongly positive despite no biological relationship

Part 3: Spearman Correlation for Non-Linear Relationships

Pearson correlation measures linear relationships. But many biological relationships are monotonic but non-linear: as X increases, Y increases, but not at a constant rate.

Spearman correlation works on ranks rather than raw values. It measures whether the rank order of X matches the rank order of Y.

# Spearman correlation for ESR1 vs PGR
let sp_esr1_pgr = spearman(esr1_log, pgr_log)
println("Spearman rho (ESR1, PGR): " + to_string(round(sp_esr1_pgr.rho, 4)))
println("Spearman p-value: " + to_string(sp_esr1_pgr.p_value))

# Compare Pearson and Spearman for all gene pairs
println("Comparison of Pearson r and Spearman rho:")
println("Gene Pair            Pearson    Spearman")
println("─────────────────    ───────    ────────")

let pairs = [
    ["ESR1", "PGR"], ["ESR1", "ERBB2"], ["ESR1", "MKI67"],
    ["ERBB2", "MKI67"], ["TP53", "MKI67"], ["BRCA1", "ESR1"]
]

pairs |> each(|pair|
    let v1 = col_values(analysis_data, pair[0]) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
    let v2 = col_values(analysis_data, pair[1]) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
    let r = cor(v1, v2)
    let sp = spearman(v1, v2)
    let label = pair[0] + "-" + pair[1]
    println(label + "    " + to_string(round(r, 4)) + "    " + to_string(round(sp.rho, 4)))
)

When Pearson and Spearman agree closely, the relationship is close to linear. When Spearman is notably larger than Pearson, the relationship is monotonic but non-linear — common with expression data where a few extreme values distort the linear fit.

Kendall’s Tau

Kendall’s tau is another rank-based correlation. It counts concordant and discordant pairs. It is more robust than Spearman with small sample sizes and ties.

# Kendall's tau for ESR1 vs PGR
let kt = kendall(esr1_log, pgr_log)
println("Kendall tau (ESR1, PGR): " + to_string(round(kt.tau, 4)))
println("Kendall p-value: " + to_string(kt.p_value))

Kendall’s tau is always smaller in magnitude than Spearman’s rho for the same data. This is normal — they have different scales. The p-values, however, are usually similar.

Part 4: Simple Linear Regression

Correlation tells you that two variables are associated. Regression goes further: it fits a line that predicts one variable from the other and quantifies the prediction.

The model: Y = beta_0 + beta_1 * X + epsilon

  • beta_0: intercept (predicted Y when X = 0)
  • beta_1: slope (change in Y per unit change in X)
  • epsilon: residual error (what the model cannot explain)
# Does ESR1 expression predict overall survival time?
let esr1_expr = col_values(analysis_data, "ESR1") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let survival_days = col_values(analysis_data, "OS.time") |> map(|x| to_float(x))

# Remove any remaining nulls
let valid_idx = zip(esr1_expr, survival_days)
    |> filter(|pair| !is_none(pair[0]) && !is_none(pair[1]))
let esr1_clean = valid_idx |> map(|pair| pair[0])
let surv_clean = valid_idx |> map(|pair| pair[1])

# Fit simple linear regression
let model_simple = lm(surv_clean, esr1_clean)
println("Simple Linear Regression: Survival ~ ESR1")
println("  Intercept (beta_0): " + to_string(round(model_simple.intercept, 2)))
println("  Slope (beta_1): " + to_string(round(model_simple.coefficients[0], 2)))
println("  R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_simple.r_squared, 4)))
println("  P-value (slope): " + to_string(model_simple.p_values[0]))

Interpreting the Simple Regression

The slope (beta_1) tells you: for each unit increase in log2(ESR1 FPKM+1), the predicted survival time changes by beta_1 days. If it is positive, higher ESR1 expression is associated with longer survival; if negative, shorter.

The R-squared tells you what fraction of the variance in survival time is explained by ESR1 expression alone. In genomics, R-squared for a single gene predicting a clinical outcome is usually very small (0.01-0.05). This does not mean the association is unimportant — survival is determined by many factors, and any single gene explains only a small fraction.

The p-value tells you whether the slope is significantly different from zero. With 1,000+ patients, even tiny effects can be significant. This is a case where statistical significance and practical significance diverge.

# Visualize the regression
scatter(esr1_clean, surv_clean, "ESR1 Expression vs Survival Time")
    |> save_plot("esr1_survival_regression.svg")

# QQ plot of residuals to check normality assumption
let residuals = zip(surv_clean, esr1_clean) |> map(|pair|
    pair[0] - (model_simple.intercept + model_simple.coefficients[0] * pair[1])
)
qq_plot(residuals, "Residuals QQ Plot") |> save_plot("esr1_survival_qq.svg")

The QQ plot is crucial. If the residuals are normally distributed, the points fall along the diagonal. Survival data is notoriously non-normal (right-skewed, censored), so the QQ plot will likely show deviation. This means the p-values from linear regression are approximate at best, which is one reason survival analysis (Chapter 11) uses specialized methods.

Part 5: Multiple Regression — Controlling for Confounders

Simple regression of survival on ESR1 ignores everything else about the patient. Older patients have shorter survival regardless of gene expression. Higher-stage tumors have shorter survival. Multiple regression lets us ask: “Does ESR1 predict survival after controlling for age and stage?”

The model: Y = beta_0 + beta_1ESR1 + beta_2Age + beta_3*Stage + epsilon

# Prepare variables for multiple regression
let age_vals = col_values(analysis_data, "age_at_initial_pathologic_diagnosis")
    |> map(|x| to_float(x))

# Encode stage as numeric (simplified)
# Stage I = 1, Stage II = 2, Stage III = 3, Stage IV = 4
let stage_vals = col_values(analysis_data, "pathologic_stage") |> map(|s|
    if contains(to_string(s), "IV") then 4.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "III") then 3.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "II") then 2.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "I") then 1.0
    else 0.0  # unknown/missing
)

# Build predictor matrix: [ESR1, Age, Stage]
let predictors = [esr1_clean, age_vals, stage_vals]
let pred_matrix = matrix(predictors)

# Fit multiple regression
let model_multi = lm(surv_clean, pred_matrix)
println("Multiple Regression: Survival ~ ESR1 + Age + Stage")
println("  Intercept: " + to_string(round(model_multi.intercept, 2)))
println("  ESR1 coefficient: " + to_string(round(model_multi.coefficients[0], 2)))
println("  Age coefficient: " + to_string(round(model_multi.coefficients[1], 2)))
println("  Stage coefficient: " + to_string(round(model_multi.coefficients[2], 2)))
println("  R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_multi.r_squared, 4)))
println("  Adjusted R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_multi.adj_r_squared, 4)))

Interpreting Multiple Regression Coefficients

Each coefficient is now a partial regression coefficient — it represents the effect of that variable holding the other variables constant.

  • ESR1 coefficient: Change in predicted survival per unit increase in log2(ESR1), holding age and stage constant. This is the adjusted effect.
  • Age coefficient: Change in predicted survival per year of age, holding ESR1 and stage constant. Expected to be negative (older patients have shorter survival).
  • Stage coefficient: Change in predicted survival per stage increase, holding ESR1 and age constant. Expected to be strongly negative (higher stage = shorter survival).
# Compare simple vs multiple regression ESR1 coefficient
println("ESR1 effect comparison:")
println("  Unadjusted (simple): " + to_string(round(model_simple.coefficients[0], 2)))
println("  Adjusted (multiple): " + to_string(round(model_multi.coefficients[0], 2)))

If the ESR1 coefficient changes substantially between simple and multiple regression, this tells you that age and stage were confounders. The unadjusted effect was partially attributable to correlations between ESR1, age, and stage.

Part 6: Residual Analysis

The validity of regression depends on its assumptions. Residual analysis checks those assumptions.

Assumptions of linear regression:

  1. Linearity: The relationship between X and Y is linear
  2. Independence: Residuals are independent of each other
  3. Homoscedasticity: Residuals have constant variance
  4. Normality: Residuals are normally distributed
# Calculate residuals from multiple regression
let predicted = zip(esr1_clean, zip(age_vals, stage_vals)) |> map(|pair|
    let esr1_val = pair[0]
    let age_val = pair[1][0]
    let stage_val = pair[1][1]
    model_multi.intercept
        + model_multi.coefficients[0] * esr1_val
        + model_multi.coefficients[1] * age_val
        + model_multi.coefficients[2] * stage_val
)

let resid_multi = zip(surv_clean, predicted) |> map(|pair| pair[0] - pair[1])

# 1. Residuals vs fitted values (check linearity and homoscedasticity)
scatter(predicted, resid_multi, "Residuals vs Fitted Values")
    |> save_plot("residuals_vs_fitted.svg")

# 2. QQ plot of residuals (check normality)
qq_plot(resid_multi, "Multiple Regression Residuals") |> save_plot("multi_reg_qq.svg")

# 3. Residual summary statistics
println("Residual summary:")
println(summary(resid_multi))
println("Residual standard deviation: " + to_string(round(stdev(resid_multi), 2)))

If the residuals-vs-fitted plot shows a funnel shape (wider on one side), you have heteroscedasticity. If it shows a curve, the linearity assumption is violated. For survival data, both problems are common because survival times are bounded below by zero and right-skewed.

Part 7: Gene-Gene Correlation Networks

Beyond pairwise correlations, we can build a network of gene-gene correlations. This reveals modules of co-expressed genes, which often correspond to biological pathways.

# Select top variable genes for correlation network
# First, compute variance across samples for each gene
let expr_data = read_tsv(expr_file)

# Get a subset of known cancer-related genes
let cancer_genes = [
    "ESR1", "ERBB2", "TP53", "MKI67", "PGR", "BRCA1",
    "EGFR", "CDH1", "PTEN", "PIK3CA", "AKT1", "MTOR",
    "MYC", "CCND1", "CDK4", "RB1", "VEGFA", "KRAS",
    "BRAF", "MAP2K1"
]

# Extract expression for these genes across all samples
let cancer_expr = expr_data |> filter_by("Ensembl_ID", |g| contains(cancer_genes, g))

# Build correlation matrix
let cancer_vals = cancer_genes |> map(|g|
    col_values(analysis_data, g) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
)
let cancer_matrix = matrix(cancer_vals)
let cancer_cor = cor_matrix(cancer_matrix)

# Heatmap of the correlation network
heatmap(cancer_cor, cancer_genes, cancer_genes, "Cancer Gene Correlation Network")
    |> save_plot("cancer_gene_network.svg")

# Build a graph where edges represent strong correlations
let gene_network = graph()

# Add nodes
cancer_genes |> each(|g| add_node(gene_network, g))

# Add edges for correlations above threshold
let threshold = 0.3
let n_genes = len(cancer_genes)

let edge_count = 0
let i = 0
cancer_genes |> each(|g1|
    let j = i + 1
    slice(cancer_genes, j, n_genes) |> each(|g2|
        let v1 = col_values(analysis_data, g1) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
        let v2 = col_values(analysis_data, g2) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
        let r_val = abs(cor(v1, v2))
        if r_val > threshold then
            add_edge(gene_network, g1, g2)
            edge_count = edge_count + 1
        end
    )
    i = i + 1
)

println("Gene correlation network:")
println("  Nodes: " + to_string(len(cancer_genes)))
println("  Edges (|r| > " + to_string(threshold) + "): " + to_string(edge_count))

# Find connected components — these are co-expression modules
let components = connected_components(gene_network)
println("  Connected components: " + to_string(len(components)))

# Show the degree of each gene (number of strong correlations)
cancer_genes |> each(|g|
    let deg = degree(gene_network, g)
    println("  " + g + ": " + to_string(deg) + " strong correlations")
)

Genes with high degree are “hub” genes — they correlate with many other genes. In cancer biology, hub genes often correspond to transcription factors or signaling pathway components that regulate many downstream targets.

Part 8: Regression to the Mean

Regression to the mean is one of the most misunderstood phenomena in biostatistics. It was discovered by Francis Galton (who also coined the term “regression” — the method is named after this phenomenon).

The trap: If you select patients with the highest gene expression at time point 1 and measure them again at time point 2, their expression will appear to decrease — even if nothing has changed. This is because extreme values include measurement error, and that error is unlikely to be equally extreme at the next measurement.

# Demonstrate regression to the mean with simulated data
# True expression is constant, but measurement adds noise

let n_patients = 200
let true_expr = rnorm(n_patients, 10.0, 2.0)  # true expression

# Measurement 1: true + noise
let noise1 = rnorm(n_patients, 0.0, 1.5)
let measure1 = zip(true_expr, noise1) |> map(|pair| pair[0] + pair[1])

# Measurement 2: true + different noise
let noise2 = rnorm(n_patients, 0.0, 1.5)
let measure2 = zip(true_expr, noise2) |> map(|pair| pair[0] + pair[1])

# Select the top 20% from measurement 1
let sorted_m1 = measure1 |> sort() |> to_list()
let cutoff = quantile(measure1, 0.80)
let top_idx = measure1 |> map(|x| x >= cutoff)

let top_m1 = zip(measure1, top_idx) |> filter(|pair| pair[1]) |> map(|pair| pair[0])
let top_m2 = zip(measure2, top_idx) |> filter(|pair| pair[1]) |> map(|pair| pair[0])

println("Regression to the mean demonstration:")
println("  Mean of top 20% at time 1: " + to_string(round(mean(top_m1), 2)))
println("  Mean of same patients at time 2: " + to_string(round(mean(top_m2), 2)))
println("  Apparent decrease: " + to_string(round(mean(top_m1) - mean(top_m2), 2)))
println("  (This 'decrease' is purely statistical, not biological)")

This is devastating for pre-post studies. If you select patients with the highest tumor marker levels and give them a treatment, the marker will likely decrease at follow-up even if the treatment does nothing. Regression to the mean masquerades as a treatment effect.

The solution: Use a control group. If both treatment and control groups are selected for high values, both will regress to the mean. Only the difference between groups reflects a true treatment effect.

# Show the same phenomenon with a scatter plot
scatter(measure1, measure2, "Measurement 1 vs Measurement 2")
    |> save_plot("regression_to_mean.svg")

# The slope of the regression line is less than 1.0
let rtm_model = lm(measure2, measure1)
println("Regression slope (should be < 1 if regression to mean): "
    + to_string(round(rtm_model.coefficients[0], 4)))

A slope less than 1.0 means that extreme values on X are associated with less extreme values on Y. This is regression to the mean. The more measurement noise, the stronger the effect.

Part 9: Building a Practical Prediction Model

Now let us combine everything into a practical prediction model for breast cancer outcomes.

# Build a multi-gene model predicting survival
# Include: ESR1, ERBB2, MKI67, Age, Stage

let esr1_v = col_values(analysis_data, "ESR1") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let erbb2_v = col_values(analysis_data, "ERBB2") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let mki67_v = col_values(analysis_data, "MKI67") |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
let age_v = col_values(analysis_data, "age_at_initial_pathologic_diagnosis")
    |> map(|x| to_float(x))
let stage_v = col_values(analysis_data, "pathologic_stage") |> map(|s|
    if contains(to_string(s), "IV") then 4.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "III") then 3.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "II") then 2.0
    else if contains(to_string(s), "I") then 1.0
    else 0.0
)
let surv_v = col_values(analysis_data, "OS.time") |> map(|x| to_float(x))

# Fit the full model
let pred_full = matrix([esr1_v, erbb2_v, mki67_v, age_v, stage_v])
let model_full = lm(surv_v, pred_full)

println("Full Model: Survival ~ ESR1 + ERBB2 + MKI67 + Age + Stage")
println("  R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_full.r_squared, 4)))
println("  Adjusted R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_full.adj_r_squared, 4)))
println("")
println("  Coefficients:")
let coef_names = ["ESR1", "ERBB2", "MKI67", "Age", "Stage"]
zip(coef_names, model_full.coefficients) |> each(|pair|
    println("    " + pair[0] + ": " + to_string(round(pair[1], 4))
        + "  (p = " + to_string(round(model_full.p_values[0], 4)) + ")")
)

# Compare models: simple vs multiple
println("Model comparison:")
println("  ESR1 only:        R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_simple.r_squared, 4)))
println("  ESR1+Age+Stage:   R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_multi.r_squared, 4)))
println("  Full (5 vars):    R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_full.r_squared, 4)))

The improvement from adding more variables shows you how much additional variance each variable explains. But beware: adding more variables always increases R-squared (even random variables will increase it). The adjusted R-squared penalizes for the number of predictors and is a better guide.

# Normalize coefficients to compare effect sizes
# Standardize each predictor to mean 0, SD 1
let esr1_z = normalize(esr1_v)
let erbb2_z = normalize(erbb2_v)
let mki67_z = normalize(mki67_v)
let age_z = normalize(age_v)
let stage_z = normalize(stage_v)

let pred_std = matrix([esr1_z, erbb2_z, mki67_z, age_z, stage_z])
let model_std = lm(surv_v, pred_std)

println("Standardized coefficients (effect size comparison):")
zip(coef_names, model_std.coefficients) |> each(|pair|
    println("  " + pair[0] + ": " + to_string(round(pair[1], 2)))
)

Standardized coefficients are directly comparable: the variable with the largest absolute standardized coefficient has the strongest effect on survival. Typically, stage dominates, followed by age, with individual gene effects being smaller but still meaningful.

Interpretation

What We Found

  1. ESR1 and PGR are strongly positively correlated (r ~ 0.7), reflecting the biology of estrogen receptor signaling.

  2. ESR1 and ERBB2 are negatively correlated (r ~ -0.25), reflecting distinct breast cancer subtypes.

  3. ESR1 expression predicts survival in simple regression, but the effect is partially confounded by age and stage.

  4. Multiple regression reveals the adjusted effect: after controlling for clinical variables, the independent contribution of ESR1 to survival prediction is smaller than the unadjusted effect suggests.

  5. Stage is the dominant predictor of survival time, as expected.

  6. Gene-gene correlation networks reveal modules of co-expressed genes corresponding to known biological pathways.

What We Cannot Conclude

  • We cannot say ESR1 expression causes longer survival. ER-positive tumors are a distinct subtype with different biology, different treatment (tamoxifen, aromatase inhibitors), and different prognosis.

  • We cannot use this linear regression model for clinical prediction of survival. Survival data requires specialized methods (Chapter 11) because of censoring.

  • We cannot conclude that low-correlation genes are biologically unrelated. Correlation measures linear (or rank-based) association. Non-linear relationships, threshold effects, and interactions are invisible to correlation.

Decision

When to Report a Correlation

Report correlations when:

  • The sample size is large enough for the confidence interval to be narrow
  • You have checked that the relationship is not driven by outliers
  • You have considered and addressed potential confounders
  • You report the confidence interval, not just the point estimate

When to Use Multiple Regression

Use multiple regression when:

  • You want the effect of X on Y adjusting for other variables
  • You suspect confounders (age, batch, clinical variables)
  • You want to compare the relative importance of multiple predictors
  • The outcome variable is continuous and approximately normal

When to Use Something Else

  • Binary outcome (alive/dead, mutated/wild-type): Use logistic regression (Chapter 10)
  • Survival time with censoring: Use Cox regression (Chapter 11)
  • Count data (read counts, variant counts): Use Poisson or negative binomial regression (Chapter 10)
  • Non-linear relationships: Consider spline regression or generalized additive models

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Ignoring Non-Linearity

# Pearson correlation can be zero even with a strong non-linear relationship
# Example: quadratic relationship
let x_vals = rnorm(200, 0.0, 3.0)
let y_quad = x_vals |> map(|x| pow(x, 2.0) + rnorm(1, 0.0, 1.0) |> head(1))
let y_flat = y_quad |> flatten()

let r_quad = cor(x_vals, y_flat)
println("Pearson r for quadratic relationship: " + to_string(round(r_quad, 4)))
println("(Near zero despite perfect quadratic relationship)")

# Always plot your data!
scatter(x_vals, y_flat, "Quadratic Relationship (r ~ 0)")
    |> save_plot("quadratic_relationship.svg")

Pitfall 2: Correlation of Ratios

In genomics, we often correlate ratios or normalized values. This creates spurious correlations because ratios share denominators.

If X = A/C and Y = B/C, then X and Y will be correlated even if A and B are independent — because both depend on C. This affects:

  • FPKM values (normalized by library size)
  • Compositional data (proportions that sum to 1)
  • Fold changes relative to the same reference
# Demonstrate spurious ratio correlation
let a_vals = rnorm(100, 50.0, 10.0)  # independent
let b_vals = rnorm(100, 50.0, 10.0)  # independent
let c_vals = rnorm(100, 50.0, 10.0)  # shared denominator

let ratio_x = zip(a_vals, c_vals) |> map(|pair| pair[0] / pair[1])
let ratio_y = zip(b_vals, c_vals) |> map(|pair| pair[0] / pair[1])

let r_ratio = cor(ratio_x, ratio_y)
println("Correlation of A/C and B/C (A, B independent): " + to_string(round(r_ratio, 4)))
println("(Positive despite A and B being independent)")

Pitfall 3: Extrapolation

Linear regression fits a line through the observed range of data. Predicting outside that range (extrapolation) is unreliable because the relationship may not be linear beyond the data.

If your TCGA patients range from age 25 to 90, do not use the model to predict survival for a 15-year-old. The linear relationship between age and survival may not hold at extreme ages.

Pitfall 4: Multicollinearity

When predictors in multiple regression are highly correlated with each other, the individual coefficients become unstable. This is multicollinearity.

# ESR1 and PGR are highly correlated — including both may cause instability
let pred_collinear = matrix([esr1_v, pgr_log, age_v])
let model_collinear = lm(surv_v, pred_collinear)

println("Model with collinear predictors (ESR1 + PGR + Age):")
println("  ESR1 coefficient: " + to_string(round(model_collinear.coefficients[0], 4)))
println("  PGR coefficient: " + to_string(round(model_collinear.coefficients[1], 4)))
println("  R-squared: " + to_string(round(model_collinear.r_squared, 4)))

# Compare to models with only one of the two
let model_esr1_only = lm(surv_v, matrix([esr1_v, age_v]))
let model_pgr_only = lm(surv_v, matrix([pgr_log, age_v]))

println("ESR1 + Age only R^2: " + to_string(round(model_esr1_only.r_squared, 4)))
println("PGR + Age only R^2: " + to_string(round(model_pgr_only.r_squared, 4)))

When two predictors carry largely the same information, adding both does not improve prediction and makes individual coefficients unreliable. Choose one or use dimensionality reduction.

Pitfall 5: Overfitting

With enough predictors, you can fit any dataset perfectly. But the model will not generalize to new data.

Rule of thumb: You need at least 10-15 observations per predictor variable. With 1,000 patients, a model with 5 predictors is fine. A model with 200 gene predictors needs 2,000-3,000 patients.

# Demonstrate with adjusted R-squared
println("Adjusted R-squared penalizes for number of predictors:")
println("  Simple (1 predictor): adj R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_simple.adj_r_squared, 4)))
println("  Multiple (3 predictors): adj R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_multi.adj_r_squared, 4)))
println("  Full (5 predictors): adj R^2 = " + to_string(round(model_full.adj_r_squared, 4)))
println("If adj R^2 stops increasing or decreases, added variables are not helping.")

Pitfall 6: P-value Fishing

With 20,000 genes, testing each one for correlation with survival will produce ~1,000 “significant” results at p < 0.05 by chance alone. Always correct for multiple testing when scanning many genes.

# Suppose we tested 100 genes for correlation with survival
# Collect p-values and apply correction
let test_genes = slice(cancer_genes, 0, 10)  # using our subset for demonstration

let p_values_raw = test_genes |> map(|g|
    let gv = col_values(analysis_data, g) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
    let sp = spearman(gv, surv_v)
    sp.p_value
)

let p_adj = p_adjust(p_values_raw, "BH")

println("Gene          Raw P         BH-adjusted P")
println("────────      ─────         ─────────────")
zip(test_genes, zip(p_values_raw, p_adj)) |> each(|pair|
    println(pair[0] + "    " + to_string(round(pair[1][0], 6))
        + "    " + to_string(round(pair[1][1], 6)))
)

Where Next?

Immediate Extensions

  • Logistic regression: When the outcome is binary (Chapter 10). Use GLM with logit link to predict ER status from gene expression.

  • Cox regression: When the outcome is survival time with censoring (Chapter 11). The correct approach for the survival question we explored here with imperfect linear regression.

  • Partial correlation: Correlation between X and Y after removing the effect of Z. Computable by correlating the residuals from regressing X on Z and Y on Z.

Advanced Topics

  • Ridge and Lasso regression: When you have more predictors than samples (the genomics default). Regularization prevents overfitting.

  • Principal component regression: Use PCA to reduce thousands of genes to a handful of components, then regress survival on the components.

  • Weighted gene co-expression network analysis (WGCNA): Scale gene-gene correlation networks to the entire transcriptome. Identify modules and relate them to clinical traits.

Practice Problems

  1. Hormone receptor genes: Compute the correlation matrix for all known hormone receptor genes (ESR1, ESR2, PGR, AR) and their downstream targets in the TCGA BRCA dataset. Which pairs have the strongest correlations?

  2. Confounding by subtype: Run simple regression of MKI67 on ERBB2 in all patients, then separately within PAM50 subtypes. Does the relationship change? This demonstrates that subtype acts as a confounder.

  3. Effect of log transformation: Compare Pearson correlations between ESR1 and PGR on raw FPKM values vs log2-transformed values. How much does the transformation change the result? Plot both.

  4. Regression to the mean: Using two replicate measurements from the same samples (if available), demonstrate regression to the mean by selecting extreme samples from replicate 1 and measuring their values in replicate 2.

  5. Multicollinearity: Build a regression model using 10 genes from the same pathway (e.g., the PI3K-AKT pathway). Examine how unstable the individual coefficients are. Then use PCA on the 10 genes and regress survival on the first 2 principal components instead.

Key takeaways from this chapter:

  • Correlation measures association, not causation. Always consider confounders.
  • Always visualize the data before computing correlations — scatter plots reveal non-linearity, outliers, and subgroups that numbers hide.
  • Multiple regression adjusts for confounders but assumes linearity and independence.
  • Spearman and Kendall correlations handle non-linear monotonic relationships.
  • Regression to the mean traps pre-post studies without control groups.
  • Multicollinearity makes individual coefficients in multiple regression unreliable when predictors are correlated.
  • Gene-gene correlation networks reveal co-expression modules, but edges are not causal relationships.
  • With thousands of genes, always correct for multiple testing.

Generalized Linear Models

“All models are wrong, but some are useful.” — George Box

The Problem

ClinVar contains over 2 million variant records classified by clinical significance — pathogenic, benign, uncertain, and others. You want to build a model that predicts whether a variant is pathogenic based on measurable properties: allele frequency in the population, gene constraint (tolerance to loss of function), variant type, and review status.

This is a classification problem with a binary outcome: pathogenic or not. If you try to use ordinary linear regression, you will get predicted probabilities outside the [0, 1] range and biased estimates. You need a generalized linear model (GLM) — specifically, logistic regression.

But the GLM framework is much broader than logistic regression. In this chapter, we also cover Poisson regression for count data (how many pathogenic variants does each gene have?) and negative binomial regression for overdispersed counts (why RNA-seq differential expression tools use NB, not Poisson). By the end, you will understand the GLM family well enough to choose the right model for any outcome type.

Why It Matters

Variant Classification Is a Prediction Problem

As clinical genomics scales to millions of patients, we encounter millions of variants of uncertain significance (VUS). Machine learning approaches like REVEL, CADD, and AlphaMissense predict pathogenicity from sequence features. Understanding logistic regression is essential because:

  1. It is the foundation of all these tools — even complex ensemble methods include logistic regression components.
  2. It produces interpretable odds ratios that clinicians can understand.
  3. It provides proper uncertainty estimates (confidence intervals on predictions).
  4. Regulatory bodies (FDA, EMA) require interpretable models for clinical decision support.

Count Data Is Everywhere in Genomics

RNA-seq produces read counts. Variant calling produces variant counts per gene. ChIP-seq produces peak counts per region. WGS produces coverage counts per base. All of these are count data, and count data violates the assumptions of ordinary linear regression (counts are non-negative integers, variance often increases with the mean).

Poisson and negative binomial regression — both GLMs — handle count data correctly. DESeq2, edgeR, and every major RNA-seq tool uses negative binomial GLMs under the hood.

The GLM Framework Unifies Everything

The GLM framework covers:

  • Linear regression: Continuous outcome, identity link, normal errors
  • Logistic regression: Binary outcome, logit link, binomial errors
  • Poisson regression: Count outcome, log link, Poisson errors
  • Negative binomial regression: Overdispersed count outcome, log link, NB errors

Once you understand the framework, choosing the right model is straightforward: look at your outcome variable, choose the appropriate distribution and link function, and fit.

The Data

ClinVar Variant Summary

ClinVar is NCBI’s public archive of relationships between human genetic variants and phenotypes. The variant summary file contains one row per variant with clinical significance, gene, variant type, and review status.

# Download ClinVar variant summary
let clinvar_url = "https://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pub/clinvar/tab_delimited/variant_summary.txt.gz"
let clinvar_file = download(clinvar_url, "variant_summary.txt.gz")

let clinvar = read_tsv(clinvar_file)
println("ClinVar records: " + to_string(len(clinvar)))
println("Columns: " + to_string(ncols(clinvar)))
println(columns(clinvar))

# Examine clinical significance categories
let clin_sig = col_values(clinvar, "ClinicalSignificance")
let sig_counts = value_counts(clin_sig)
println("Clinical significance distribution:")
println(sig_counts |> arrange("count", "desc") |> head(15))

gnomAD Constraint Metrics

gnomAD provides gene-level constraint metrics: how tolerant each gene is to loss-of-function (LoF) variants. Genes under strong constraint (intolerant of LoF) are more likely to harbor disease-causing variants.

# Download gnomAD v4.1 constraint metrics
let gnomad_url = "https://storage.googleapis.com/gcp-public-data--gnomad/release/4.1/constraint/gnomad.v4.1.constraint_metrics.tsv"
let gnomad_file = download(gnomad_url, "gnomad_constraint.tsv")

let constraint = read_tsv(gnomad_file)
println("gnomAD constraint: " + to_string(len(constraint)) + " genes")
println("Columns:")
println(columns(constraint))

# Key constraint columns:
# - gene: gene symbol
# - transcript: canonical transcript
# - oe_lof: observed/expected ratio for LoF variants (lower = more constrained)
# - oe_lof_upper: upper confidence bound
# - pLI: probability of LoF intolerance (higher = more constrained)
# - oe_mis: observed/expected for missense
# - lof.z: Z-score for LoF constraint

let pli_vals = col_values(constraint, "pLI")
    |> filter(|x| !is_none(x))
    |> map(|x| to_float(x))

println("pLI summary:")
println(summary(pli_vals))

histogram(pli_vals, "gnomAD pLI Distribution") |> save_plot("pli_distribution.svg")

The pLI distribution is bimodal: most genes cluster near 0 (LoF tolerant) or near 1 (LoF intolerant), with relatively few in between. This reflects the biology — genes are either essential (constraint) or dispensable (tolerance).

Preparing the Analysis Dataset

# Filter ClinVar to single-gene variants with clear pathogenic/benign status
# Restrict to GRCh38 assembly
let clinvar_clean = clinvar
    |> filter_by("Assembly", |a| a == "GRCh38")
    |> filter_by("ClinicalSignificance", |s|
        s == "Pathogenic" || s == "Benign" || s == "Likely pathogenic" || s == "Likely benign"
    )

# Create binary outcome: pathogenic (1) vs benign (0)
let clinvar_binary = clinvar_clean |> mutate("is_pathogenic", |row|
    if contains(row["ClinicalSignificance"], "athogenic") then 1.0
    else 0.0
)

println("Analysis dataset: " + to_string(len(clinvar_binary)) + " variants")
let outcome = col_values(clinvar_binary, "is_pathogenic")
let path_count = outcome |> filter(|x| x == 1.0) |> len()
let benign_count = outcome |> filter(|x| x == 0.0) |> len()
println("  Pathogenic: " + to_string(path_count))
println("  Benign: " + to_string(benign_count))
println("  Prevalence: " + to_string(round(to_float(path_count) / to_float(len(outcome)) * 100.0, 1)) + "%")

# Join with gnomAD constraint data to get gene-level features
let analysis = inner_join(clinvar_binary, constraint, "GeneSymbol", "gene")
println("After joining with gnomAD: " + to_string(len(analysis)) + " variants")

Exploration

Why Linear Regression Fails for Binary Outcomes

Before learning logistic regression, we need to understand why ordinary linear regression is inappropriate for binary outcomes.

# Attempt linear regression on binary outcome
let y_binary = col_values(analysis, "is_pathogenic") |> map(|x| to_float(x))
let pli = col_values(analysis, "pLI")
    |> map(|x| if is_none(x) then 0.5 else to_float(x))

let bad_model = lm(y_binary, pli)
println("Linear regression on binary outcome:")
println("  Intercept: " + to_string(round(bad_model.intercept, 4)))
println("  pLI slope: " + to_string(round(bad_model.coefficients[0], 4)))
println("  R-squared: " + to_string(round(bad_model.r_squared, 4)))

# Compute predicted values
let predicted_linear = pli |> map(|x| bad_model.intercept + bad_model.coefficients[0] * x)

# Check for predictions outside [0, 1]
let below_zero = predicted_linear |> filter(|p| p < 0.0) |> len()
let above_one = predicted_linear |> filter(|p| p > 1.0) |> len()
println("  Predictions below 0: " + to_string(below_zero))
println("  Predictions above 1: " + to_string(above_one))
println("  (These are impossible probabilities!)")

Linear regression on a binary outcome produces predictions that can be negative or greater than 1. These cannot be interpreted as probabilities. The model also violates the homoscedasticity assumption — the variance of a binary variable depends on its mean (var = p * (1-p)), so residuals are inherently heteroscedastic.

# Scatter plot shows the problem visually
scatter(pli, y_binary, "Linear Regression on Binary Outcome (Wrong!)")
    |> save_plot("linear_binary_wrong.svg")

The scatter plot shows points clustered at 0 and 1 with a straight line trying to fit through them. The line inevitably extends beyond the valid range. We need a model that maps any linear combination of predictors to the [0, 1] interval.

Exploring Predictor-Outcome Relationships

# Distribution of pLI by pathogenicity status
let pli_pathogenic = analysis
