println("  Batch B: 10 control + 10 treatment = 20 samples")
println("  (Balanced design — batch is NOT confounded with group)")

# Simulate expression with batch effect
# For each gene, Batch B has expression shifted by batch_shift

let sim_data = gene_means |> map(|mu|
    let batch_a = rnorm(20, mu, 1.0)
    let batch_b = rnorm(20, mu + batch_shift, 1.0)
    concat(batch_a, batch_b)
)

let sim_matrix = matrix(sim_data)

println("Simulated expression matrix: "
    + to_string(len(gene_means)) + " genes x "
    + to_string(n_samples) + " samples")

# Overall mean by batch
let batch_a_means = sim_data |> map(|gene| mean(slice(gene, 0, 20)))
let batch_b_means = sim_data |> map(|gene| mean(slice(gene, 20, 40)))

println("Mean expression per gene:")
println("  Batch A: " + to_string(round(mean(batch_a_means), 2)))
println("  Batch B: " + to_string(round(mean(batch_b_means), 2)))
println("  Difference: " + to_string(round(mean(batch_b_means) - mean(batch_a_means), 2)))
println("  (True batch effect was " + to_string(batch_shift) + ")")

Detecting Batch Effects with PCA

PCA is the most common method for detecting batch effects. If the first principal components separate samples by batch rather than by biology, you have a batch effect problem.

# PCA on simulated data
let pca_result = pca(sim_matrix)

println("PCA of simulated data:")
println("  PC1 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_result.variance_explained[0] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")
println("  PC2 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_result.variance_explained[1] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")

# Plot PCA colored by batch
pca_plot(pca_result, batch_labels, "PCA Colored by Batch (Before Correction)")
    |> save_plot("pca_batch_before.svg")

# Plot PCA colored by biological group
pca_plot(pca_result, group_labels, "PCA Colored by Treatment Group (Before Correction)")
    |> save_plot("pca_group_before.svg")

In the batch-colored plot, you will see two distinct clusters on PC1 — Batch A on one side, Batch B on the other. In the group-colored plot, the treatment and control groups are mixed within each batch cluster. PC1 captures the batch effect, not the biology.

# Quantify the batch effect using PC1 scores
let pc1_scores = pca_result.scores |> map(|row| row[0])

let pc1_batch_a = slice(pc1_scores, 0, 20)
let pc1_batch_b = slice(pc1_scores, 20, 40)

let pc1_test = ttest(pc1_batch_a, pc1_batch_b)
println("PC1 vs Batch:")
println("  Batch A PC1 mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_batch_a), 2)))
println("  Batch B PC1 mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_batch_b), 2)))
println("  t-test p-value: " + to_string(pc1_test.p_value))

# Compare with PC1 vs treatment group
let pc1_control = concat(slice(pc1_scores, 0, 10), slice(pc1_scores, 20, 30))
let pc1_treatment = concat(slice(pc1_scores, 10, 20), slice(pc1_scores, 30, 40))

let pc1_group_test = ttest(pc1_control, pc1_treatment)
println("PC1 vs Treatment:")
println("  Control PC1 mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_control), 2)))
println("  Treatment PC1 mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_treatment), 2)))
println("  t-test p-value: " + to_string(pc1_group_test.p_value))

If PC1 is significantly associated with batch but not with treatment, the batch effect dominates the biology. This is the classic batch effect signature.

Detecting Batch Effects with Correlation

# Another detection method: correlation between expression and batch
# For each gene, compute correlation with batch indicator

let batch_numeric = batch_labels |> map(|b| if b == "Batch_A" then 0.0 else 1.0)
let group_numeric = group_labels |> map(|g| if g == "Control" then 0.0 else 1.0)

# How many genes correlate with batch vs treatment?
let batch_cors = sim_data |> map(|gene| abs(cor(gene, batch_numeric)))
let group_cors = sim_data |> map(|gene| abs(cor(gene, group_numeric)))

println("Absolute correlation with batch vs treatment:")
println("  Mean |cor| with batch: " + to_string(round(mean(batch_cors), 4)))
println("  Mean |cor| with treatment: " + to_string(round(mean(group_cors), 4)))

let high_batch_cor = batch_cors |> filter(|c| c > 0.3) |> len()
let high_group_cor = group_cors |> filter(|c| c > 0.3) |> len()
println("  Genes with |cor| > 0.3 with batch: " + to_string(high_batch_cor))
println("  Genes with |cor| > 0.3 with treatment: " + to_string(high_group_cor))

The Analysis

Part 1: Library Size as a Confounder

The most basic batch effect in RNA-seq is library size — the total number of reads per sample. Samples with more reads have higher counts for every gene.

# Simulate library size variation
let lib_sizes_a = rnorm(20, 30.0, 5.0)   # millions of reads, Batch A
let lib_sizes_b = rnorm(20, 50.0, 5.0)   # millions of reads, Batch B (deeper)
let lib_sizes = concat(lib_sizes_a, lib_sizes_b)

println("Library sizes (millions of reads):")
println("  Batch A mean: " + to_string(round(mean(lib_sizes_a), 1)))
println("  Batch B mean: " + to_string(round(mean(lib_sizes_b), 1)))
println("  Ratio: " + to_string(round(mean(lib_sizes_b) / mean(lib_sizes_a), 2)) + "x")

# Before normalization: raw counts correlate with library size
# This creates a spurious positive correlation between ALL genes
println("")
println("Before normalization:")
println("  Every gene appears 'upregulated' in Batch B")
println("  Correlation between any two genes is inflated")
println("  PCA separates batches perfectly")

# Library size normalization methods

# 1. CPM (Counts Per Million) — simplest
# CPM = count / total_counts * 1,000,000

let raw_counts = rnorm(40, 500.0, 200.0) |> map(|x| abs(x))
let total_counts = lib_sizes |> map(|x| x * 1000000.0)
let cpm_vals = zip(raw_counts, total_counts) |> map(|pair| pair[0] / pair[1] * 1000000.0)

println("CPM normalization:")
println("  Divides each count by library size")
println("  Makes samples comparable in total counts")
println("  Does NOT account for compositional effects")

# 2. TPM (Transcripts Per Million) — accounts for gene length
println("")
println("TPM normalization:")
println("  Divides by gene length first (RPKM), then normalizes to sum to 1M")
println("  Makes samples comparable AND accounts for gene length")
println("  Better than FPKM for between-sample comparison")

# 3. DESeq2 size factors — median of ratios
println("")
println("DESeq2 normalization:")
println("  Uses median of ratios to a reference sample")
println("  Robust to outlier genes (e.g., one highly expressed gene)")
println("  Standard for differential expression analysis")

# Demonstrate CPM normalization on our simulated data
# Apply CPM to remove library size effect

let normalized_data = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    zip(gene, lib_sizes) |> map(|pair|
        pair[0] / pair[1] * 1.0  # simplified normalization
    )
)

# Re-run PCA after normalization
let norm_matrix = matrix(normalized_data)
let pca_norm = pca(norm_matrix)

println("PCA after library size normalization:")
println("  PC1 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_norm.variance_explained[0] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")
println("  PC2 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_norm.variance_explained[1] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")

pca_plot(pca_norm, batch_labels, "PCA After Normalization (Colored by Batch)")
    |> save_plot("pca_batch_normalized.svg")

Library size normalization helps but may not remove all batch effects. Deeper batch effects (GC content bias, RNA degradation differences, different kit versions) require more sophisticated correction.

Part 2: ComBat-Style Batch Correction

ComBat (from the sva R package) is the most widely used batch correction method. It uses an empirical Bayes framework to shrink batch effect estimates toward a common value, which is more robust than gene-by-gene correction.

The principle:

For each gene, model expression as:

Y_ij = alpha + Xbeta + batch_jgamma + delta_j*epsilon

Where:

  • alpha: overall mean
  • X*beta: biological effects (treatment, etc.)
  • gamma: additive batch effect (shift)
  • delta: multiplicative batch effect (scaling)
  • epsilon: residual

ComBat estimates gamma and delta for each gene, applies empirical Bayes shrinkage, then subtracts the batch effects from the data.

# Regression-based batch correction (simplified ComBat)
# For each gene, regress out the batch effect

let corrected_data = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    # Fit: expression ~ batch
    let model = lm(gene, batch_numeric)
    let batch_effect = model.coefficients[0]

    # Subtract batch effect from Batch B samples
    gene |> map(|val|
        val  # keep Batch A as-is
    )
    # More precisely: subtract fitted batch component
    zip(gene, batch_numeric) |> map(|pair|
        pair[0] - batch_effect * pair[1]
    )
)

let corrected_matrix = matrix(corrected_data)
let pca_corrected = pca(corrected_matrix)

println("PCA after regression-based batch correction:")
println("  PC1 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_corrected.variance_explained[0] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")
println("  PC2 variance explained: " + to_string(round(pca_corrected.variance_explained[1] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")

pca_plot(pca_corrected, batch_labels, "PCA After Batch Correction (Colored by Batch)")
    |> save_plot("pca_batch_corrected.svg")

pca_plot(pca_corrected, group_labels, "PCA After Batch Correction (Colored by Group)")
    |> save_plot("pca_group_corrected.svg")

After correction, the PCA should no longer separate samples by batch. The biological signal (treatment vs control) should now be more visible.

# Verify batch effect removal
let pc1_corrected = pca_corrected.scores |> map(|row| row[0])

let pc1_corr_a = slice(pc1_corrected, 0, 20)
let pc1_corr_b = slice(pc1_corrected, 20, 40)

let batch_test_after = ttest(pc1_corr_a, pc1_corr_b)
println("Batch effect in PC1 after correction:")
println("  Batch A mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_corr_a), 4)))
println("  Batch B mean: " + to_string(round(mean(pc1_corr_b), 4)))
println("  p-value: " + to_string(round(batch_test_after.p_value, 4)))
println("  (Should be non-significant if correction worked)")

# Check that biological signal is preserved
let pc1_corr_ctrl = concat(slice(pc1_corrected, 0, 10), slice(pc1_corrected, 20, 30))
let pc1_corr_trt = concat(slice(pc1_corrected, 10, 20), slice(pc1_corrected, 30, 40))

let group_test_after = ttest(pc1_corr_ctrl, pc1_corr_trt)
println("Treatment effect in PC1 after correction:")
println("  p-value: " + to_string(round(group_test_after.p_value, 4)))

Part 3: Why You Should Include Batch as a Covariate Instead

There is a philosophical debate in genomics:

Approach A: Correct the data, then analyze. Remove batch effects from the expression matrix, then do differential expression analysis on the corrected data.

Approach B: Include batch as a covariate in the model. Leave the data uncorrected and add batch as a covariate in the statistical model (e.g., DESeq2 design formula: ~ batch + treatment).

Approach B is generally preferred for hypothesis testing because:

  1. It preserves the full statistical model (uncertainty from batch estimation is included in the standard errors)
  2. It does not alter the data (you analyze the original measurements)
  3. It handles continuous confounders (you cannot “correct” for age the way you correct for batch)
  4. It gives correct p-values and confidence intervals

Approach A is useful for:

  • Visualization (PCA plots, heatmaps)
  • Clustering (you want biology-driven clusters)
  • Machine learning (you want batch-free features)
# Demonstrate the two approaches

# Approach A: Correct then analyze
# (Already done above — corrected_data)
let de_corrected = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    let corrected_gene = zip(gene, batch_numeric) |> map(|pair|
        pair[0] - lm(gene, batch_numeric).coefficients[0] * pair[1]
    )
    let ctrl = concat(slice(corrected_gene, 0, 10), slice(corrected_gene, 20, 30))
    let trt = concat(slice(corrected_gene, 10, 20), slice(corrected_gene, 30, 40))
    ttest(ctrl, trt).p_value
)

# Approach B: Include batch as covariate
let de_covariate = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    # Multiple regression: expression ~ treatment + batch
    let model = lm(gene, matrix([group_numeric, batch_numeric]))
    model.p_values[0]  # p-value for treatment effect
)

# Count significant genes with each approach
let sig_corrected = de_corrected |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
let sig_covariate = de_covariate |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()

println("Differential expression results:")
println("  Approach A (correct then test): " + to_string(sig_corrected) + " significant genes")
println("  Approach B (batch as covariate): " + to_string(sig_covariate) + " significant genes")
println("")
println("  Both should give similar results when batch and biology are orthogonal")
println("  Approach B is preferred for formal testing")

Part 4: Confounding — When Batch and Biology Are Entangled

The most dangerous scenario is when batch is correlated with the biological variable of interest. This happens when:

  • All cases were processed in one batch and all controls in another
  • One lab contributed mostly early-stage tumors and another mostly late-stage
  • Different sequencing platforms were used for different groups
# Simulate a CONFOUNDED design
# Batch A: 20 controls
# Batch B: 20 treatments
# Now batch and treatment are perfectly confounded!

let confounded_batch = concat(
    rnorm(20, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| "Batch_A"),
    rnorm(20, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| "Batch_B")
)
let confounded_group = concat(
    rnorm(20, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| "Control"),
    rnorm(20, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| "Treatment")
)

let confounded_batch_num = confounded_batch |> map(|b| if b == "Batch_A" then 0.0 else 1.0)
let confounded_group_num = confounded_group |> map(|g| if g == "Control" then 0.0 else 1.0)

# These are perfectly correlated!
let confound_cor = cor(confounded_batch_num, confounded_group_num)
println("Correlation between batch and treatment: " + to_string(confound_cor))
println("PERFECT CONFOUNDING — cannot separate batch from treatment")
println("")
println("Any gene that differs between batches also differs between groups")
println("Any gene that differs between groups also differs between batches")
println("NO statistical method can disentangle these effects")

# Show that regression fails with perfect confounding
let confounded_gene = concat(
    rnorm(20, 5.0, 1.0),  # Batch A / Control
    rnorm(20, 8.0, 1.0)   # Batch B / Treatment
)

# Is the difference due to batch or treatment?
# We literally cannot tell
let ttest_result = ttest(slice(confounded_gene, 0, 20), slice(confounded_gene, 20, 40))
println("T-test p-value: " + to_string(ttest_result.p_value))
println("  Is this batch or biology? UNKNOWABLE")
println("")
println("Lesson: Confounding must be prevented at the DESIGN stage")
println("  Randomize samples across batches")
println("  If randomization is impossible, ensure batch and biology are")
println("  as uncorrelated as possible")

Part 5: Simpson’s Paradox in Genomics

Simpson’s paradox occurs when a trend that appears in grouped data reverses when the groups are combined. This is a direct consequence of confounding.

# Simpson's paradox example
# Gene X is upregulated in treatment in BOTH batches
# But overall, it appears DOWNregulated in treatment

# Batch A: low overall expression, treatment slightly higher
let batch_a_ctrl = rnorm(30, 3.0, 0.5)
let batch_a_trt = rnorm(10, 3.5, 0.5)

# Batch B: high overall expression, treatment slightly higher
let batch_b_ctrl = rnorm(10, 8.0, 0.5)
let batch_b_trt = rnorm(30, 8.5, 0.5)

# Within each batch: treatment > control
println("Within-batch effects:")
println("  Batch A: Control mean = " + to_string(round(mean(batch_a_ctrl), 2))
    + ", Treatment mean = " + to_string(round(mean(batch_a_trt), 2))
    + " (treatment higher)")
println("  Batch B: Control mean = " + to_string(round(mean(batch_b_ctrl), 2))
    + ", Treatment mean = " + to_string(round(mean(batch_b_trt), 2))
    + " (treatment higher)")

# Combined: control samples come mostly from high-expression Batch B
# treatment samples come mostly from low-expression Batch A
let all_ctrl = concat(batch_a_ctrl, batch_b_ctrl)
let all_trt = concat(batch_a_trt, batch_b_trt)

println("")
println("Combined effect (ignoring batch):")
println("  Control mean = " + to_string(round(mean(all_ctrl), 2))
    + " (n=" + to_string(len(all_ctrl)) + ")")
println("  Treatment mean = " + to_string(round(mean(all_trt), 2))
    + " (n=" + to_string(len(all_trt)) + ")")

let overall_test = ttest(all_ctrl, all_trt)
println("  T-test p-value: " + to_string(round(overall_test.p_value, 4)))
println("")
println("PARADOX: Treatment is HIGHER within each batch")
println("         But LOWER overall because batch confounds the comparison")
println("         More controls came from the high-expression batch")

# The fix: include batch in the model
let combined_expr = concat(concat(batch_a_ctrl, batch_a_trt),
                           concat(batch_b_ctrl, batch_b_trt))
let combined_group = concat(
    concat(rnorm(30, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 0.0),
           rnorm(10, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 1.0)),
    concat(rnorm(10, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 0.0),
           rnorm(30, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 1.0))
)
let combined_batch = concat(
    rnorm(40, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 0.0),
    rnorm(40, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 1.0)
)

let corrected_model = lm(combined_expr, matrix([combined_group, combined_batch]))
println("Multiple regression (expression ~ treatment + batch):")
println("  Treatment coefficient: " + to_string(round(corrected_model.coefficients[0], 4)))
println("  Batch coefficient: " + to_string(round(corrected_model.coefficients[1], 4)))
println("  Treatment p-value: " + to_string(round(corrected_model.p_values[0], 4)))
println("  (Now correctly shows treatment effect within batches)")

Part 6: Population Stratification in GWAS

Population stratification is a confounding problem specific to genetics. If cases and controls come from different ancestral populations, any allele that differs in frequency between populations will appear associated with the disease — even if it has no causal effect.

# Simulate population stratification
# Population A (European): higher frequency of allele, lower disease risk
# Population B (East Asian): lower frequency of allele, higher disease risk

let n_european = 500
let n_east_asian = 500

# Allele frequency differs between populations (not related to disease)
let af_european = 0.30
let af_east_asian = 0.15

# Disease prevalence differs between populations (for other reasons)
let disease_european = 0.05
let disease_east_asian = 0.10

# Generate genotypes
let geno_eur = rbinom(n_european, 2, af_european) |> map(|x| to_float(x))
let geno_eas = rbinom(n_east_asian, 2, af_east_asian) |> map(|x| to_float(x))

# Generate disease status (independent of genotype!)
let disease_eur = rbinom(n_european, 1, disease_european) |> map(|x| to_float(x))
let disease_eas = rbinom(n_east_asian, 1, disease_east_asian) |> map(|x| to_float(x))

let all_geno = concat(geno_eur, geno_eas)
let all_disease = concat(disease_eur, disease_eas)
let all_pop = concat(
    rnorm(n_european, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 0.0),
    rnorm(n_east_asian, 0.0, 0.0) |> map(|_| 1.0)
)

# Naive association (ignoring population)
let naive_chi2 = chi_square(
    [
        all_geno |> filter(|g| g > 0.0) |> len(),
        all_geno |> filter(|g| g == 0.0) |> len()
    ],
    [
        all_disease |> filter(|d| d == 1.0) |> len(),
        all_disease |> filter(|d| d == 0.0) |> len()
    ]
)

# Corrected association (including population as covariate)
let corrected_assoc = glm(all_disease, matrix([all_geno, all_pop]), "binomial")

println("Population stratification example:")
println("  Allele has NO causal effect on disease")
println("  But populations differ in both allele frequency and disease risk")
println("")
println("  Naive test (ignoring population): p = " + to_string(round(naive_chi2.p_value, 4)))
println("  Corrected test (adjusting for population): p = "
    + to_string(round(corrected_assoc.p_values[0], 4)))
println("")
if naive_chi2.p_value < 0.05 && corrected_assoc.p_values[0] > 0.05 then
    println("  The naive test gives a FALSE POSITIVE")
    println("  The corrected test correctly shows no association")
end

# GWAS solves this with principal components
# PCA of genome-wide genotypes captures population structure
# Include top PCs (typically 10-20) as covariates in the association model

println("GWAS population stratification correction:")
println("  1. Run PCA on genome-wide genotype data")
println("  2. Include top 10-20 PCs as covariates")
println("  3. Compute genomic inflation factor (lambda_GC)")
println("     lambda = 1.0 means no inflation (good)")
println("     lambda > 1.1 means residual stratification (adjust more)")
println("  4. Use QQ plot to check for systematic p-value inflation")

Part 7: Applying Corrections to Real Data

# Work with the GTEx data to show tissue effects
# GTEx has tissues as columns — these are not batch effects per se,
# but we can use the structure to demonstrate PCA-based analysis

let gtex_data = read_tsv(gtex_file)

# Extract numeric expression values (skip gene ID and description columns)
let tissue_cols = columns(gtex_data) |> slice(2, ncols(gtex_data))

# Get expression for a subset of genes (top variable genes)
let gtex_subset = gtex_data |> head(500)  # first 500 genes for speed

# Build a matrix of tissues x genes (transposed from the file)
# Each tissue is a "sample" — we are looking at tissue as a batch-like effect
println("GTEx analysis:")
println("  Using " + to_string(len(tissue_cols)) + " tissues as 'samples'")
println("  Using 500 genes for PCA")

# Extract expression values for each tissue
let tissue_data = tissue_cols |> map(|tissue|
    col_values(gtex_subset, tissue) |> map(|x| log2(to_float(x) + 1.0))
)

let tissue_matrix = matrix(tissue_data)

# PCA of tissues based on gene expression
let gtex_pca = pca(tissue_matrix)

println("GTEx PCA:")
println("  PC1 explains " + to_string(round(gtex_pca.variance_explained[0] * 100.0, 1)) + "% of variance")
println("  PC2 explains " + to_string(round(gtex_pca.variance_explained[1] * 100.0, 1)) + "% of variance")

pca_plot(gtex_pca, tissue_cols, "GTEx Tissues PCA") |> save_plot("gtex_pca.svg")

In the GTEx PCA, brain tissues cluster together, muscle tissues cluster together, etc. This is not a batch effect — it is biology. The lesson is that PCA alone cannot distinguish batch effects from real biology. You need to know what the batches are and check whether PCA components correlate with them.

Part 8: Detecting Unknown Batches with Surrogate Variables

Sometimes batch information is not recorded or is incomplete. Surrogate Variable Analysis (SVA) uses the expression data itself to detect hidden sources of variation.

# The principle of surrogate variable analysis:
# 1. Fit a model with only the biological variable of interest
# 2. Examine the residuals — they contain batch effects + noise
# 3. Apply PCA/SVD to the residuals
# 4. The top components are "surrogate variables" — proxies for unknown batches
# 5. Include surrogate variables as covariates in the final model

println("Surrogate Variable Analysis (SVA) principle:")
println("  1. Fit expression ~ treatment (ignoring batch)")
println("  2. Residuals = batch effects + noise")
println("  3. PCA of residuals → surrogate variables")
println("  4. Fit expression ~ treatment + SV1 + SV2 + ...")
println("  5. SVs capture unknown batch effects without needing batch labels")
println("")
println("BioLang implementation:")

# Compute residuals from biology-only model
let residual_data = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    let model = lm(gene, group_numeric)
    # Residuals capture everything not explained by treatment
    zip(gene, group_numeric) |> map(|pair|
        pair[0] - (model.intercept + model.coefficients[0] * pair[1])
    )
)

let residual_matrix = matrix(residual_data)
let svd_result = pca(residual_matrix)

println("Surrogate variables from residual PCA:")
println("  SV1 variance explained: " + to_string(round(svd_result.variance_explained[0] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")
println("  SV2 variance explained: " + to_string(round(svd_result.variance_explained[1] * 100.0, 1)) + "%")

# SV1 should correlate with batch if batch is the dominant hidden effect
let sv1 = svd_result.scores |> map(|row| row[0])
let sv1_batch_cor = cor(sv1, batch_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x)))
println("Correlation of SV1 with actual batch: " + to_string(round(abs(sv1_batch_cor), 4)))
println("  (High correlation confirms SV1 captures the batch effect)")

Part 9: Before vs After — A Complete Workflow

# Complete batch correction workflow on the simulated data

println("=== COMPLETE BATCH CORRECTION WORKFLOW ===")
println("")

# Step 1: Detect batch effects
println("Step 1: Detect batch effects")
let raw_pca = pca(sim_matrix)
let pc1_batch_assoc = ttest(
    slice(raw_pca.scores |> map(|r| r[0]), 0, 20),
    slice(raw_pca.scores |> map(|r| r[0]), 20, 40)
)
println("  PC1 vs batch p-value: " + to_string(pc1_batch_assoc.p_value))
if pc1_batch_assoc.p_value < 0.05 then
    println("  BATCH EFFECT DETECTED in PC1")
end

# Step 2: Check for confounding
println("")
println("Step 2: Check for confounding")
let batch_group_cor = cor(
    batch_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x)),
    group_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x))
)
println("  Correlation(batch, treatment): " + to_string(round(batch_group_cor, 4)))
if abs(batch_group_cor) < 0.3 then
    println("  LOW confounding — correction is feasible")
else if abs(batch_group_cor) < 0.7 then
    println("  MODERATE confounding — proceed with caution")
else
    println("  HIGH confounding — results may be unreliable")
end

# Step 3: Choose correction strategy
println("")
println("Step 3: Choose correction strategy")
println("  For hypothesis testing: include batch as covariate")
println("  For visualization: apply regression-based correction")

# Step 4: Apply correction for visualization
println("")
println("Step 4: Correct data for visualization")
let viz_corrected = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    let batch_effect = lm(gene, batch_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x))).coefficients[0]
    zip(gene, batch_numeric) |> map(|pair|
        pair[0] - batch_effect * to_float(pair[1])
    )
)

let viz_pca = pca(matrix(viz_corrected))
pca_plot(viz_pca, batch_labels, "PCA After Correction (Batch)") |> save_plot("pca_corrected_batch.svg")
pca_plot(viz_pca, group_labels, "PCA After Correction (Group)") |> save_plot("pca_corrected_group.svg")

# Step 5: DE analysis with batch as covariate
println("")
println("Step 5: Differential expression with batch covariate")
let sig_genes = 0
let de_results = sim_data |> map(|gene|
    let model = lm(gene, matrix([
        group_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x)),
        batch_numeric |> map(|x| to_float(x))
    ]))
    record(
        "treatment_coef", model.coefficients[0],
        "batch_coef", model.coefficients[1],
        "treatment_p", model.p_values[0]
    )
)

let de_table = table(de_results)
let de_p = col_values(de_table, "treatment_p") |> map(|x| to_float(x))
let de_p_adj = p_adjust(de_p, "BH")

let sig_after_correction = de_p_adj |> filter(|p| p < 0.05) |> len()
println("  Significant genes (BH < 0.05): " + to_string(sig_after_correction))

# Step 6: Verify correction
println("")
println("Step 6: Verify batch effect removal")
let pc1_after = ttest(
    slice(viz_pca.scores |> map(|r| r[0]), 0, 20),
    slice(viz_pca.scores |> map(|r| r[0]), 20, 40)
)
println("  PC1 vs batch p-value after correction: " + to_string(round(pc1_after.p_value, 4)))
println("  (Should be non-significant)")

Interpretation

The Hierarchy of Batch Effect Solutions

From best to worst:

  1. Prevention: Randomize samples across batches at the design stage. This makes batch orthogonal to biology. No correction needed.

  2. Covariate adjustment: Include batch as a covariate in the statistical model. Preserves the data, gives correct p-values, handles both known and estimated (surrogate variable) batch effects.

  3. Data correction: Remove batch effects from the data (ComBat, limma removeBatchEffect). Useful for visualization and clustering but can distort standard errors. Always analyze the uncorrected data with covariates for hypothesis testing.

  4. Ignoring batch effects: Produces false discoveries, missed true signals, and irreproducible results. Never acceptable.

When Correction Fails

Correction fails when:

  • Batch is perfectly confounded with biology (no statistical fix)
  • The batch effect is non-linear (standard methods assume linearity)
  • The batch effect is gene-specific and complex (standard methods assume simple additive/multiplicative effects)
  • Sample sizes per batch are too small for reliable estimation

What We Learned from the Simulations

  1. A batch shift of 2 log2-units dominates PCA, making the biological signal invisible
  2. Library size normalization removes part but not all of the batch effect
  3. Regression-based correction effectively removes the batch effect when batch and biology are orthogonal
  4. When batch and biology are confounded, no correction can recover the biological signal
  5. Simpson’s paradox is a real threat — always check for confounders before interpreting overall trends

Decision

Checklist for Every Multi-Batch Study

Before analyzing any multi-batch dataset, answer these questions:

println("Batch Effect Checklist:")
println("─────────────────────")
println("")
println("1. [ ] Do you know all the batches?")
println("   (sequencing center, date, lane, operator, kit version)")
println("")
println("2. [ ] Is batch confounded with your biological variable?")
println("   Compute correlation(batch, biology)")
println("   If |r| > 0.3, you have a problem")
println("")
println("3. [ ] Does PCA separate samples by batch?")
println("   Color PCA by batch — is there clustering?")
println("   Test PC1-PC5 for association with batch")
println("")
println("4. [ ] Have you chosen a correction strategy?")
println("   For DE testing: batch as covariate in the model")
println("   For visualization: ComBat or regression correction")
println("   For both: correct for viz, covariate for testing")
println("")
println("5. [ ] Have you verified the correction?")
println("   PCA after correction should not separate by batch")
println("   Biological signal should be preserved or enhanced")
println("")
println("6. [ ] Have you documented the batch structure?")
println("   Report which samples were in which batch")
println("   Report the correction method used")
println("   Report before/after PCA plots")

When to Abort a Study

Sometimes batch effects are so severe or confounded that the study cannot produce reliable results. Consider abandoning the analysis when:

  • Batch is perfectly confounded with the biological variable
  • Batch effects are so large that biological signal is undetectable even after correction
  • The number of samples per batch is too small (< 5) for reliable batch estimation
  • Key clinical covariates are missing, making it impossible to check for confounding

In these cases, the honest conclusion is: “This study design does not support the intended analysis. New data with a balanced batch design is needed.”

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Over-Correction

# Batch correction can remove real biology if the biological effect
# is correlated with batch

println("Over-correction danger:")
println("  If 70% of ER+ samples are in Batch A and 70% of ER- in Batch B,")
println("  correcting for batch will partially remove the ER effect")
println("  Solution: Include ER status in the correction model")
println("  ComBat allows specifying biological variables to preserve")
println("")
println("  ComBat formula: correct(data, batch, model.matrix(~ biology))")
println("  This removes batch while preserving the biological signal")

Pitfall 2: Correcting Normalized and Raw Data Differently

println("Consistency in correction:")
println("  WRONG: Normalize data, then correct for batch")
println("         (normalization may interact with batch correction)")
println("  WRONG: Correct for batch, then normalize")
println("         (correction may break normalization assumptions)")
println("")
println("  RIGHT for visualization: Normalize → log transform → ComBat")
println("  RIGHT for DE testing: Use DESeq2/edgeR with ~batch+condition")
println("         (these tools normalize and model simultaneously)")

Pitfall 3: Applying Batch Correction Across Studies

println("Cross-study batch correction:")
println("  Combining data from different studies is extremely challenging")
println("  Each study may use different platforms, protocols, and populations")
println("")
println("  Warning signs:")
println("  - PCA perfectly separates studies (always check this first)")
println("  - Effect sizes differ dramatically between studies")
println("  - Gene rankings change completely between studies")
println("")
println("  Better approach: meta-analysis (Chapter 15)")
println("  - Analyze each study separately")
println("  - Combine results (effect sizes) rather than data")
println("  - This avoids the need for cross-study batch correction")

Pitfall 4: Ignoring Sample-Level QC

# Individual samples can be outliers due to technical failure
# Remove these before batch correction

println("Sample-level QC before batch correction:")
println("  1. Check library size per sample — flag outliers")
println("  2. Check % mapped reads — low mapping suggests contamination")
println("  3. Check % ribosomal RNA — high rRNA suggests poor depletion")
println("  4. Check PCA — samples far from their expected cluster may be")
println("     mislabeled or contaminated")
println("  5. Remove outlier samples BEFORE batch correction")
println("     (outliers distort batch effect estimates)")

Pitfall 5: Not Reporting Batch Information

println("Reporting requirements:")
println("  Every genomics paper should report:")
println("  - How many batches/processing groups existed")
println("  - What variables define the batches")
println("  - Whether batch is confounded with biology (correlation)")
println("  - What correction method was used (if any)")
println("  - Before and after PCA plots showing batch effect and correction")
println("")
println("  Reviewers should reject papers that:")
println("  - Do not mention batch effects")
println("  - Combine multi-center data without discussion")
println("  - Show PCA plots without coloring by batch")

Pitfall 6: Forgetting About Non-Obvious Confounders

println("Non-obvious confounders in genomics:")
println("")
println("  RNA-seq:")
println("  - RNA integrity number (RIN) — degraded RNA affects gene-level estimates")
println("  - Time from sample collection to freezing")
println("  - Amount of input RNA")
println("  - 3' bias from degradation")
println("")
println("  GWAS:")
println("  - Population stratification (ancestry)")
println("  - Relatedness (family structure)")
println("  - Genotyping array version")
println("  - Imputation reference panel")
println("")
println("  Clinical data:")
println("  - Age (confounder for almost everything)")
println("  - Sex (affects expression of ~1/3 of genes)")
println("  - BMI (correlates with inflammation gene expression)")
println("  - Medications (especially steroids, which affect gene expression)")
println("  - Time of day of sample collection (circadian genes)")

Where Next?

Immediate Extensions

  • Dimensionality reduction (Chapter 13): PCA is just the starting point. UMAP and t-SNE are commonly used for visualization in single-cell RNA-seq, where batch effects between experiments are a major challenge.

  • Clustering (Chapter 14): Batch effects can create artificial clusters. Always check whether clusters correspond to biological groups or technical batches before interpreting them.

  • Meta-analysis (Chapter 15): The principled alternative to combining raw data across studies. Analyze each study separately, then combine effect sizes. This avoids cross-study batch correction entirely.

Advanced Topics

  • Harmony and Scanorama: Batch correction methods designed specifically for single-cell RNA-seq, where each experiment has different cell compositions and capture efficiencies.

  • Mutual nearest neighbors (MNN): A method that identifies shared cell types across batches and uses them as anchors for correction. More principled than global correction for heterogeneous samples.

  • Causal inference: When you cannot randomize (observational studies), methods like propensity score matching, instrumental variables, and Mendelian randomization can help distinguish causal effects from confounding.

Practice Problems

  1. GTEx batch detection: Download GTEx sample-level data (not just medians) and color PCA by sequencing center, ischemic time, and tissue type. Which technical variable explains the most variance?

  2. Simulated confounding: Create a simulation where batch is 50% confounded with treatment (60% of treatments in Batch A, 40% in Batch B). How does partial confounding affect the DE results? How well does covariate adjustment work?

  3. Over-correction: Simulate data where treatment causes a 2-fold change in 100 genes, with batch perfectly correlated with treatment. Apply batch correction and show that the biological signal is removed. Then simulate the balanced design and show that correction preserves the biology.

  4. Population stratification: Using 1000 Genomes Project data, compute PCA of genotypes. Show that the top PCs separate continental populations. Simulate a GWAS with population-stratified cases and controls, and show that PC adjustment removes the inflation.

  5. Library size correlation: Take any RNA-seq count matrix. For each gene, compute its correlation with total library size. How many genes are significantly correlated? Apply TPM or CPM normalization and repeat. How many genes are still correlated?

Key takeaways from this chapter:

  • Batch effects are systematic technical variations that affect all samples processed together. They are present in virtually all genomic data.
  • PCA is the primary tool for detecting batch effects. If PC1 separates batches, the batch effect dominates the biology.
  • Confounding occurs when batch correlates with the biological variable. No statistical method can fix perfect confounding.
  • For hypothesis testing, include batch as a covariate in the model rather than correcting the data.
  • For visualization and clustering, use regression-based or ComBat correction on the data.
  • Simpson’s paradox can reverse the apparent direction of an effect when confounders are ignored.
  • Population stratification is a form of confounding in GWAS, corrected by including ancestry PCs as covariates.
  • Prevention (balanced experimental design) is always better than post-hoc correction.
  • Report batch information, correction methods, and before/after PCA plots in every multi-batch study.

Chapter 13: Dimensionality Reduction

The Problem

You have a single-cell RNA-seq dataset: 2,700 peripheral blood mononuclear cells, each measured across roughly 20,000 genes. You want to visualize which cells are similar to each other. You want to discover cell types. You want to understand what gene programs drive the differences.

But you cannot scatter-plot 20,000 dimensions. You cannot compute distances meaningfully in a space this large. And most of those 20,000 genes are noise — housekeeping genes expressed at the same level in every cell, or dropout zeros where the measurement failed entirely.

How do you reduce 20,000 dimensions to 2 or 3 while preserving the meaningful biological structure?

This is the dimensionality reduction problem, and it is the gateway to every single-cell analysis, every bulk transcriptomics comparison, every clinical genomics quality check. Get it wrong, and you will see patterns that do not exist. Get it right, and entire cell atlases emerge from the noise.

Why It Matters

Dimensionality reduction is not just visualization. It is a fundamental analytical step that determines the success or failure of downstream analysis:

In single-cell RNA-seq, PCA followed by UMAP is the standard pipeline. Every cell atlas — the Human Cell Atlas, Tabula Sapiens, the Mouse Cell Atlas — depends on these methods to organize millions of cells into interpretable structures. If your PCA captures batch effects instead of biology, every cluster you find downstream is wrong.

In bulk RNA-seq, PCA is the primary quality control tool. Before running any differential expression analysis, you plot PC1 vs PC2. If samples do not group by biological condition — if instead they group by sequencing lane, RNA extraction date, or technician — you have a batch effect that must be addressed before any statistical test is valid.

In GWAS, PCA on genotype data reveals population structure. Failing to correct for population structure in a case-control study produces thousands of false positive associations. The top principal components of genotype data are routinely included as covariates in every GWAS regression model.

In clinical genomics, dimensionality reduction on tumor expression profiles reveals molecular subtypes that predict treatment response. The PAM50 breast cancer classifier, used clinically worldwide, is fundamentally a projection of expression data onto a low-dimensional subtype space.

The Data

We use two datasets in this chapter.

Dataset 1: PBMC 3k (single-cell RNA-seq)

The 3,000 PBMC dataset from 10x Genomics is the standard benchmark for single-cell methods. It contains peripheral blood mononuclear cells from a healthy donor, processed with the Chromium Single Cell 3’ protocol.

Reference: https://www.10xgenomics.com/datasets/3-k-pbm-cs-from-a-healthy-donor-1-standard-1-1-0

For this chapter, we work with a pre-processed expression matrix where cells are rows and genes are columns — the standard format after Cell Ranger processing. We assume basic quality control has been performed: cells with fewer than 200 genes detected are removed, genes detected in fewer than 3 cells are removed, and cells with more than 5% mitochondrial reads are filtered out.

# Download the PBMC 3k filtered expression matrix
# The 10x Genomics dataset page provides the filtered gene-barcode matrix
# After Cell Ranger processing, we have a cells x genes table

# For this chapter we work with a pre-processed TSV
# (In practice you would use read_10x or similar to parse the HDF5/MEX format)

let pbmc = read_tsv("pbmc3k_filtered.tsv")

println("PBMC dataset dimensions:")
println("  Cells: " + str(len(pbmc)))
println("  Genes: " + str(ncols(pbmc) - 1))  # subtract 1 for cell barcode column

Dataset 2: GTEx median TPM (bulk RNA-seq)

The GTEx (Genotype-Tissue Expression) project provides gene expression data across 54 human tissues from nearly 1,000 donors.

URL: https://storage.googleapis.com/adult-gtex/bulk-gex/v8/rna-seq/GTEx_Analysis_2017-06-05_v8_RNASeQCv1.1.9_gene_median_tpm.gct.gz

# Download GTEx v8 median TPM per tissue
let gtex_url = "https://storage.googleapis.com/adult-gtex/bulk-gex/v8/rna-seq/GTEx_Analysis_2017-06-05_v8_RNASeQCv1.1.9_gene_median_tpm.gct.gz"
let gtex_file = download(gtex_url, "gtex_median_tpm.gct.gz")

# GCT format: skip first 2 header lines, then tab-separated
# Columns: gene_id, gene_name, then one column per tissue
let gtex = read_tsv(gtex_file)

println("GTEx dimensions:")
println("  Genes: " + str(len(gtex)))
println("  Tissues: " + str(ncols(gtex) - 2))  # subtract gene_id and gene_name

Exploration

Understanding the Curse of Dimensionality

Before applying any method, we need to understand why high-dimensional data is fundamentally different from low-dimensional data.

In high dimensions, distances become meaningless. In a 20,000-dimensional space, the ratio of the farthest point to the nearest point approaches 1 as dimensionality increases. Every point is approximately equidistant from every other point. This means that nearest-neighbor methods, distance-based clustering, and any visualization that relies on preserving distances will fail if applied directly to the raw gene expression matrix.

# Demonstrate the curse of dimensionality
# Compute pairwise distances in the full gene space

let all_genes = columns(pbmc) |> filter(|c| c != "barcode")

# Sample 100 cells for illustration
let sample_cells = sample(pbmc, 100)

# Extract expression values for all genes
let expr_matrix = sample_cells |> select(all_genes)

# Compute distance matrix in the full space
let full_dist = dist_matrix(expr_matrix, "euclidean")

# Extract all pairwise distances
let distances = []
let n = len(full_dist)
let i = 0
while i < n do
  let j = i + 1
  while j < n do
    distances = concat(distances, [full_dist[i][j]])
    j = j + 1
  end
  i = i + 1
end

# Look at the distribution of distances
let dist_stats = {
  mean: mean(distances),
  sd: stdev(distances),
  min: min(distances),
  max: max(distances),
  cv: stdev(distances) / mean(distances)
}

println("Distance statistics in full 20,000-D space:")
println("  Mean distance: " + str(round(dist_stats.mean, 2)))
println("  SD of distances: " + str(round(dist_stats.sd, 2)))
println("  CV (SD/mean): " + str(round(dist_stats.cv, 4)))
println("  Min/Max ratio: " + str(round(dist_stats.min / dist_stats.max, 4)))

# The CV will be very small — distances are concentrated
# This means "nearest neighbor" is barely distinguishable from "farthest neighbor"

Looking at Variance Per Gene

Not all genes are informative. Most genes are either not expressed, expressed at a constant level, or dominated by technical noise. The first step in any dimensionality reduction is to identify the genes that carry biological signal.

# Compute variance for each gene across cells
let gene_names = columns(pbmc) |> filter(|c| c != "barcode")
let gene_variances = gene_names |> map(|g| {
  name: g,
  variance: variance(col_values(pbmc, g)),
  mean_expr: mean(col_values(pbmc, g))
})

# Sort by variance, descending
let sorted_genes = gene_variances |> sort(|a, b| b.variance - a.variance)

# Top 20 most variable genes
println("Top 20 most variable genes:")
sorted_genes |> head(20) |> each(|g|
  println("  " + g.name + ": var=" + str(round(g.variance, 2)) +
          " mean=" + str(round(g.mean_expr, 2)))
)

# How many genes have meaningful variance?
let high_var = sorted_genes |> filter(|g| g.variance > 1.0)
println("Genes with variance > 1.0: " + str(len(high_var)))
println("That is " + str(round(100.0 * len(high_var) / len(sorted_genes), 1)) + "% of all genes")

The typical result: fewer than 2,000 genes (10% of the genome) carry most of the variance. The rest are noise. This is why dimensionality reduction works — the data actually lives on a much lower-dimensional manifold.

The Mean-Variance Relationship

In RNA-seq data, variance scales with the mean. Highly expressed genes have higher absolute variance simply because they have more counts. This is a problem for PCA, which finds directions of maximum variance — it would be dominated by highly expressed genes regardless of their biological relevance.

# Plot mean vs variance to see the relationship
let mean_var = gene_variances |> map(|g| {
  log_mean: log10(g.mean_expr + 0.01),
  log_var: log10(g.variance + 0.01)
})

let mv_table = table(mean_var)
scatter(mv_table, "log_mean", "log_var",
  title: "Mean-Variance Relationship (PBMC 3k)",
  x_label: "log10(Mean Expression)",
  y_label: "log10(Variance)")
  |> save_plot("mean_variance_relationship.svg")

# This is why we normalize and select highly variable genes (HVGs)
# before PCA — to remove the mean-variance confound

Normalization: Preparing for PCA

Before PCA, we must normalize the data. The standard single-cell preprocessing pipeline is: (1) normalize to total counts per cell, (2) log-transform, (3) select highly variable genes.

# Step 1: Library-size normalization
# Scale each cell to the same total count (e.g., 10,000)
let target_sum = 10000.0

let normalized = pbmc |> mutate(|row| {
  let total = gene_names |> map(|g| row[g]) |> sum()
  let scale = target_sum / total
  let result = { barcode: row.barcode }
  gene_names |> each(|g|
    result[g] = row[g] * scale
  )
  result
})

# Step 2: Log-transform (log1p to handle zeros)
let log_normalized = normalized |> mutate(|row| {
  let result = { barcode: row.barcode }
  gene_names |> each(|g|
    result[g] = log(1.0 + row[g])
  )
  result
})

# Step 3: Select highly variable genes (top 2000)
let hvg_variances = gene_names |> map(|g| {
  name: g,
  variance: variance(col_values(log_normalized, g))
})

let hvg_sorted = hvg_variances |> sort(|a, b| b.variance - a.variance)
let hvgs = hvg_sorted |> head(2000) |> map(|g| g.name)

println("Selected " + str(len(hvgs)) + " highly variable genes for PCA")

# Step 4: Center and scale (z-score each gene)
let scaled = log_normalized |> select(concat(["barcode"], hvgs))
let scaled_data = normalize(scaled |> select(hvgs), "zscore")

println("Prepared expression matrix: " + str(len(scaled_data)) + " cells x " +
        str(ncols(scaled_data)) + " genes")

The Analysis

PCA: Finding the Axes of Maximum Variance

Principal Component Analysis finds orthogonal directions (principal components) that capture the maximum variance in the data. Each PC is a linear combination of the original genes. The first PC captures the most variance, the second captures the most variance orthogonal to the first, and so on.

# Run PCA on the scaled expression matrix
let pca_result = pca(scaled_data, n_components: 50)

# pca_result contains:
#   .components   — the PC coordinates for each cell (n_cells x n_components)
#   .variance     — variance explained by each component
#   .loadings     — gene weights for each component (n_genes x n_components)

println("PCA complete. Top 10 components — variance explained:")
let total_var = sum(pca_result.variance)
let cumulative = 0.0

pca_result.variance |> head(10) |> each(|v|
  let i = 0
  let pct = 100.0 * v / total_var
  cumulative = cumulative + pct
  println("  PC" + str(i + 1) + ": " + str(round(pct, 2)) + "% (cumulative: " +
          str(round(cumulative, 2)) + "%)")
  i = i + 1
)

What PCA Actually Does — The Mathematics

PCA is an eigenvalue decomposition. For a centered data matrix X (cells x genes), PCA computes the covariance matrix C = X^T X / (n-1), then finds its eigenvectors and eigenvalues. The eigenvectors are the principal component directions. The eigenvalues are the variance explained by each direction.

But you don’t need to compute the full covariance matrix (which would be 20,000 x 20,000). The singular value decomposition (SVD) of X gives the same result more efficiently: X = U S V^T, where the columns of V are the PC loadings (eigenvectors of the covariance matrix) and the diagonal of S squared gives the eigenvalues.

# BioLang's pca() uses SVD internally
# We can verify by computing SVD directly on a small example

let small_data = scaled_data |> head(100) |> select(hvgs |> head(50))
let small_matrix = matrix(small_data)

let svd_result = svd(small_matrix)
# svd_result.u — left singular vectors (cell space)
# svd_result.s — singular values
# svd_result.v — right singular vectors (gene space / loadings)

# Eigenvalues from SVD
let eigenvalues = svd_result.s |> map(|s| s * s / (len(small_matrix) - 1))

println("Top 5 eigenvalues from direct SVD:")
eigenvalues |> head(5) |> each(|e|
  println("  " + str(round(e, 4)))
)

Choosing the Number of PCs: The Elbow Plot

How many PCs should you keep? There is no single correct answer, but the elbow plot provides guidance. Plot the variance explained by each PC. Look for the “elbow” — the point where the curve flattens, indicating that additional PCs are capturing noise rather than signal.

# Create the elbow plot (scree plot)
let var_explained = pca_result.variance |> head(50)
let var_pct = var_explained |> map(|v| 100.0 * v / total_var)

let elbow_data = var_pct |> map(|v|
  let i = 0
  let result = { pc: i + 1, variance_pct: v }
  i = i + 1
  result
)

let elbow_table = table(elbow_data)
scatter(elbow_table, "pc", "variance_pct",
  title: "Scree Plot — Variance Explained by PC",
  x_label: "Principal Component",
  y_label: "% Variance Explained")
  |> save_plot("scree_plot.svg")

# Also plot cumulative variance
let cum_var = []
let running = 0.0
var_pct |> each(|v|
  running = running + v
  cum_var = concat(cum_var, [running])
)

let cum_data = cum_var |> map(|v|
  let i = 0
  let result = { pc: i + 1, cumulative_pct: v }
  i = i + 1
  result
)

let cum_table = table(cum_data)
scatter(cum_table, "pc", "cumulative_pct",
  title: "Cumulative Variance Explained",
  x_label: "Principal Component",
  y_label: "Cumulative % Variance")
  |> save_plot("cumulative_variance.svg")

# Typical single-cell: 10-30 PCs capture most biological signal
# A common heuristic: keep PCs until cumulative variance reaches 80-90%
# or until the scree plot elbow

In the PBMC 3k dataset, you will typically see an elbow around PC 10-15. The first 2-3 PCs separate major cell types (T cells from monocytes from B cells). PCs 4-15 capture finer distinctions (CD4+ vs CD8+ T cells, classical vs non-classical monocytes). Beyond PC 20, you are mostly fitting noise.

Interpreting PC Loadings — What Genes Drive Each Component

Each principal component is a linear combination of genes. The loadings (weights) tell you which genes contribute most to each PC. These loadings have biological meaning — they represent gene programs.

# Get the loadings for PC1 and PC2
# Loadings: which genes have the highest weights in each PC

let pc1_loadings = hvgs |> map(|g|
  let i = 0  # index into hvgs
  { gene: g, loading: pca_result.loadings[i][0] }
)

let pc2_loadings = hvgs |> map(|g|
  let i = 0
  { gene: g, loading: pca_result.loadings[i][1] }
)

# Sort by absolute loading to find the driving genes
let pc1_sorted = pc1_loadings |> sort(|a, b| abs(b.loading) - abs(a.loading))
let pc2_sorted = pc2_loadings |> sort(|a, b| abs(b.loading) - abs(a.loading))

println("Top 10 genes driving PC1:")
pc1_sorted |> head(10) |> each(|g|
  println("  " + g.gene + ": " + str(round(g.loading, 4)))
)

println("Top 10 genes driving PC2:")
pc2_sorted |> head(10) |> each(|g|
  println("  " + g.gene + ": " + str(round(g.loading, 4)))
)

# In PBMC data, PC1 typically separates lymphoid vs myeloid cells
# Key PC1 genes: LYZ, S100A9 (myeloid markers) vs NKG7, GNLY (lymphoid)
# PC2 often separates T cells from B cells and NK cells

Visualizing PCA: The Workhorse Plot

The PC1 vs PC2 scatter plot is the most common visualization in genomics. It reveals the global structure of your data at a glance.

# Create PC1 vs PC2 scatter plot
let pc_coords = pca_result.components

let pc_table = table(pc_coords |> map(|row| {
  PC1: row[0],
  PC2: row[1],
  PC3: row[2]
}))

scatter(pc_table, "PC1", "PC2",
  title: "PBMC 3k — PCA (PC1 vs PC2)",
  x_label: "PC1 (" + str(round(var_pct[0], 1)) + "% variance)",
  y_label: "PC2 (" + str(round(var_pct[1], 1)) + "% variance)")
  |> save_plot("pbmc_pca.svg")

# Also check PC1 vs PC3 — sometimes the third component
# reveals structure hidden in the first two
scatter(pc_table, "PC1", "PC3",
  title: "PBMC 3k — PCA (PC1 vs PC3)",
  x_label: "PC1 (" + str(round(var_pct[0], 1)) + "% variance)",
  y_label: "PC3 (" + str(round(var_pct[2], 1)) + "% variance)")
  |> save_plot("pbmc_pca_pc1_pc3.svg")

t-SNE: Preserving Local Neighborhoods

t-distributed Stochastic Neighbor Embedding (t-SNE) is a nonlinear method that excels at preserving local structure. Unlike PCA, it does not seek directions of maximum variance. Instead, it tries to place cells that are close in the high-dimensional space close together in 2D.

The algorithm:

  1. Compute pairwise similarities in high-D using Gaussian kernels
  2. Define pairwise similarities in 2D using a Student’s t-distribution
  3. Minimize the KL divergence between the two distributions

The t-distribution in step 2 (hence the name) has heavy tails, which helps prevent the “crowding problem” — in low dimensions, moderately distant points would all pile up in a narrow shell if Gaussian kernels were used.

# Run t-SNE on the PCA-reduced data (not on raw expression!)
# This is critical: t-SNE on 20,000 raw genes is slow and noisy
# Standard practice: PCA to 30-50 dims, then t-SNE on PCs

let pc_input = pca_result.components |> map(|row| row |> head(30))
let tsne_default = tsne(pc_input, perplexity: 30)

let tsne_table = table(tsne_default |> map(|row| {
  tSNE1: row[0],
  tSNE2: row[1]
}))

scatter(tsne_table, "tSNE1", "tSNE2",
  title: "PBMC 3k — t-SNE (perplexity=30)",
  x_label: "t-SNE 1",
  y_label: "t-SNE 2")
  |> save_plot("pbmc_tsne_default.svg")

The Perplexity Parameter: When t-SNE Lies

The perplexity parameter controls how many neighbors each point considers. It roughly corresponds to the expected number of neighbors. Low perplexity emphasizes very local structure; high perplexity captures more global patterns.

This is where t-SNE lies. Different perplexity values produce dramatically different plots. Cluster sizes and distances between clusters in a t-SNE plot are NOT meaningful. A cluster that appears twice as large as another may contain the same number of cells. Two clusters that appear far apart may be more similar than two clusters that appear close.

# Run t-SNE with different perplexity values to see the effect
let perplexities = [5, 15, 30, 50, 100]

perplexities |> each(|p|
  let result = tsne(pc_input, perplexity: p)
  let result_table = table(result |> map(|row| {
    tSNE1: row[0],
    tSNE2: row[1]
  }))

  scatter(result_table, "tSNE1", "tSNE2",
    title: "t-SNE perplexity=" + str(p),
    x_label: "t-SNE 1",
    y_label: "t-SNE 2")
    |> save_plot("tsne_perplexity_" + str(p) + ".svg")

  println("Perplexity " + str(p) + " complete")
)

# With perplexity=5: many tiny, tight clusters — overemphasis on local structure
# With perplexity=30: balanced view, standard choice
# With perplexity=100: clusters merge, global structure dominates
# ALL of these are valid — which is why t-SNE is dangerous for quantitative claims

UMAP: The Modern Alternative

Uniform Manifold Approximation and Projection (UMAP) has largely replaced t-SNE as the default visualization method in single-cell genomics. It is faster, better preserves global structure, and produces more reproducible layouts.

UMAP constructs a topological representation of the high-dimensional data using simplicial complexes, then finds a low-dimensional layout that preserves this topological structure. In practice, this means UMAP better preserves the relationships between clusters — clusters that are biologically related tend to appear near each other.

# Run UMAP on the PCA-reduced data
let umap_result = umap(pc_input, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.5)

let umap_table = table(umap_result |> map(|row| {
  UMAP1: row[0],
  UMAP2: row[1]
}))

scatter(umap_table, "UMAP1", "UMAP2",
  title: "PBMC 3k — UMAP",
  x_label: "UMAP 1",
  y_label: "UMAP 2")
  |> save_plot("pbmc_umap.svg")

UMAP Parameters: n_neighbors and min_dist

Like t-SNE’s perplexity, UMAP has parameters that affect the visualization. Understanding them prevents misinterpretation.

n_neighbors controls how many neighbors define the local structure. Low values (5-15) emphasize fine local structure. High values (50-200) capture broader patterns. Default: 15.

min_dist controls how tightly points are packed. Low values (0.0-0.1) produce tighter clusters with more empty space between them. High values (0.5-1.0) produce a more uniform spread. Default: 0.1.

# Compare UMAP with different parameter settings
let umap_configs = [
  { nn: 5,  md: 0.1, label: "local-tight" },
  { nn: 15, md: 0.1, label: "default-tight" },
  { nn: 15, md: 0.5, label: "default-spread" },
  { nn: 50, md: 0.1, label: "global-tight" },
  { nn: 50, md: 0.5, label: "global-spread" }
]

umap_configs |> each(|cfg|
  let result = umap(pc_input, n_neighbors: cfg.nn, min_dist: cfg.md)
  let result_table = table(result |> map(|row| {
    UMAP1: row[0],
    UMAP2: row[1]
  }))

  scatter(result_table, "UMAP1", "UMAP2",
    title: "UMAP n_neighbors=" + str(cfg.nn) + " min_dist=" + str(cfg.md),
    x_label: "UMAP 1",
    y_label: "UMAP 2")
    |> save_plot("umap_" + cfg.label + ".svg")
)

# Key observation: all of these show the same major clusters
# but with very different visual appearance
# UMAP is more robust than t-SNE to parameter choice, but still varies

PCA vs t-SNE vs UMAP: When to Use Each

Each method has strengths and appropriate use cases:

MethodPreservesSpeedReproducibleBest For
PCAGlobal variance structureFastYes (deterministic)QC, batch detection, pre-processing
t-SNELocal neighborhoodsSlowNo (stochastic)Publication figures with clear clusters
UMAPLocal + some globalFastMostly (seed-dependent)Exploratory analysis, large datasets

Use PCA when: you need quantitative interpretation (loadings, variance explained), you are checking for batch effects, you need a deterministic result, or you are preparing input for downstream methods.

Use t-SNE when: you want to visualize clearly separated clusters in publication figures, and you understand that distances between clusters are not meaningful.

Use UMAP when: you are doing exploratory analysis, working with large datasets (>100k cells), or need to preserve relationships between clusters.

# Side-by-side comparison: color cells by a known marker gene

# Extract marker gene expression
let cd3d_expr = col_values(log_normalized, "CD3D")   # T cell marker
let cd14_expr = col_values(log_normalized, "CD14")    # Monocyte marker
let ms4a1_expr = col_values(log_normalized, "MS4A1")  # B cell marker (CD20)

# Create combined table with all three embeddings
let combined = table(pc_coords |> map(|row|
  let i = 0
  let result = {
    PC1: row[0],
    PC2: row[1],
    tSNE1: tsne_default[i][0],
    tSNE2: tsne_default[i][1],
    UMAP1: umap_result[i][0],
    UMAP2: umap_result[i][1],
    CD3D: cd3d_expr[i],
    CD14: cd14_expr[i],
    MS4A1: ms4a1_expr[i]
  }
  i = i + 1
  result
))

# In all three views, T cells (CD3D+), monocytes (CD14+), and B cells (MS4A1+)
# should form distinct groups
# PCA shows them as broad clouds with some overlap
# t-SNE shows them as tight, well-separated clusters
# UMAP shows them as connected but distinct islands

PCA on GTEx: Tissues Separate by Biology

Now we apply PCA to the GTEx bulk RNA-seq data. This is a fundamentally different use case — instead of thousands of cells, we have tens of tissues, each summarized by a single median expression vector.

# Prepare GTEx data for PCA
# Columns are tissues, rows are genes
# We need to transpose: make tissues the observations

let tissue_names = columns(gtex) |> filter(|c| c != "Name" and c != "Description")

println("GTEx tissues: " + str(len(tissue_names)))

# Select the top 5000 most variable genes across tissues
let gene_vars = []
let gene_ids = col_values(gtex, "Name")
let gene_symbols = col_values(gtex, "Description")

tissue_names |> head(1) |> each(|t|
  println("Example tissue column: " + t)
)

# Compute variance across tissues for each gene
let var_per_gene = gene_ids |> map(|gid|
  let row_idx = 0  # would need to find the row for this gene
  let values = tissue_names |> map(|t| col_values(gtex, t)[row_idx])
  { gene: gid, variance: variance(values) }
)

# Select top 5000 most variable genes
let top_genes = var_per_gene |> sort(|a, b| b.variance - a.variance) |> head(5000)
let selected_gene_ids = top_genes |> map(|g| g.gene)

# Build tissue x gene matrix
let tissue_data = tissue_names |> map(|t|
  let values = col_values(gtex, t)
  let result = { tissue: t }
  selected_gene_ids |> each(|gid|
    let idx = 0  # index of this gene
    result[gid] = log2(values[idx] + 1.0)
  )
  result
)

let tissue_table = table(tissue_data)

# Run PCA
let gene_cols = selected_gene_ids
let tissue_expr = tissue_table |> select(gene_cols)
let tissue_normalized = normalize(tissue_expr, "zscore")
let tissue_pca = pca(tissue_normalized, n_components: 10)

# Plot PC1 vs PC2 with tissue labels
let tissue_pc_table = table(tissue_names |> map(|t|
  let i = 0
  { tissue: t, PC1: tissue_pca.components[i][0], PC2: tissue_pca.components[i][1] }
))

scatter(tissue_pc_table, "PC1", "PC2",
  title: "GTEx — PCA of Tissue Expression Profiles",
  x_label: "PC1",
  y_label: "PC2")
  |> save_plot("gtex_tissue_pca.svg")

println("GTEx PCA complete")
println("PC1 likely separates brain tissues from all others")
println("PC2 likely separates testis and other reproductive tissues")

In the GTEx data, PCA reveals beautiful biological structure:

  • Brain tissues cluster tightly together, separated from all other tissues on PC1
  • Testis is an extreme outlier on PC2 (unique transcriptional program)
  • Blood and immune tissues cluster together
  • Muscle tissues (skeletal, cardiac) cluster together
  • Liver stands somewhat alone (unique metabolic program)

This is PCA working as a biological discovery tool. Nobody told the algorithm which tissues are similar — it recovered known biology purely from expression patterns.

PCA for Batch Effect Detection

One of the most critical applications of PCA in genomics is detecting batch effects. If your PC1 separates by sequencing lane rather than by biological condition, you have a problem that no downstream test can fix.

# Simulated batch effect detection example
# Suppose we have expression data with sample metadata

let expr_data = read_tsv("expression_with_batches.tsv")
let metadata = read_tsv("sample_metadata.tsv")

# Run PCA on expression
let expr_cols = columns(expr_data) |> filter(|c| c != "sample_id")
let expr_matrix = expr_data |> select(expr_cols)
let expr_scaled = normalize(expr_matrix, "zscore")
let batch_pca = pca(expr_scaled, n_components: 10)

# Add metadata to PC coordinates
let pc_with_meta = table(metadata |> map(|m|
  let i = 0
  {
    sample: m.sample_id,
    condition: m.condition,      # biological variable of interest
    batch: m.sequencing_batch,   # technical variable
    PC1: batch_pca.components[i][0],
    PC2: batch_pca.components[i][1]
  }
))

# Test: does PC1 correlate with batch or condition?
let pc1_values = col_values(pc_with_meta, "PC1")

# Group PC1 by condition
let cond_groups = pc_with_meta |> group_by("condition")
let cond_a = cond_groups |> filter(|g| g.key == "treatment") |> map(|g| g.PC1)
let cond_b = cond_groups |> filter(|g| g.key == "control") |> map(|g| g.PC1)
let cond_test = ttest(cond_a, cond_b)

# Group PC1 by batch
let batch_groups = pc_with_meta |> group_by("batch")
let batch_1 = batch_groups |> filter(|g| g.key == "batch1") |> map(|g| g.PC1)
let batch_2 = batch_groups |> filter(|g| g.key == "batch2") |> map(|g| g.PC1)
let batch_test = ttest(batch_1, batch_2)

println("PC1 association with condition: p=" + str(round(cond_test.pvalue, 4)))
println("PC1 association with batch: p=" + str(round(batch_test.pvalue, 4)))

# If batch_test.pvalue << cond_test.pvalue, you have a batch effect
# The data must be corrected before differential expression

PCA as Pre-processing: The Standard Pipeline

In single-cell genomics, PCA is not the final step. It is the pre-processing step before clustering, UMAP visualization, and trajectory inference. The standard pipeline is:

  1. Normalize and log-transform
  2. Select highly variable genes
  3. Scale (z-score)
  4. PCA to 30-50 dimensions
  5. Use PCs (not raw genes) for all downstream analysis

This is because: (a) PCA denoises the data by discarding low-variance components, (b) distances in PC space are more meaningful than in gene space, (c) it is computationally efficient — downstream algorithms work on 30-50 dimensions instead of 20,000.

# The standard single-cell pipeline in BioLang

# Step 1-3: Already done above (normalize, log, HVG selection, scaling)

# Step 4: PCA to 30 dimensions
let n_pcs = 30
let final_pca = pca(scaled_data, n_components: n_pcs)
let pc_embedding = final_pca.components |> map(|row| row |> head(n_pcs))

println("PC embedding: " + str(len(pc_embedding)) + " cells x " +
        str(n_pcs) + " dimensions")

# Step 5a: Cluster in PC space (covered in Chapter 14)
let clusters = leiden(pc_embedding, resolution: 1.0)
println("Found " + str(len(unique(clusters))) + " clusters")

# Step 5b: UMAP for visualization, using PC embedding as input
let final_umap = umap(pc_embedding, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.1)

# Combine everything
let cell_data = table(pbmc |> map(|row|
  let i = 0
  {
    barcode: row.barcode,
    cluster: clusters[i],
    UMAP1: final_umap[i][0],
    UMAP2: final_umap[i][1],
    PC1: pc_embedding[i][0],
    PC2: pc_embedding[i][1]
  }
))

# Plot UMAP colored by cluster
scatter(cell_data, "UMAP1", "UMAP2",
  color: "cluster",
  title: "PBMC 3k — UMAP colored by Leiden cluster",
  x_label: "UMAP 1",
  y_label: "UMAP 2")
  |> save_plot("pbmc_umap_clusters.svg")

# Count cells per cluster
let cluster_counts = cell_data |> count_by("cluster")
println("Cells per cluster:")
cluster_counts |> each(|c|
  println("  Cluster " + str(c.cluster) + ": " + str(c.n) + " cells")
)

Annotating PCs with Known Biology

Once we have clusters and PC embeddings, we can ask: which genes define each cluster? And do the PCs correspond to known biological programs?

# For each cluster, find the top marker genes
# A marker gene is one that is highly expressed in one cluster vs all others

let cluster_ids = unique(clusters) |> sort(|a, b| a - b)

cluster_ids |> each(|cid|
  println("Cluster " + str(cid) + " top markers:")

  # Get cell indices in this cluster vs all others
  let in_cluster = []
  let out_cluster = []
  let i = 0
  clusters |> each(|c|
    if c == cid then
      in_cluster = concat(in_cluster, [i])
    else
      out_cluster = concat(out_cluster, [i])
    end
    i = i + 1
  )

  # Test each HVG for differential expression
  let markers = hvgs |> head(500) |> map(|g|
    let vals = col_values(log_normalized, g)
    let in_vals = in_cluster |> map(|idx| vals[idx])
    let out_vals = out_cluster |> map(|idx| vals[idx])
    let test = wilcoxon(in_vals, out_vals)
    {
      gene: g,
      pvalue: test.pvalue,
      mean_in: mean(in_vals),
      mean_out: mean(out_vals),
      log_fc: mean(in_vals) - mean(out_vals)
    }
  )

  # Sort by p-value and show top 5
  let top_markers = markers
    |> filter(|m| m.log_fc > 0)  # upregulated in this cluster
    |> sort(|a, b| a.pvalue - b.pvalue)
    |> head(5)

  top_markers |> each(|m|
    println("  " + m.gene + ": logFC=" + str(round(m.log_fc, 2)) +
            " p=" + str(round(m.pvalue, 6)))
  )
)

# Expected markers in PBMC data:
# T cells:     CD3D, CD3E, IL7R (CD4+), CD8A, CD8B (CD8+)
# Monocytes:   LYZ, S100A8, S100A9, CD14
# B cells:     MS4A1 (CD20), CD79A, CD79B
# NK cells:    NKG7, GNLY, GZMB
# Dendritic:   FCER1A, CST3
# Platelets:   PPBP, PF4

The Eigenvalue Decomposition in Detail

To fully understand what PCA gives you, let us look at the eigenvalue decomposition directly.

# Compute the correlation matrix of the HVGs
let cor_mat = cor_matrix(scaled_data |> select(hvgs |> head(100)))

# The eigenvalues of the correlation matrix equal the variance
# explained by each PC (when data is scaled)
let eigenvals = eigenvalues(cor_mat)

println("Top 10 eigenvalues of the correlation matrix:")
eigenvals |> head(10) |> each(|e|
  println("  " + str(round(e, 4)))
)

# Eigenvalue > 1 means the PC explains more variance than any single gene
# This is the Kaiser criterion: keep PCs with eigenvalue > 1
let kaiser_count = eigenvals |> filter(|e| e > 1.0) |> len()
println("PCs with eigenvalue > 1 (Kaiser criterion): " + str(kaiser_count))

# Warning: the Kaiser criterion is a rough heuristic
# In genomics with thousands of genes, it often suggests too many PCs
# The elbow plot is more reliable for biological data

Dimensionality Reduction on Clinical Data

PCA is not just for expression data. In clinical genomics, you often have a mix of continuous and categorical variables — age, BMI, lab values, mutation status, copy number — and want to see if patients cluster into meaningful groups.

# Clinical data: tumor samples with mixed measurements
let clinical = read_tsv("clinical_data.tsv")

# Select numeric columns for PCA
let numeric_cols = ["age", "tumor_size", "ki67_pct", "mutation_burden",
                    "ctdna_fraction", "wbc_count", "albumin", "ldh"]

let clinical_numeric = clinical |> select(numeric_cols)
let clinical_scaled = normalize(clinical_numeric, "zscore")

# Handle missing values
let clinical_clean = drop_null(clinical_scaled)
println("Samples after dropping nulls: " + str(len(clinical_clean)))

# PCA on clinical variables
let clin_pca = pca(clinical_clean, n_components: 5)

# What clinical features drive PC1?
let clin_loadings = numeric_cols |> map(|col|
  let i = 0
  { feature: col, pc1_loading: clin_pca.loadings[i][0], pc2_loading: clin_pca.loadings[i][1] }
)

println("Clinical feature loadings:")
clin_loadings |> sort(|a, b| abs(b.pc1_loading) - abs(a.pc1_loading)) |> each(|l|
  println("  " + l.feature + ": PC1=" + str(round(l.pc1_loading, 3)) +
          " PC2=" + str(round(l.pc2_loading, 3)))
)

# Visualize clinical PCA
let clin_pc_table = table(clinical |> map(|row|
  let i = 0
  {
    sample: row.sample_id,
    stage: row.stage,
    PC1: clin_pca.components[i][0],
    PC2: clin_pca.components[i][1]
  }
))

scatter(clin_pc_table, "PC1", "PC2",
  color: "stage",
  title: "Clinical Data PCA — colored by tumor stage",
  x_label: "PC1",
  y_label: "PC2")
  |> save_plot("clinical_pca.svg")

Interpretation

What PCA Tells You (and What It Doesn’t)

PCA tells you:

  • How much of the variance is low-dimensional. If 80% of variance is in the first 5 PCs, the data has strong structure.
  • Which genes co-vary. The loadings reveal gene programs — groups of genes that vary together across cells or samples.
  • Whether your data has batch effects. If technical variables correlate with the top PCs, you have a problem.
  • How many distinct groups exist. The number of clear elbows in the scree plot gives a rough estimate.

PCA does NOT tell you:

  • What the groups are. PCA is unsupervised — it does not use labels.
  • Whether nonlinear relationships exist. PCA is strictly linear.
  • Whether small but important subpopulations exist. PCA prioritizes large-variance directions, which may miss rare cell types.

What t-SNE and UMAP Tell You (and What They Don’t)

t-SNE and UMAP tell you:

  • Whether distinct clusters exist. Points that cluster together are genuinely similar in the original space.
  • Local neighborhood structure. Nearby points in the plot are truly nearby in the original space.

They do NOT tell you:

  • Distances between clusters. The spacing is an artifact of the algorithm.
  • Cluster sizes. A large visual cluster may contain fewer cells than a small one.
  • Continuous gradients. Both methods discretize continuous variation into apparent clusters.
  • Statistical significance. Clusters in a UMAP plot are not automatically statistically significant cell types.
# Demonstrate that UMAP distances are not quantitative
# Compute actual distances between cluster centroids in PC space
# vs apparent distances in UMAP space

let centroids_pc = cluster_ids |> map(|cid|
  let mask = clusters |> map(|c| c == cid)
  let pc_vals = pc_embedding |> filter(|row| mask[0])  # simplified
  # In practice: average PC coordinates for cells in this cluster
  { cluster: cid, type: "PC_space" }
)

# Key point: the rank order of inter-cluster distances in PC space
# may not match the rank order in UMAP space
# Two clusters that are far apart in UMAP may be closer in PC space
# than two clusters that appear adjacent in UMAP
println("Warning: UMAP distances are NOT quantitative measures of similarity")
println("Always verify apparent relationships by checking PC space distances")
println("or by computing marker gene overlap between clusters")

Decision

Choosing Your Approach

Given your specific problem, here is the decision tree:

Exploratory analysis of single-cell data: PCA (30 PCs) -> UMAP -> Leiden clustering. This is the standard pipeline. Do not skip PCA.

Quality control of bulk RNA-seq: PCA only. Plot PC1 vs PC2. Color by biological condition AND by all technical variables (batch, lane, extraction date, operator). If any technical variable explains more variance than your biological question, correct the batch effect before proceeding.

Publication figure: Both PCA and UMAP/t-SNE. Show PCA for quantitative claims (variance explained, loadings). Show UMAP/t-SNE for visual clarity. Report the parameters you used.

Discovering gene programs: PCA loadings analysis. The genes with high absolute loadings on a PC form a biological program. Run gene ontology enrichment on these genes to interpret the program.

# Gene program discovery from PC loadings

# Get top 50 genes for PC1 (positive loadings)
let pc1_pos = pc1_sorted
  |> filter(|g| g.loading > 0)
  |> head(50)
  |> map(|g| g.gene)

# Get top 50 genes for PC1 (negative loadings)
let pc1_neg = pc1_sorted
  |> filter(|g| g.loading < 0)
  |> head(50)
  |> map(|g| g.gene)

println("PC1 positive gene program (myeloid):")
println("  " + str(pc1_pos |> head(10)))

println("PC1 negative gene program (lymphoid):")
println("  " + str(pc1_neg |> head(10)))

# You would run these through gene ontology enrichment to confirm:
# pc1_pos should enrich for innate immunity, phagocytosis
# pc1_neg should enrich for adaptive immunity, T cell receptor signaling

Reporting Dimensionality Reduction Results

When reporting dimensionality reduction in a paper or presentation:

  1. State the preprocessing: normalization, gene selection, scaling
  2. State the number of PCs used and how you chose it (elbow, cumulative variance threshold, or formal test)
  3. Show the scree plot in supplementary materials
  4. For UMAP/t-SNE: report all parameters (perplexity, n_neighbors, min_dist, random seed, number of iterations)
  5. Do not make distance claims from UMAP/t-SNE plots
  6. Show PC loadings when claiming biological programs
# Summary statistics for methods section

let var_explained_pct = pca_result.variance |> head(n_pcs) |> map(|v| 100.0 * v / total_var)
let total_captured = sum(var_explained_pct)

println("=== Methods Section Statistics ===")
println("Expression matrix: " + str(len(pbmc)) + " cells x " + str(len(hvgs)) + " HVGs")
println("Normalization: library-size to 10,000, log1p, z-score per gene")
println("PCA: " + str(n_pcs) + " components retained (" +
        str(round(total_captured, 1)) + "% total variance)")
println("UMAP: n_neighbors=15, min_dist=0.1, metric=euclidean")
println("Clustering: Leiden, resolution=1.0")
println("Clusters identified: " + str(len(unique(clusters))))

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Running t-SNE/UMAP on Raw Gene Expression

Never run t-SNE or UMAP directly on the raw gene expression matrix. The noise in 20,000 genes drowns out the signal. Always reduce to 30-50 PCs first. This denoising step is essential.

# DO NOT do this:
# let bad_tsne = tsne(raw_expression_matrix)

# DO this instead:
# let pca_reduced = pca(scaled_data, n_components: 30)
# let good_tsne = tsne(pca_reduced.components)

println("Always run PCA before t-SNE or UMAP")
println("PCA denoises the data by discarding low-variance components")
println("Without this step, nonlinear methods amplify noise")

Pitfall 2: Over-Interpreting t-SNE Cluster Separation

A common mistake is to claim that two cell types are “highly distinct” because they appear well-separated in a t-SNE plot. The visual separation is an artifact of the algorithm’s optimization. Two groups with 10% gene expression overlap can look as separated as two groups with 0% overlap.

Pitfall 3: Ignoring the Elbow Plot

Choosing too few PCs discards biological signal. Choosing too many includes noise dimensions that degrade clustering. Spend time on the scree plot.

# How the number of PCs affects clustering

let pc_counts = [5, 10, 15, 20, 30, 50]
