pc_counts |> each(|n|
  let embedding = final_pca.components |> map(|row| row |> head(n))
  let cl = leiden(embedding, resolution: 1.0)
  let n_clusters = len(unique(cl))
  println("PCs=" + str(n) + " -> " + str(n_clusters) + " clusters")
)

# Too few PCs (5): may merge distinct cell types
# Too many PCs (50): may split cell types by noise
# Sweet spot: where the scree plot elbows (typically 10-30 for single-cell)

Pitfall 4: Not Checking for Confounders in PCA

If PC1 correlates with your variable of interest AND with a technical confounder, you cannot tell which one is driving the pattern. Always test PCs against all known technical variables.

# Check all PCs against technical variables
let technical_vars = ["sequencing_batch", "library_prep_date",
                      "rna_integrity_number", "percent_mito"]

println("Testing PCs against technical variables:")
technical_vars |> each(|var|
  # For continuous variables, use correlation
  # For categorical variables, use ANOVA
  println("  " + var + ": [test result here]")
)

Pitfall 5: Treating UMAP/t-SNE as Deterministic

Both t-SNE and UMAP are stochastic algorithms. Running them twice with different random seeds produces different layouts. If a biological claim depends on the specific layout of a UMAP plot, it is not robust.

# Demonstrate stochasticity
let umap_run1 = umap(pc_input, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.1)
let umap_run2 = umap(pc_input, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.1)

# The overall cluster structure should be the same
# But the rotation, reflection, and exact positions will differ
# If your conclusion changes between runs, it is not robust
println("UMAP is stochastic — always verify that conclusions are")
println("consistent across multiple random seeds")

Pitfall 6: PCA on Non-Centered Data

PCA assumes the data is centered (mean-subtracted). If you skip centering, PC1 will point toward the mean of the data, wasting your most informative component on a trivial direction.

# Centering matters
let uncentered = log_normalized |> select(hvgs)
let centered = normalize(uncentered, "zscore")

let pca_uncentered = pca(uncentered, n_components: 5)
let pca_centered = pca(centered, n_components: 5)

# Compare variance explained
println("Uncentered PCA — PC1 variance: " +
        str(round(100.0 * pca_uncentered.variance[0] / sum(pca_uncentered.variance), 1)) + "%")
println("Centered PCA — PC1 variance: " +
        str(round(100.0 * pca_centered.variance[0] / sum(pca_centered.variance), 1)) + "%")

# The uncentered PC1 will explain a huge amount of variance
# but it is trivially pointing toward the mean expression level
# The centered PC1 captures actual biological variation

Where Next?

Dimensionality reduction opens the door to several downstream analyses:

Chapter 14: Clustering & Classification — Once you have a PCA embedding, you can cluster cells or samples into groups. Leiden clustering on PCA coordinates is the standard approach in single-cell genomics. K-means on PCA is used for tumor subtyping.

Trajectory inference — If your cells represent a developmental process (stem cell differentiation, immune activation), dimensionality reduction reveals continuous trajectories rather than discrete clusters. Methods like diffusion maps and pseudotime ordering build on PCA/UMAP embeddings.

Data integration — When combining datasets from different labs, technologies, or species, PCA-based methods like Harmony, Scanorama, and BBKNN align the PC spaces across datasets to enable joint analysis.

Gene regulatory network inference — The co-variation structure captured by PCA can be used to infer which transcription factors regulate which genes. Genes with similar PC loading profiles are likely co-regulated.

Transfer learning — A PCA model trained on a large reference dataset (like the Human Cell Atlas) can be used to project new datasets into the same coordinate system. This enables automated cell type annotation by finding the nearest reference cell type in PC space.

# Example: project a new dataset into the PBMC reference PCA space
# This is the basis of automated cell type annotation

# Suppose we have a new sample
let new_sample = read_tsv("new_pbmc_sample.tsv")

# Normalize the same way as the reference
let new_normalized = normalize(new_sample |> select(hvgs), "zscore")

# Project into reference PC space using the saved loadings
# new_PCs = new_data %*% reference_loadings
let new_projected = new_normalized |> map(|row|
  let pc_coords = []
  let pc_idx = 0
  while pc_idx < n_pcs do
    let score = hvgs |> map(|g|
      let gene_idx = 0
      row[g] * pca_result.loadings[gene_idx][pc_idx]
    ) |> sum()
    pc_coords = concat(pc_coords, [score])
    pc_idx = pc_idx + 1
  end
  pc_coords
)

# Now new_projected is in the same coordinate system as the reference
# You can classify cells by nearest-neighbor in this space
println("Projected " + str(len(new_projected)) + " new cells into reference PC space")
println("Ready for automated cell type annotation")

Summary

ConceptKey Takeaway
Curse of dimensionalityDistances become meaningless in high-D; reduce first
PCALinear, deterministic, quantitative; use for QC and preprocessing
Scree plotChoose PCs at the elbow; typical range 10-30 for single-cell
PC loadingsReveal gene programs; have biological interpretation
t-SNEGood local structure; distances between clusters are meaningless
UMAPBetter global structure than t-SNE; faster; standard for single-cell
Perplexity/n_neighborsParameters matter; always try multiple values
Pre-processingNormalize, log-transform, select HVGs, scale, THEN reduce
Batch effectsCheck top PCs against technical variables before proceeding
Stochasticityt-SNE and UMAP are non-deterministic; verify robustness

Dimensionality reduction is the bridge between raw high-dimensional measurements and biological understanding. It is not the destination — it is the foundation on which clustering, trajectory analysis, and biological interpretation are built. Master PCA first, understand its linearity limitations, then use UMAP/t-SNE for visualization while respecting their constraints.

Chapter 14: Clustering & Classification

The Problem

You have gene expression profiles from 1,097 breast cancer tumors in The Cancer Genome Atlas (TCGA). Clinicians treat breast cancer differently depending on the molecular subtype — Luminal A, Luminal B, HER2-enriched, Basal-like, and Normal-like. But these subtypes were originally discovered by unsupervised clustering of expression data. The question is: if you cluster the tumors without knowing the subtypes, do the clusters match the known biology?

More broadly: given a matrix of measurements (gene expression, protein levels, methylation, mutation profiles), how do you discover natural groups in the data? How do you decide how many groups exist? And how do you avoid the dangerous trap of finding patterns in noise?

This chapter covers unsupervised clustering — the art of discovering structure that you did not know existed. It also covers the critical discipline of validating that the structure is real.

Why It Matters

Clustering is not just an exploratory exercise. In genomics, clusters become clinical categories that determine treatment:

Breast cancer subtypes. The PAM50 classifier assigns each tumor to one of five molecular subtypes. Luminal A tumors have excellent prognosis and may avoid chemotherapy. Basal-like tumors are aggressive and require intensive treatment. This classification, which saves lives, was discovered by clustering gene expression profiles.

Single-cell atlas construction. Every cell atlas relies on clustering to define cell types. The Human Cell Atlas, which aims to map every cell in the human body, is fundamentally a clustering project. Each cluster becomes a cell type with a name, a marker gene signature, and a place in the developmental hierarchy.

Cancer of unknown primary (CUP). When a metastatic tumor is found but the primary site is unknown, expression-based classifiers assign it to the most likely tissue of origin. These classifiers use clustering — the unknown tumor is assigned to the cluster of known tumors it most resembles.

Drug response prediction. Clustering patients by multi-omic profiles can reveal subgroups with different drug responses. The I-SPY 2 adaptive clinical trial uses expression-based clustering to match patients to experimental therapies.

The Data

TCGA Breast Cancer (BRCA) expression data

URL: https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.htseq_fpkm.tsv.gz

This file contains FPKM-normalized gene expression for all TCGA-BRCA samples. Rows are genes (Ensembl IDs), columns are samples.

TCGA BRCA molecular subtypes

URL: https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.molecular_subtype.tsv

This file contains the PAM50 molecular subtype classification for each sample.

# Download TCGA BRCA expression data
let expr_url = "https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.htseq_fpkm.tsv.gz"
let expr_file = download(expr_url, "tcga_brca_expr.tsv.gz")

let brca_expr = read_tsv(expr_file)
println("Expression matrix:")
println("  Genes: " + str(len(brca_expr)))
println("  Samples: " + str(ncols(brca_expr) - 1))

# Download molecular subtype labels
let subtype_url = "https://gdc-hub.s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/download/TCGA-BRCA.molecular_subtype.tsv"
let subtype_file = download(subtype_url, "tcga_brca_subtypes.tsv")

let subtypes = read_tsv(subtype_file)
println("Subtype annotations: " + str(len(subtypes)) + " samples")

# Check subtype distribution
let subtype_counts = subtypes |> count_by("subtype")
println("Subtype distribution:")
subtype_counts |> each(|s|
  println("  " + s.subtype + ": " + str(s.n))
)

Exploration

Preparing the Data for Clustering

Before clustering, we must prepare the data the same way we did for PCA in Chapter 13: filter low-expression genes, log-transform, select the most variable features, and scale.

# Get sample IDs (column names, excluding the gene ID column)
let sample_ids = columns(brca_expr) |> filter(|c| c != "Ensembl_ID")
println("Total samples: " + str(len(sample_ids)))

# Filter genes: keep only those expressed in at least 10% of samples
# and with mean FPKM > 1
let gene_ids = col_values(brca_expr, "Ensembl_ID")

let gene_stats = gene_ids |> map(|g|
  let row_vals = sample_ids |> map(|s| col_values(brca_expr, s)[0])  # simplified
  let expressed = row_vals |> filter(|v| v > 0) |> len()
  let pct_expressed = 100.0 * expressed / len(sample_ids)
  let mean_expr = mean(row_vals)
  { gene: g, pct_expressed: pct_expressed, mean_expr: mean_expr }
)

let expressed_genes = gene_stats
  |> filter(|g| g.pct_expressed > 10.0 and g.mean_expr > 1.0)
  |> map(|g| g.gene)

println("Genes after expression filter: " + str(len(expressed_genes)))

# Log2-transform (FPKM + 1)
# Then select top 5000 most variable genes
# Then z-score normalize across samples

# Build a samples x genes matrix (transpose the data)
let sample_data = sample_ids |> map(|s|
  let vals = col_values(brca_expr, s)
  let result = { sample: s }
  expressed_genes |> head(5000) |> each(|g|
    result[g] = log2(vals[0] + 1.0)  # simplified indexing
  )
  result
)

let sample_table = table(sample_data)

Selecting Features: The Most Variable Genes

For clustering, feature selection is critical. Too many features add noise. Too few may miss the signal. The standard approach is to select the most variable genes.

# Compute variance for each gene across samples
let gene_list = expressed_genes |> head(5000)
let gene_var = gene_list |> map(|g|
  let values = col_values(sample_table, g)
  { gene: g, variance: variance(values), mean: mean(values) }
)

# Select top 2000 most variable genes
let top_genes = gene_var
  |> sort(|a, b| b.variance - a.variance)
  |> head(2000)
  |> map(|g| g.gene)

println("Selected " + str(len(top_genes)) + " most variable genes for clustering")

# Show the top 10
println("Top 10 most variable genes:")
gene_var |> sort(|a, b| b.variance - a.variance) |> head(10) |> each(|g|
  println("  " + g.gene + ": var=" + str(round(g.variance, 3)) +
          " mean=" + str(round(g.mean, 3)))
)

# Scale the selected genes (z-score across samples)
let cluster_data = sample_table |> select(top_genes)
let scaled_data = normalize(cluster_data, "zscore")

println("Clustering matrix: " + str(len(scaled_data)) + " samples x " +
        str(ncols(scaled_data)) + " genes")

Dimensionality Reduction Before Clustering

As we learned in Chapter 13, clustering in the full gene space is prone to the curse of dimensionality. We first reduce to PCA coordinates.

# PCA for denoising and dimensionality reduction
let pca_result = pca(scaled_data, n_components: 30)

# Scree plot to choose number of PCs
let total_var = sum(pca_result.variance)
let var_pct = pca_result.variance |> map(|v| 100.0 * v / total_var)

println("Variance explained by top PCs:")
let cum = 0.0
var_pct |> head(15) |> each(|v|
  let i = 0
  cum = cum + v
  println("  PC" + str(i + 1) + ": " + str(round(v, 2)) +
          "% (cumulative: " + str(round(cum, 1)) + "%)")
  i = i + 1
)

# Use 15 PCs for clustering (typical for bulk RNA-seq)
let n_pcs = 15
let pc_embedding = pca_result.components |> map(|row| row |> head(n_pcs))

println("PC embedding: " + str(len(pc_embedding)) + " samples x " + str(n_pcs) + " PCs")

The Analysis

K-Means Clustering

K-means is the simplest and most widely used clustering algorithm. It partitions n observations into k clusters by minimizing the within-cluster sum of squared distances to the centroid.

The algorithm:

  1. Choose k random points as initial centroids
  2. Assign each observation to the nearest centroid
  3. Recompute centroids as the mean of assigned observations
  4. Repeat steps 2-3 until convergence
# K-means clustering with k=5 (to match the 5 PAM50 subtypes)
let km5 = kmeans(pc_embedding, k: 5)

# km5 contains:
#   .labels     — cluster assignment for each sample (0-indexed)
#   .centroids  — centroid coordinates

println("K-means (k=5) cluster sizes:")
let km5_counts = km5.labels |> value_counts()
km5_counts |> each(|c|
  println("  Cluster " + str(c.value) + ": " + str(c.count) + " samples")
)

Choosing K: The Elbow Method

But how do you know that k=5 is the right choice? What if the data contains 3 clusters, or 7? The elbow method runs k-means for a range of k values and plots the within-cluster sum of squares (WCSS, also called inertia) against k.

# Elbow method: run k-means for k=2 to k=10
let k_range = [2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10]
let elbow_results = k_range |> map(|k|
  let result = kmeans(pc_embedding, k: k)

  # Compute within-cluster sum of squares (WCSS)
  let wcss = 0.0
  let i = 0
  result.labels |> each(|label|
    let centroid = result.centroids[label]
    let point = pc_embedding[i]
    let dist_sq = point |> map(|p|
      let j = 0
      let d = p - centroid[j]
      j = j + 1
      d * d
    ) |> sum()
    wcss = wcss + dist_sq
    i = i + 1
  )

  println("k=" + str(k) + " WCSS=" + str(round(wcss, 1)))
  { k: k, wcss: wcss }
)

# Plot the elbow
let elbow_table = table(elbow_results)
scatter(elbow_table, "k", "wcss",
  title: "K-Means Elbow Plot",
  x_label: "Number of Clusters (k)",
  y_label: "Within-Cluster Sum of Squares")
  |> save_plot("kmeans_elbow.svg")

# The elbow is where the decrease in WCSS slows down sharply
# For TCGA BRCA, expect an elbow around k=4 or k=5

Choosing K: Silhouette Analysis

The silhouette score measures how similar each observation is to its own cluster compared to the nearest neighboring cluster. It ranges from -1 (wrong cluster) to +1 (well-matched to its cluster).

# Compute silhouette scores for different k values
let silhouette_results = k_range |> map(|k|
  let result = kmeans(pc_embedding, k: k)

  # Compute silhouette for each sample
  let sil_scores = []
  let i = 0
  result.labels |> each(|label|
    let point = pc_embedding[i]

    # a(i) = mean distance to same-cluster points
    let same_dists = []
    let j = 0
    result.labels |> each(|other_label|
      if other_label == label and j != i then
        let d = point |> map(|p|
          let dim = 0
          let diff = p - pc_embedding[j][dim]
          dim = dim + 1
          diff * diff
        ) |> sum() |> sqrt()
        same_dists = concat(same_dists, [d])
      end
      j = j + 1
    )
    let a_i = if len(same_dists) > 0 then mean(same_dists) else 0.0

    # b(i) = min over other clusters of mean distance to that cluster
    let other_clusters = unique(result.labels) |> filter(|c| c != label)
    let b_i = other_clusters |> map(|oc|
      let oc_dists = []
      let jj = 0
      result.labels |> each(|ol|
        if ol == oc then
          let d = point |> map(|p|
            let dim = 0
            let diff = p - pc_embedding[jj][dim]
            dim = dim + 1
            diff * diff
          ) |> sum() |> sqrt()
          oc_dists = concat(oc_dists, [d])
        end
        jj = jj + 1
      )
      mean(oc_dists)
    ) |> min()

    let s_i = if a_i < b_i then (b_i - a_i) / b_i else (b_i - a_i) / a_i
    sil_scores = concat(sil_scores, [s_i])
    i = i + 1
  )

  let mean_sil = mean(sil_scores)
  println("k=" + str(k) + " mean silhouette=" + str(round(mean_sil, 4)))
  { k: k, mean_silhouette: mean_sil }
)

let sil_table = table(silhouette_results)
scatter(sil_table, "k", "mean_silhouette",
  title: "Silhouette Score vs k",
  x_label: "Number of Clusters (k)",
  y_label: "Mean Silhouette Score")
  |> save_plot("silhouette_scores.svg")

# Higher silhouette = better clustering
# If k=4 and k=5 have similar scores, biological knowledge tips the balance

Hierarchical Clustering

Hierarchical clustering builds a tree (dendrogram) of nested clusters. Unlike k-means, it does not require you to specify k in advance. You choose k by cutting the dendrogram at a certain height.

There are two approaches:

  • Agglomerative (bottom-up): Start with each point as its own cluster. Merge the two closest clusters at each step. Repeat until one cluster remains.
  • Divisive (top-down): Start with all points in one cluster. Split recursively.

The key parameter is the linkage criterion — how you measure the distance between two clusters:

  • Single linkage: Minimum distance between any pair of points
  • Complete linkage: Maximum distance between any pair of points
  • Average linkage: Mean distance between all pairs of points
  • Ward’s method: Minimizes the increase in total within-cluster variance
# Compute the distance matrix for hierarchical clustering
let dist_mat = dist_matrix(pc_embedding, "euclidean")

println("Distance matrix: " + str(len(dist_mat)) + " x " + str(len(dist_mat[0])))

# Descriptive statistics of pairwise distances
let all_dists = []
let i = 0
while i < len(dist_mat) do
  let j = i + 1
  while j < len(dist_mat) do
    all_dists = concat(all_dists, [dist_mat[i][j]])
    j = j + 1
  end
  i = i + 1
end

let dist_stats = {
  mean: mean(all_dists),
  median: median(all_dists),
  sd: stdev(all_dists),
  min: min(all_dists),
  max: max(all_dists)
}

println("Pairwise distance statistics:")
println("  Mean: " + str(round(dist_stats.mean, 2)))
println("  Median: " + str(round(dist_stats.median, 2)))
println("  SD: " + str(round(dist_stats.sd, 2)))
println("  Range: [" + str(round(dist_stats.min, 2)) + ", " +
        str(round(dist_stats.max, 2)) + "]")

Heatmaps with Row/Column Clustering

The classic visualization for clustered expression data is a heatmap with dendrograms on both rows (genes) and columns (samples). This simultaneously shows the expression patterns and the cluster structure.

# Create a heatmap of the top variable genes across samples
# Use the k=5 clustering to order samples

let km_labels = km5.labels

# Order samples by cluster
let ordered_indices = [0, 1, 2, 3, 4] |> map(|cluster|
  let indices = []
  let i = 0
  km_labels |> each(|l|
    if l == cluster then
      indices = concat(indices, [i])
    end
    i = i + 1
  )
  indices
) |> flatten()

# Select a subset of top variable genes for the heatmap (top 100)
let heatmap_genes = top_genes |> head(100)
let heatmap_data = scaled_data |> select(heatmap_genes)

# Create the heatmap
heatmap(heatmap_data,
  title: "TCGA BRCA — Top 100 Variable Genes",
  x_label: "Samples (ordered by cluster)",
  y_label: "Genes")
  |> save_plot("brca_heatmap.svg")

println("Heatmap saved — samples ordered by k-means cluster")
println("Look for block-diagonal structure: genes that are high in one")
println("cluster and low in others define the cluster identity")

Leiden Community Detection

Leiden clustering is the standard method for single-cell RNA-seq. Unlike k-means, it does not require specifying the number of clusters. Instead, it builds a nearest-neighbor graph and finds communities of densely connected nodes.

The algorithm:

  1. Build a k-nearest-neighbor (KNN) graph in PC space
  2. Optimize the modularity function to find communities
  3. The resolution parameter controls the granularity
# Leiden clustering at different resolutions
let resolutions = [0.2, 0.5, 0.8, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0]

let leiden_results = resolutions |> map(|res|
  let clusters = leiden(pc_embedding, resolution: res)
  let n_clusters = len(unique(clusters))
  println("Resolution " + str(res) + ": " + str(n_clusters) + " clusters")
  { resolution: res, n_clusters: n_clusters, labels: clusters }
)

# Resolution 0.2: 2-3 large clusters (Luminal vs Basal)
# Resolution 0.5: 4-5 clusters (approaching PAM50)
# Resolution 1.0: 6-8 clusters (may split subtypes further)
# Resolution 2.0: 10+ clusters (over-splitting)

# The resolution controls granularity — there is no single "correct" value
# Choose based on biological interpretability

Evaluating Clusters Against Known Subtypes

The TCGA BRCA dataset has a gold standard: the PAM50 molecular subtype classification. We can evaluate our unsupervised clustering against this reference.

# Match samples between our clustering and the known subtypes
let our_labels = km5.labels

# Create a contingency table: our clusters x PAM50 subtypes
let known_subtypes = col_values(subtypes, "subtype")
let sample_order = col_values(subtypes, "sample")

# Build the contingency table
let our_clusters = unique(our_labels) |> sort(|a, b| a - b)
let pam50_types = unique(known_subtypes) |> filter(|s| s != "NA")

println("Contingency table: Our clusters vs PAM50 subtypes")
println("")

# Header
let header = "Cluster"
pam50_types |> each(|t|
  header = header + "\t" + t
)
println(header)

# Rows
our_clusters |> each(|cl|
  let row = str(cl)
  pam50_types |> each(|t|
    let count = 0
    let i = 0
    our_labels |> each(|l|
      if l == cl and known_subtypes[i] == t then
        count = count + 1
      end
      i = i + 1
    )
    row = row + "\t" + str(count)
  )
  println(row)
)

Adjusted Rand Index

The Rand Index measures agreement between two clusterings. It counts the number of pairs of observations that are either in the same cluster in both clusterings or in different clusters in both. The Adjusted Rand Index (ARI) corrects for chance — an ARI of 0 means agreement no better than random, and 1 means perfect agreement.

# Compute the Adjusted Rand Index between our clustering and PAM50

# First, compute the raw Rand Index
let n = len(our_labels)
let a = 0  # pairs in same cluster in both
let b = 0  # pairs in different clusters in both
let c = 0  # same in ours, different in PAM50
let d = 0  # different in ours, same in PAM50

let i = 0
while i < n do
  let j = i + 1
  while j < n do
    let same_ours = our_labels[i] == our_labels[j]
    let same_pam50 = known_subtypes[i] == known_subtypes[j]

    if same_ours and same_pam50 then
      a = a + 1
    end
    if same_ours and !same_pam50 then
      c = c + 1
    end
    if !same_ours and same_pam50 then
      d = d + 1
    end
    if !same_ours and !same_pam50 then
      b = b + 1
    end

    j = j + 1
  end
  i = i + 1
end

let total_pairs = n * (n - 1) / 2
let rand_index = (a + b) / total_pairs

println("Raw Rand Index: " + str(round(rand_index, 4)))

# Adjusted Rand Index (corrects for chance)
# ARI = (RI - Expected_RI) / (Max_RI - Expected_RI)
# For k-means with k=5 on BRCA data, expect ARI around 0.4-0.7
# depending on preprocessing

# The ARI will not be 1.0 because:
# 1. K-means is a simple method — it finds spherical clusters
# 2. PAM50 subtypes have complex, non-spherical boundaries
# 3. Some subtypes (especially Normal-like) are poorly defined
# 4. Our feature selection may not match the PAM50 gene set

Comparing Clustering Methods

Different clustering methods find different structures. The right method depends on the shape of the clusters in your data.

# Compare k-means, hierarchical, and Leiden clustering

# K-means (already computed)
let km_labels = km5.labels
let km_n = len(unique(km_labels))

# Leiden at resolution chosen to give ~5 clusters
let leiden_labels = leiden(pc_embedding, resolution: 0.5)
let leiden_n = len(unique(leiden_labels))

println("Method comparison:")
println("  K-means (k=5): " + str(km_n) + " clusters")
println("  Leiden (res=0.5): " + str(leiden_n) + " clusters")

# For each method, show the cluster sizes
println("")
println("K-means cluster sizes:")
let km_vc = km_labels |> value_counts()
km_vc |> sort(|a, b| b.count - a.count) |> each(|c|
  println("  Cluster " + str(c.value) + ": " + str(c.count))
)

println("Leiden cluster sizes:")
let lei_vc = leiden_labels |> value_counts()
lei_vc |> sort(|a, b| b.count - a.count) |> each(|c|
  println("  Cluster " + str(c.value) + ": " + str(c.count))
)

# Agreement between methods
# If two methods find the same clusters, the structure is robust
# If they disagree, the data may not have clear clusters

Identifying Cluster-Defining Genes

Once clusters are found, the next question is: what genes define each cluster? These are the marker genes or signature genes.

# For each cluster, find genes with the highest differential expression
# compared to all other clusters

let cluster_markers = our_clusters |> map(|cl|
  println("Finding markers for cluster " + str(cl) + "...")

  let in_cluster = []
  let out_cluster = []
  let idx = 0
  our_labels |> each(|l|
    if l == cl then
      in_cluster = concat(in_cluster, [idx])
    else
      out_cluster = concat(out_cluster, [idx])
    end
    idx = idx + 1
  )

  # Test top genes for differential expression
  let markers = top_genes |> head(500) |> map(|g|
    let all_vals = col_values(scaled_data, g)
    let in_vals = in_cluster |> map(|i| all_vals[i])
    let out_vals = out_cluster |> map(|i| all_vals[i])

    let test_result = ttest(in_vals, out_vals)
    {
      gene: g,
      pvalue: test_result.pvalue,
      mean_in: mean(in_vals),
      mean_out: mean(out_vals),
      log_fc: mean(in_vals) - mean(out_vals)
    }
  )

  # Top 10 upregulated markers
  let top_markers = markers
    |> filter(|m| m.log_fc > 0)
    |> sort(|a, b| a.pvalue - b.pvalue)
    |> head(10)

  println("  Cluster " + str(cl) + " top markers:")
  top_markers |> each(|m|
    println("    " + m.gene + ": logFC=" + str(round(m.log_fc, 2)) +
            " p=" + str(round(m.pvalue, 6)))
  )

  { cluster: cl, markers: top_markers }
)

# Expected markers for breast cancer subtypes:
# Luminal A/B: ESR1 (estrogen receptor), GATA3, FOXA1
# HER2-enriched: ERBB2, GRB7
# Basal-like: KRT5, KRT17, EGFR

Visualization: UMAP Colored by Cluster and Subtype

# Create UMAP embedding for visualization
let umap_result = umap(pc_embedding, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.5)

# Plot colored by our clusters
let umap_cluster_table = table(sample_ids |> map(|s|
  let i = 0
  {
    sample: s,
    cluster: our_labels[i],
    UMAP1: umap_result[i][0],
    UMAP2: umap_result[i][1]
  }
))

scatter(umap_cluster_table, "UMAP1", "UMAP2",
  color: "cluster",
  title: "TCGA BRCA — UMAP colored by K-means cluster",
  x_label: "UMAP 1",
  y_label: "UMAP 2")
  |> save_plot("brca_umap_kmeans.svg")

# Plot colored by PAM50 subtype
let umap_subtype_table = table(sample_ids |> map(|s|
  let i = 0
  {
    sample: s,
    subtype: known_subtypes[i],
    UMAP1: umap_result[i][0],
    UMAP2: umap_result[i][1]
  }
))

scatter(umap_subtype_table, "UMAP1", "UMAP2",
  color: "subtype",
  title: "TCGA BRCA — UMAP colored by PAM50 subtype",
  x_label: "UMAP 1",
  y_label: "UMAP 2")
  |> save_plot("brca_umap_pam50.svg")
