# align with the supervised subtypes?
println("Compare the two UMAP plots:")
println("  If clusters align with subtypes, the biology is strong")
println("  Misaligned regions indicate ambiguous or transitional tumors")

The Circular Analysis Trap

This is the single most dangerous pitfall in clustering:

You cluster the data using a set of genes. Then you test whether those same genes are differentially expressed between the clusters. Of course they are — you selected the clusters specifically to maximize differences on those genes.

This is circular reasoning. The p-values are meaningless because the hypothesis was generated from the same data used to test it.

# CIRCULAR ANALYSIS — DO NOT DO THIS

# Step 1: Cluster using all genes
# let clusters = kmeans(expression_data, k: 5)

# Step 2: Test each gene for differential expression between clusters
# let de_results = genes |> map(|g|
#   let groups = clusters |> map(|c| expression_data[c][g])
#   anova(groups...)
# )

# Step 3: Report "500 genes are significantly different between clusters"
# THIS IS MEANINGLESS — you are testing the same data you clustered on

# CORRECT APPROACH 1: Split-sample validation
# Cluster on half the samples, test on the other half

let n_samples = len(our_labels)
let half = n_samples / 2

# Random split
let indices = [0, 1, 2] |> map(|i| i)  # simplified: all sample indices
let train_idx = indices |> head(half)
let test_idx = indices |> tail(n_samples - half)

# Cluster on training set
let train_data = train_idx |> map(|i| pc_embedding[i])
let train_km = kmeans(train_data, k: 5)

# Assign test samples to nearest training centroid
let test_labels = test_idx |> map(|i|
  let point = pc_embedding[i]
  let min_dist = 999999.0
  let best_cluster = 0
  train_km.centroids |> each(|centroid|
    let c_idx = 0
    let d = point |> map(|p|
      let dim = 0
      let diff = p - centroid[dim]
      dim = dim + 1
      diff * diff
    ) |> sum() |> sqrt()
    if d < min_dist then
      min_dist = d
      best_cluster = c_idx
    end
    c_idx = c_idx + 1
  )
  best_cluster
)

# NOW test genes on the test set — this is valid
println("Split-sample validation:")
println("  Training set: " + str(len(train_idx)) + " samples")
println("  Test set: " + str(len(test_idx)) + " samples")
println("  Test set cluster sizes: " + str(test_labels |> value_counts()))

# CORRECT APPROACH 2: Use independent features
# Cluster on expression, validate with copy number or methylation data
println("")
println("Alternative: validate clusters using independent data modalities")
println("  Cluster on: gene expression")
println("  Validate on: copy number, methylation, survival, clinical features")

Cluster Stability: Bootstrap Assessment

How do you know your clusters are real and not artifacts of noise? One approach is bootstrap resampling: repeatedly resample the data with replacement, cluster each resample, and check if the same clusters appear consistently.

# Bootstrap cluster stability
let n_bootstrap = 100
let n_samples_total = len(pc_embedding)

let stability_matrix = []  # n_samples x n_samples: fraction of times co-clustered

# Initialize co-clustering counts
let co_cluster_count = []
let co_sampled_count = []
let i_init = 0
while i_init < n_samples_total do
  let row = []
  let row2 = []
  let j_init = 0
  while j_init < n_samples_total do
    row = concat(row, [0])
    row2 = concat(row2, [0])
    j_init = j_init + 1
  end
  co_cluster_count = concat(co_cluster_count, [row])
  co_sampled_count = concat(co_sampled_count, [row2])
  i_init = i_init + 1
end

# Run bootstrap iterations
let boot = 0
while boot < n_bootstrap do
  # Sample with replacement
  let boot_indices = []
  let b = 0
  while b < n_samples_total do
    let idx = round(rnorm(1)[0] * n_samples_total / 4 + n_samples_total / 2, 0)
    if idx < 0 then idx = 0 end
    if idx >= n_samples_total then idx = n_samples_total - 1 end
    boot_indices = concat(boot_indices, [idx])
    b = b + 1
  end

  # Cluster the bootstrap sample
  let boot_data = boot_indices |> map(|i| pc_embedding[i])
  let boot_km = kmeans(boot_data, k: 5)

  # Update co-clustering matrix
  # (simplified — full implementation would track all pairs)
  boot = boot + 1
end

println("Bootstrap stability assessment: " + str(n_bootstrap) + " iterations")
println("Stable clusters should have co-clustering frequency > 0.8")
println("Unstable clusters have co-clustering frequency < 0.5")

Cluster Validation with Clinical Variables

The most convincing validation of biological clusters is their association with independent clinical outcomes — especially survival.

# Test whether clusters predict survival differently
# This is external validation — survival was not used for clustering

let clinical_data = read_tsv("tcga_brca_clinical.tsv")

# Merge cluster assignments with survival data
let cluster_survival = table(sample_ids |> map(|s|
  let i = 0
  {
    sample: s,
    cluster: our_labels[i],
    os_months: 0,       # would come from clinical_data
    os_status: 0        # 0=alive, 1=deceased
  }
))

# Kaplan-Meier by cluster
our_clusters |> each(|cl|
  let cl_data = cluster_survival |> filter_by("cluster", cl)
  let times = col_values(cl_data, "os_months")
  let events = col_values(cl_data, "os_status")

  let km = kaplan_meier(times, events)
  println("Cluster " + str(cl) + ": median survival = " +
          str(round(km.median_survival, 1)) + " months")
)

# Log-rank test between clusters (using cox_ph as proxy)
let all_times = col_values(cluster_survival, "os_months")
let all_events = col_values(cluster_survival, "os_status")
let all_clusters = col_values(cluster_survival, "cluster")

let cox_result = cox_ph(all_times, all_events, table({ cluster: all_clusters }))
println("Cox PH test for cluster effect on survival:")
println("  p-value: " + str(round(cox_result.pvalue, 6)))

# If significant, the clusters have prognostic value
# This is the strongest validation that clustering found real biology

Supervised Classification: When Labels Are Available

When you have labels for some samples (like the PAM50 subtypes), you can train a classifier to predict the labels for new samples. This is supervised classification, and it complements unsupervised clustering.

# Simple nearest-centroid classifier for PAM50 subtypes

# Compute centroids for each subtype
let subtype_names = unique(known_subtypes) |> filter(|s| s != "NA")

let centroids = subtype_names |> map(|st|
  let st_indices = []
  let i = 0
  known_subtypes |> each(|s|
    if s == st then
      st_indices = concat(st_indices, [i])
    end
    i = i + 1
  )

  # Average PC coordinates for this subtype
  let centroid = []
  let pc = 0
  while pc < n_pcs do
    let vals = st_indices |> map(|i| pc_embedding[i][pc])
    centroid = concat(centroid, [mean(vals)])
    pc = pc + 1
  end

  { subtype: st, centroid: centroid, n_samples: len(st_indices) }
)

println("Subtype centroids computed:")
centroids |> each(|c|
  println("  " + c.subtype + ": " + str(c.n_samples) + " samples")
)

# Leave-one-out cross-validation
let correct = 0
let total = 0
let i = 0
known_subtypes |> each(|true_label|
  if true_label != "NA" then
    let point = pc_embedding[i]

    # Find nearest centroid (excluding this sample's contribution)
    let min_dist = 999999.0
    let predicted = ""
    centroids |> each(|c|
      let d = point |> map(|p|
        let dim = 0
        let diff = p - c.centroid[dim]
        dim = dim + 1
        diff * diff
      ) |> sum() |> sqrt()

      if d < min_dist then
        min_dist = d
        predicted = c.subtype
      end
    )

    if predicted == true_label then
      correct = correct + 1
    end
    total = total + 1
  end
  i = i + 1
)

let accuracy = 100.0 * correct / total
println("Nearest-centroid classification accuracy: " +
        str(round(accuracy, 1)) + "%")
println("(Leave-one-out cross-validation)")

# For PAM50 subtypes, expect ~70-80% accuracy with this simple method
# Misclassifications are mostly between Luminal A and Luminal B
# which are genuinely difficult to distinguish

Correlation Matrix and Heatmap

The correlation matrix between samples reveals the block structure that clustering exploits.

# Compute sample-sample correlation matrix
let cor_mat = cor_matrix(scaled_data)

# Visualize as a heatmap (ordered by cluster)
heatmap(cor_mat,
  title: "Sample-Sample Correlation Matrix (ordered by cluster)",
  x_label: "Samples",
  y_label: "Samples")
  |> save_plot("brca_correlation_heatmap.svg")

# The block-diagonal structure (high-correlation blocks along the diagonal)
# corresponds to clusters
# Off-diagonal high-correlation regions indicate similarity between clusters

# Summary statistics of within-cluster vs between-cluster correlations
our_clusters |> each(|cl|
  let indices = []
  let i = 0
  our_labels |> each(|l|
    if l == cl then indices = concat(indices, [i]) end
    i = i + 1
  )

  # Within-cluster correlations
  let within_cors = []
  let ii = 0
  while ii < len(indices) do
    let jj = ii + 1
    while jj < len(indices) do
      within_cors = concat(within_cors, [cor_mat[indices[ii]][indices[jj]]])
      jj = jj + 1
    end
    ii = ii + 1
  end

  println("Cluster " + str(cl) + ": mean within-cluster correlation = " +
          str(round(mean(within_cors), 3)) + " (n=" + str(len(indices)) + " samples)")
)

Interpretation

What Clustering Found in TCGA BRCA

When you cluster TCGA breast cancer tumors by gene expression, you typically recover four to five major groups that correspond well to the PAM50 molecular subtypes:

  1. Luminal A: Hormone receptor positive, low proliferation. Driven by ESR1, GATA3, FOXA1. Best prognosis.
  2. Luminal B: Hormone receptor positive, high proliferation. Higher Ki67. More aggressive than Luminal A.
  3. HER2-enriched: ERBB2 amplified. Distinct cluster driven by ERBB2 and nearby genes on chromosome 17q12.
  4. Basal-like: Triple-negative (ER-, PR-, HER2-). Driven by basal keratins (KRT5, KRT17) and EGFR. Worst prognosis.
  5. Normal-like: Resembles normal breast tissue. This subtype is controversial — it may represent contamination with normal tissue rather than a true tumor subtype.

The fact that unsupervised clustering recovers these biologically meaningful and clinically actionable groups is powerful evidence that the molecular subtypes are real, not arbitrary divisions.

When Clustering Finds Batch Effects

Sometimes clustering finds technical artifacts instead of biology. The most common scenario: you cluster samples from multiple institutions or sequencing centers, and the clusters separate by institution rather than by disease.

# Check if clusters correlate with technical variables

let institutions = col_values(subtypes, "institution")  # if available
let sequencing_platform = col_values(subtypes, "platform")  # if available

# Contingency table: cluster vs institution
println("Cluster vs Institution (checking for batch effects):")
our_clusters |> each(|cl|
  let cl_institutions = []
  let i = 0
  our_labels |> each(|l|
    if l == cl then
      cl_institutions = concat(cl_institutions, [institutions[i]])
    end
    i = i + 1
  )
  let inst_counts = cl_institutions |> value_counts()
  println("  Cluster " + str(cl) + ": " + str(inst_counts))
)

# If any cluster is dominated by a single institution,
# the clustering may be driven by batch effects
# Solution: batch correction before clustering (Chapter 12)

Comparing Clusterings Quantitatively

When you run multiple clustering methods, you need a way to compare them quantitatively. Beyond the ARI, the Normalized Mutual Information (NMI) is another popular metric.

# Compare all pairwise clusterings

let methods = [
  { name: "K-means", labels: km_labels },
  { name: "Leiden", labels: leiden_labels }
]

println("Clustering comparison:")
let m1_idx = 0
while m1_idx < len(methods) do
  let m2_idx = m1_idx + 1
  while m2_idx < len(methods) do
    let m1 = methods[m1_idx]
    let m2 = methods[m2_idx]

    # Compute agreement (simplified Rand Index)
    let agree = 0
    let total = 0
    let i = 0
    while i < len(m1.labels) do
      let j = i + 1
      while j < len(m1.labels) do
        let same1 = m1.labels[i] == m1.labels[j]
        let same2 = m2.labels[i] == m2.labels[j]
        if same1 == same2 then agree = agree + 1 end
        total = total + 1
        j = j + 1
      end
      i = i + 1
    end

    let ri = agree / total
    println("  " + m1.name + " vs " + m2.name + ": Rand Index = " +
            str(round(ri, 4)))

    m2_idx = m2_idx + 1
  end
  m1_idx = m1_idx + 1
end

Decision

When to Use Each Clustering Method

MethodBest ForParametersCluster Shape
K-meansLarge datasets, spherical clustersk (number of clusters)Spherical
HierarchicalSmall-medium datasets, dendrograms neededLinkage method, cut heightAny (with right linkage)
LeidenSingle-cell, graph-based dataResolutionArbitrary

The Complete Clustering Pipeline

# The recommended pipeline for tumor subtype discovery:

# 1. Prepare data
# let expr = read_tsv("expression.tsv")
# let scaled = normalize(select_hvgs(expr, n: 2000), "zscore")

# 2. Reduce dimensions
# let pca_result = pca(scaled, n_components: 30)
# let pc_data = pca_result.components |> map(|r| r |> head(15))

# 3. Explore k range
# [2..10] |> each(|k| kmeans(pc_data, k: k) |> evaluate)

# 4. Cluster with chosen k
# let clusters = kmeans(pc_data, k: 5)

# 5. Validate externally
# - Compare to known subtypes (ARI)
# - Test against survival (Kaplan-Meier, Cox PH)
# - Check for batch effects
# - Split-sample or bootstrap stability

# 6. Characterize clusters
# - Find marker genes (differential expression)
# - Gene ontology enrichment on markers
# - Correlate with clinical variables

# 7. Visualize
# - UMAP colored by cluster
# - Heatmap with cluster dendrogram
# - Forest plot of cluster vs survival

println("Complete clustering pipeline defined")
println("Always validate clusters with independent data")
println("Never test significance on the same features used for clustering")

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Choosing K by “Looking at the UMAP”

Do not choose the number of clusters by counting the visual groups in a UMAP or t-SNE plot. These methods can split continuous variation into apparent discrete clusters, and can merge distinct groups that happen to be close in the embedding. Use the elbow method, silhouette scores, or domain knowledge.

Pitfall 2: Assuming Clusters Are Cell Types

In single-cell data, a Leiden cluster is not automatically a cell type. It is a group of cells that are transcriptionally similar. Some clusters may represent cell states (activated vs resting T cells) rather than cell types. Some may represent doublets (two cells captured in one droplet). Some may represent low-quality cells. Biological validation with known marker genes is essential.

Pitfall 3: Ignoring Cluster Size Imbalance

K-means tends to produce clusters of roughly equal size, even when the true groups have very different sizes. If you have 800 Luminal A tumors and 50 Normal-like tumors, k-means may incorrectly split the large group to balance sizes. Leiden clustering handles imbalanced groups better.

# K-means can force equal-sized clusters

# Synthetic example: one large group and one small group
let big_group = rnorm(500) |> map(|x| [x, rnorm(1)[0]])
let small_group = rnorm(20) |> map(|x| [x + 10, rnorm(1)[0] + 10])
let all_points = concat(big_group, small_group)

let km2 = kmeans(all_points, k: 2)
let sizes = km2.labels |> value_counts()
println("True sizes: 500, 20")
println("K-means sizes: " + str(sizes))
# K-means may produce sizes like 260, 260 instead of 500, 20

Pitfall 4: Not Reporting Clustering Parameters

Every clustering result depends on specific parameter choices. Always report:

  • The features used (which genes, how many)
  • The preprocessing (normalization, scaling)
  • The dimensionality reduction (number of PCs)
  • The clustering method and parameters
  • The number of clusters and how it was chosen
  • The validation approach

Pitfall 5: Overclaiming Novel Subtypes

It is easy to increase k and find “novel subtypes.” But more clusters is not more discovery. Before claiming a new subtype, verify:

  1. It is stable across bootstrap resamples
  2. It has distinct marker genes
  3. It has a distinct clinical phenotype
  4. It is reproducible in an independent cohort
# Testing a "novel" cluster for independence

# Suppose cluster 6 is a potential novel subtype
# Test whether it is genuinely different from its nearest neighbor cluster

# let novel = samples_in_cluster_6
# let nearest = samples_in_nearest_cluster

# 1. Differential expression between novel and nearest cluster
# let de_genes = test_all_genes(novel, nearest)
# let sig_genes = de_genes |> filter(|g| g.adj_pvalue < 0.05 and abs(g.log_fc) > 1)

# 2. If fewer than 50 genes are significant, the "novel" subtype
#    may just be a variant of the nearest cluster

# 3. Validate in an independent cohort
# let external_data = read_tsv("independent_cohort.tsv")
# let external_clusters = classify_by_centroids(external_data, centroids)
# Does cluster 6 appear in the external data?

println("Novel subtype claims require:")
println("  1. Distinct marker gene signature")
println("  2. Clinical or phenotypic distinction")
println("  3. Reproducibility in independent data")
println("  4. Bootstrap stability > 0.8")

Where Next?

Chapter 15: Meta-Analysis — When multiple studies have clustered the same disease, meta-analysis combines the evidence. Are the subtypes consistent across cohorts? Which clusters replicate and which are dataset-specific?

Multi-omic clustering — Instead of clustering on gene expression alone, integrate copy number, methylation, mutation, and protein data. Methods like iCluster and MOFA perform joint dimensionality reduction across data types before clustering. The TCGA Pan-Cancer Atlas used multi-omic clustering to define molecular subtypes across 33 cancer types.

Spatial transcriptomics — When you have spatial coordinates for each cell (from technologies like Visium, MERFISH, or Slide-seq), clustering must consider both expression similarity and spatial proximity. Spatially-aware clustering reveals tissue architecture.

Trajectory analysis — When cells form continuous developmental trajectories rather than discrete clusters, methods like pseudotime ordering (Monocle, Slingshot) trace paths through the PCA/UMAP space. The choice between clustering and trajectory analysis depends on the biology: differentiation is a trajectory, cell type identity is a cluster.

# Summary of clustering methods for genomics

println("=== Clustering Method Decision Guide ===")
println("")
println("Bulk RNA-seq tumor subtyping:")
println("  -> K-means or hierarchical on PCA, validate with survival")
println("")
println("Single-cell cell type discovery:")
println("  -> Leiden on PCA-derived KNN graph, validate with markers")
println("")
println("Multi-omic integration:")
println("  -> Joint dimensionality reduction (MOFA/iCluster), then cluster")
println("")
println("Time-series or developmental data:")
println("  -> Consider trajectory analysis instead of clustering")
println("")
println("Always validate. Always report parameters. Never trust a single run.")

Summary

ConceptKey Takeaway
K-meansSimple, fast, requires k; finds spherical clusters
HierarchicalNo k needed; produces dendrogram; slow for large data
LeidenGraph-based; handles non-spherical clusters; standard for single-cell
Elbow methodPlot WCSS vs k; look for the bend
SilhouetteMeasures cluster cohesion vs separation; range -1 to +1
ARICompares two clusterings; corrected for chance; 0=random, 1=perfect
Circular analysisNever test significance on features used for clustering
Split-sampleCluster on half, validate on half
Bootstrap stabilityResample and re-cluster; stable clusters appear consistently
Clinical validationThe ultimate test: do clusters predict outcomes?

Chapter 15: Meta-Analysis

The Problem

Three genome-wide association studies have examined the same variant for its association with type 2 diabetes. Study A (n=5,000) reports an odds ratio of 1.35 with p=0.02. Study B (n=3,200) reports OR=1.18 with p=0.15. Study C (n=8,500) reports OR=1.28 with p=0.005. Is this variant truly associated with the disease?

Study A says yes. Study B says no. Study C says yes emphatically. A naive reading would conclude “two out of three studies agree.” But that ignores sample size, effect size magnitude, confidence intervals, and the possibility that the studies are measuring slightly different things.

Meta-analysis is the formal statistical framework for combining results across studies. It produces a single pooled estimate with proper uncertainty quantification, tests for consistency across studies, and can detect when studies disagree for systematic reasons.

Why It Matters

Individual genomics studies are almost always underpowered. A typical GWAS has 80% power to detect an odds ratio of 1.3 at genome-wide significance (p < 5e-8) only when the sample size exceeds 10,000 cases and 10,000 controls. Most studies are smaller. This means:

  • True associations are missed (false negatives)
  • Detected associations have inflated effect sizes (winner’s curse)
  • Results vary dramatically between studies by chance

Meta-analysis solves these problems by combining evidence:

The GWAS Catalog at EBI (https://www.ebi.ac.uk/gwas/) contains over 500,000 variant-trait associations from thousands of studies. Many of the most important GWAS discoveries came from meta-analyses — the GIANT consortium for height (>5 million individuals), the PGC for psychiatric disorders, the DIAGRAM consortium for diabetes.

The GTEx consortium performs meta-analysis across tissues to identify expression quantitative trait loci (eQTLs) — genetic variants that affect gene expression. Their multi-tissue meta-analysis (METASOFT) identifies eQTLs that are shared across tissues vs tissue-specific.

Drug target validation increasingly relies on Mendelian randomization, which is itself a form of meta-analysis — combining effect sizes from multiple genetic instruments to estimate causal effects.

The Data

GWAS Catalog full download

URL: https://www.ebi.ac.uk/gwas/api/search/downloads/full

This file contains all published GWAS associations with effect sizes, p-values, sample sizes, and study information.

# Download the GWAS Catalog
let gwas_url = "https://www.ebi.ac.uk/gwas/api/search/downloads/full"
let gwas_file = download(gwas_url, "gwas_catalog_full.tsv")

let gwas = read_tsv(gwas_file)
println("GWAS Catalog entries: " + str(len(gwas)))
println("Columns: " + str(columns(gwas) |> len()))

# Examine the structure
println("Key columns:")
columns(gwas) |> head(20) |> each(|c| println("  " + c))

For the meta-analysis examples, we will focus on specific genes and traits with multiple independent studies reporting effect sizes.

# Find a well-studied variant-trait pair with multiple studies
# Look for SNPs associated with type 2 diabetes

let t2d_studies = gwas |> filter_by("DISEASE/TRAIT", "Type 2 diabetes")
println("Type 2 diabetes GWAS entries: " + str(len(t2d_studies)))

# Find SNPs reported by multiple studies
let snp_counts = t2d_studies |> count_by("SNPS")
let multi_study_snps = snp_counts |> filter(|s| s.n >= 3) |> arrange("n")

println("SNPs reported in 3+ T2D studies: " + str(len(multi_study_snps)))
multi_study_snps |> tail(10) |> each(|s|
  println("  " + s.SNPS + ": " + str(s.n) + " studies")
)

Exploration

Understanding Effect Sizes and Their Uncertainties

Before combining studies, we need to understand what each study reports. The key quantities are:

  • Effect size: odds ratio (OR) for case-control studies, beta coefficient for quantitative traits
  • Standard error (SE): the uncertainty in the effect size
  • Confidence interval: typically 95% CI = effect +/- 1.96 * SE
  • P-value: significance of the effect

All meta-analysis methods work with effect sizes and their standard errors. If a study only reports a p-value (common in older GWAS), the effect size cannot be directly meta-analyzed — you must use p-value combination methods instead.

# Extract effect sizes for a specific SNP across studies
# Example: rs7903146 in TCF7L2 — the strongest T2D risk variant

let target_snp = "rs7903146"
let snp_studies = t2d_studies |> filter(|row|
  row.SNPS == target_snp or row.SNPS == target_snp
)

println("Studies for " + target_snp + ":")
println("  Number of studies: " + str(len(snp_studies)))

# Extract effect sizes and sample sizes
let study_data = snp_studies |> mutate(|row| {
  study: row.STUDY,
  first_author: row.FIRST_AUTHOR,
  year: row.DATE,
  or_beta: row.OR_BETA,
  ci_95: row.CI_95,
  pvalue: row.P_VALUE,
  sample_size: row.SAMPLE_SIZE,
  journal: row.JOURNAL
})

println("")
study_data |> each(|s|
  println("  " + str(s.first_author) + " (" + str(s.year) + "):")
  println("    OR/Beta: " + str(s.or_beta))
  println("    95% CI: " + str(s.ci_95))
  println("    p-value: " + str(s.pvalue))
  println("    Sample size: " + str(s.sample_size))
  println("")
)

Converting Between Effect Size Scales

Different studies may report effect sizes on different scales. Binary outcomes use odds ratios (OR), while quantitative traits use beta coefficients. For meta-analysis, all studies must be on the same scale.

# For odds ratios, meta-analysis is done on the log(OR) scale
# because log(OR) is approximately normally distributed

# Example: three studies report ORs for a diabetes variant
let studies = [
  { name: "Study A", or: 1.35, se_log_or: 0.12, n: 5000 },
  { name: "Study B", or: 1.18, se_log_or: 0.15, n: 3200 },
  { name: "Study C", or: 1.28, se_log_or: 0.08, n: 8500 }
]

# Convert to log(OR) scale
let log_or_studies = studies |> map(|s| {
  name: s.name,
  effect: log(s.or),              # log(OR)
  se: s.se_log_or,                # SE of log(OR)
  variance: s.se_log_or * s.se_log_or,
  weight: 1.0 / (s.se_log_or * s.se_log_or),  # inverse-variance weight
  n: s.n,
  or: s.or
})

println("Studies on log(OR) scale:")
log_or_studies |> each(|s|
  let ci_lo = exp(s.effect - 1.96 * s.se)
  let ci_hi = exp(s.effect + 1.96 * s.se)
  println("  " + s.name + ": log(OR)=" + str(round(s.effect, 4)) +
          " SE=" + str(round(s.se, 4)) +
          " OR=" + str(round(s.or, 3)) +
          " 95%CI=[" + str(round(ci_lo, 3)) + "," + str(round(ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
)

Visualizing Study-Level Results

Before computing the pooled estimate, visualize each study’s result with its confidence interval. This is the individual-study view that will become part of the forest plot.

# Plot individual study results with CIs
let forest_data = log_or_studies |> map(|s| {
  study: s.name,
  estimate: s.or,
  ci_lower: exp(s.effect - 1.96 * s.se),
  ci_upper: exp(s.effect + 1.96 * s.se),
  weight: s.weight
})

let forest_table = table(forest_data)

# Visual inspection: do the CIs overlap?
println("Individual study results (OR scale):")
forest_data |> each(|s|
  let ci_width = s.ci_upper - s.ci_lower
  println("  " + s.study + ": OR=" + str(round(s.estimate, 3)) +
          " [" + str(round(s.ci_lower, 3)) + ", " +
          str(round(s.ci_upper, 3)) + "]" +
          " CI width=" + str(round(ci_width, 3)))
)

# If CIs overlap substantially, studies agree
# If CIs barely overlap or don't overlap, there may be heterogeneity

The Analysis

Fixed-Effects Meta-Analysis (Inverse-Variance Weighting)

The simplest meta-analysis model is the fixed-effects model. It assumes that all studies are estimating the same true effect, and differences between studies are due to sampling variation alone.

The pooled estimate is the weighted average of the study effects, where each study is weighted by the inverse of its variance (i.e., more precise studies get more weight):

Pooled effect = sum(w_i * theta_i) / sum(w_i) SE of pooled = 1 / sqrt(sum(w_i))

where w_i = 1 / SE_i^2 and theta_i is the effect estimate from study i.

# Fixed-effects meta-analysis
let total_weight = log_or_studies |> map(|s| s.weight) |> sum()
let weighted_sum = log_or_studies |> map(|s| s.weight * s.effect) |> sum()

let pooled_effect = weighted_sum / total_weight
let pooled_se = 1.0 / sqrt(total_weight)
let pooled_or = exp(pooled_effect)
let pooled_ci_lo = exp(pooled_effect - 1.96 * pooled_se)
let pooled_ci_hi = exp(pooled_effect + 1.96 * pooled_se)
let pooled_z = pooled_effect / pooled_se
let pooled_p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(pooled_z)))

println("=== Fixed-Effects Meta-Analysis ===")
println("Pooled log(OR): " + str(round(pooled_effect, 4)))
println("Pooled SE: " + str(round(pooled_se, 4)))
println("Pooled OR: " + str(round(pooled_or, 3)))
println("95% CI: [" + str(round(pooled_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(pooled_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("Z-score: " + str(round(pooled_z, 2)))
println("P-value: " + str(round(pooled_p, 8)))
println("")

# Weight contribution from each study
log_or_studies |> each(|s|
  let pct_weight = 100.0 * s.weight / total_weight
  println("  " + s.name + " contributes " + str(round(pct_weight, 1)) +
          "% of the weight (n=" + str(s.n) + ")")
)

Testing for Heterogeneity: The Q Statistic

The fixed-effects model assumes all studies estimate the same true effect. The Q statistic tests this assumption. Q is the weighted sum of squared deviations from the pooled effect:

Q = sum(w_i * (theta_i - pooled)^2)

Under the null hypothesis of no heterogeneity, Q follows a chi-squared distribution with k-1 degrees of freedom (where k is the number of studies).

# Cochran's Q test for heterogeneity
let q_stat = log_or_studies |> map(|s|
  let dev = s.effect - pooled_effect
  s.weight * dev * dev
) |> sum()

let k = len(log_or_studies)
let df_q = k - 1

# P-value from chi-squared distribution
# Using the approximation: for df=2, critical value at 0.05 is 5.991
let q_pvalue = 1.0 - pnorm(sqrt(2.0 * q_stat) - sqrt(2.0 * df_q - 1.0))

println("=== Heterogeneity Test ===")
println("Cochran's Q: " + str(round(q_stat, 3)))
println("Degrees of freedom: " + str(df_q))
println("P-value: " + str(round(q_pvalue, 4)))

if q_pvalue < 0.10 then
  println("-> Significant heterogeneity detected (p < 0.10)")
  println("   Consider random-effects model")
else
  println("-> No significant heterogeneity (p >= 0.10)")
  println("   Fixed-effects model is appropriate")
end

The I-Squared Statistic

While Q tests whether heterogeneity exists, I-squared quantifies how much of the variability in effect sizes is due to true heterogeneity (as opposed to sampling variation).

I^2 = max(0, (Q - df) / Q) * 100%

Interpretation:

  • I^2 = 0%: No heterogeneity; all variability is sampling error
  • I^2 = 25%: Low heterogeneity
  • I^2 = 50%: Moderate heterogeneity
  • I^2 = 75%: High heterogeneity
# I-squared statistic
let i_squared = max(0, (q_stat - df_q) / q_stat) * 100.0

println("I-squared: " + str(round(i_squared, 1)) + "%")

if i_squared < 25 then
  println("-> Low heterogeneity: studies are consistent")
  println("   Fixed-effects meta-analysis is appropriate")
end
if i_squared >= 25 and i_squared < 50 then
  println("-> Low-to-moderate heterogeneity")
  println("   Fixed-effects is likely still appropriate")
end
if i_squared >= 50 and i_squared < 75 then
  println("-> Moderate heterogeneity")
  println("   Random-effects model recommended")
  println("   Investigate sources of heterogeneity")
end
if i_squared >= 75 then
  println("-> High heterogeneity")
  println("   Random-effects model essential")
  println("   Pool estimate may not be meaningful without understanding why studies differ")
end

Random-Effects Meta-Analysis (DerSimonian-Laird)

When heterogeneity is present, the fixed-effects model is too aggressive — it gives confidence intervals that are too narrow because it ignores the between-study variation. The random-effects model adds a between-study variance component (tau-squared) to account for this.

The DerSimonian-Laird method estimates tau-squared from the data:

tau^2 = max(0, (Q - df) / (sum(w_i) - sum(w_i^2)/sum(w_i)))

Then the random-effects weights become:

w_i* = 1 / (SE_i^2 + tau^2)

# DerSimonian-Laird random-effects meta-analysis

# Estimate tau-squared (between-study variance)
let sum_w = log_or_studies |> map(|s| s.weight) |> sum()
let sum_w2 = log_or_studies |> map(|s| s.weight * s.weight) |> sum()
let c = sum_w - sum_w2 / sum_w

let tau_sq = max(0, (q_stat - df_q) / c)

println("=== Random-Effects Meta-Analysis (DerSimonian-Laird) ===")
println("Tau-squared (between-study variance): " + str(round(tau_sq, 6)))
println("Tau (between-study SD): " + str(round(sqrt(tau_sq), 4)))

# Compute random-effects weights
let re_studies = log_or_studies |> map(|s| {
  name: s.name,
  effect: s.effect,
  se: s.se,
  re_variance: s.variance + tau_sq,
  re_weight: 1.0 / (s.variance + tau_sq),
  n: s.n
})

let re_total_weight = re_studies |> map(|s| s.re_weight) |> sum()
let re_weighted_sum = re_studies |> map(|s| s.re_weight * s.effect) |> sum()

let re_pooled_effect = re_weighted_sum / re_total_weight
let re_pooled_se = 1.0 / sqrt(re_total_weight)
let re_pooled_or = exp(re_pooled_effect)
let re_pooled_ci_lo = exp(re_pooled_effect - 1.96 * re_pooled_se)
let re_pooled_ci_hi = exp(re_pooled_effect + 1.96 * re_pooled_se)
let re_z = re_pooled_effect / re_pooled_se
let re_p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(re_z)))

println("")
println("Pooled log(OR): " + str(round(re_pooled_effect, 4)))
println("Pooled SE: " + str(round(re_pooled_se, 4)))
println("Pooled OR: " + str(round(re_pooled_or, 3)))
println("95% CI: [" + str(round(re_pooled_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(re_pooled_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("P-value: " + str(round(re_p, 8)))

# Compare fixed vs random effects
println("")
println("=== Comparison ===")
println("Fixed-effects OR: " + str(round(pooled_or, 3)) +
        " [" + str(round(pooled_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(pooled_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("Random-effects OR: " + str(round(re_pooled_or, 3)) +
        " [" + str(round(re_pooled_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(re_pooled_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("")
println("Note: Random-effects CI is always wider than or equal to fixed-effects CI")
println("The random-effects estimate is more conservative — it accounts for")
println("the possibility that the true effect varies between studies")

# Weight redistribution
println("")
println("Weight redistribution:")
re_studies |> each(|s|
  let fe_pct = 100.0 * (1.0 / s.se / s.se) / total_weight
  let re_pct = 100.0 * s.re_weight / re_total_weight
  println("  " + s.name + ": FE=" + str(round(fe_pct, 1)) +
          "% -> RE=" + str(round(re_pct, 1)) + "%")
)
println("Random-effects gives relatively more weight to small studies")

Forest Plot: The Essential Visualization

The forest plot is the standard visualization for meta-analysis. It shows each study’s estimate with its confidence interval, the pooled estimate, and the relative weight of each study.

# Create the forest plot data

let fp_data = log_or_studies |> map(|s| {
  study: s.name + " (n=" + str(s.n) + ")",
  estimate: s.or,
  ci_lower: exp(s.effect - 1.96 * s.se),
  ci_upper: exp(s.effect + 1.96 * s.se),
  weight: round(100.0 * s.weight / total_weight, 1)
})

# Add the pooled estimates
let fp_pooled_fe = {
  study: "Fixed Effects (pooled)",
  estimate: pooled_or,
  ci_lower: pooled_ci_lo,
  ci_upper: pooled_ci_hi,
  weight: 100.0
}

let fp_pooled_re = {
  study: "Random Effects (pooled)",
  estimate: re_pooled_or,
  ci_lower: re_pooled_ci_lo,
  ci_upper: re_pooled_ci_hi,
  weight: 100.0
}

let all_fp_data = concat(fp_data, [fp_pooled_fe, fp_pooled_re])
let fp_table = table(all_fp_data)

forest_plot(fp_table, "study", "estimate", "ci_lower", "ci_upper",
  title: "Meta-Analysis: " + target_snp + " and Type 2 Diabetes",
  x_label: "Odds Ratio",
  null_value: 1.0)
  |> save_plot("meta_analysis_forest.svg")

println("Forest plot saved to meta_analysis_forest.svg")
println("The diamond represents the pooled estimate")
println("The vertical line at OR=1.0 represents the null (no effect)")

Combining P-Values: Fisher’s Method

When effect sizes are not available (common with older studies), you can still combine p-values. Fisher’s method uses the fact that under the null hypothesis, -2 * sum(log(p_i)) follows a chi-squared distribution with 2k degrees of freedom.

# Fisher's method for combining p-values

let pvalues = [0.02, 0.15, 0.005]

let fisher_stat = pvalues |> map(|p| -2.0 * log(p)) |> sum()
let fisher_df = 2 * len(pvalues)

# Chi-squared p-value approximation
# For large df, chi-squared is approximately normal
let fisher_z = (fisher_stat - fisher_df) / sqrt(2.0 * fisher_df)
let fisher_p = 1.0 - pnorm(fisher_z)

println("=== Fisher's Method for Combining P-Values ===")
println("Individual p-values: " + str(pvalues))
println("Test statistic: " + str(round(fisher_stat, 3)))
println("Degrees of freedom: " + str(fisher_df))
println("Combined p-value: " + str(round(fisher_p, 8)))
println("")

# Note: Fisher's method is very sensitive to a single strong result
# One study with p=0.001 can make the combined p-value significant
# even if other studies show nothing

Stouffer’s Method: Weighted P-Value Combination

Stouffer’s method converts p-values to z-scores, weights them (typically by the square root of the sample size), and combines:

Z_pooled = sum(w_i * Z_i) / sqrt(sum(w_i^2))

# Stouffer's weighted Z method

let study_ps = [
  { pvalue: 0.02,  n: 5000 },
  { pvalue: 0.15,  n: 3200 },
  { pvalue: 0.005, n: 8500 }
]

# Convert p-values to z-scores (one-sided)
let z_scores = study_ps |> map(|s| {
  z: qnorm(1.0 - s.pvalue / 2.0),   # two-sided p to z
  weight: sqrt(s.n),                  # weight by sqrt(n)
  n: s.n
})

let weighted_z_sum = z_scores |> map(|s| s.weight * s.z) |> sum()
let weight_ss = z_scores |> map(|s| s.weight * s.weight) |> sum()
let pooled_z_stouffer = weighted_z_sum / sqrt(weight_ss)
let stouffer_p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(pooled_z_stouffer)))

println("=== Stouffer's Weighted Z Method ===")
z_scores |> each(|s|
  println("  Z=" + str(round(s.z, 3)) + " weight=" +
          str(round(s.weight, 1)) + " (n=" + str(s.n) + ")")
)
println("Pooled Z: " + str(round(pooled_z_stouffer, 3)))
println("Combined p-value: " + str(round(stouffer_p, 8)))
println("")
println("Stouffer's method is preferred over Fisher's when sample sizes vary")
println("because it gives more weight to larger studies")

Real Example: Combining GWAS Hits from the GWAS Catalog

Now let us apply meta-analysis to real data from the GWAS Catalog. We will find all studies reporting on a specific variant and combine their results.

# Find all reports for a well-studied variant
# rs7903146 in TCF7L2 — strongest known T2D risk variant

let target_reports = gwas |> filter(|row|
  row.SNPS == "rs7903146"
)

println("All GWAS reports for rs7903146:")
println("  Total entries: " + str(len(target_reports)))

# Filter to those with reported OR/beta values
let with_effects = target_reports |> filter(|row|
  row.OR_BETA != "" and row.OR_BETA != "NR"
)

println("  With effect sizes: " + str(len(with_effects)))

# Extract and summarize
let effect_summary = with_effects |> mutate(|row| {
  trait: row.DISEASE_TRAIT,
  first_author: row.FIRST_AUTHOR,
  year: row.DATE,
  or_beta: row.OR_BETA,
  pvalue: row.P_VALUE,
  sample_size: row.SAMPLE_SIZE
})

# Group by trait to see which traits this variant is associated with
let trait_counts = effect_summary |> count_by("trait")
println("")
println("Traits associated with rs7903146:")
trait_counts |> sort(|a, b| b.n - a.n) |> head(10) |> each(|t|
  println("  " + t.trait + ": " + str(t.n) + " studies")
)

# Focus on T2D studies for meta-analysis
let t2d_effects = effect_summary |> filter(|row|
  row.trait == "Type 2 diabetes"
)

println("")
println("T2D-specific studies for meta-analysis: " + str(len(t2d_effects)))

Publication Bias: The Funnel Plot

Publication bias is the tendency for studies with statistically significant results to be published, while studies with null results remain in file drawers. This biases meta-analyses toward larger effect sizes.

The funnel plot visualizes this: it plots each study’s effect size against its precision (1/SE or sample size). In the absence of publication bias, the plot should be symmetric around the pooled estimate. Asymmetry — especially missing small-study null results in the lower left — suggests publication bias.

# Create a funnel plot

let funnel_data = log_or_studies |> map(|s| {
  effect: s.effect,          # log(OR) on x-axis
  precision: 1.0 / s.se,    # 1/SE on y-axis (more precise studies at top)
  study: s.name
})

let funnel_table = table(funnel_data)

scatter(funnel_table, "effect", "precision",
  title: "Funnel Plot: " + target_snp,
  x_label: "Effect Size (log OR)",
  y_label: "Precision (1/SE)")
  |> save_plot("funnel_plot.svg")

# Add reference lines
println("Funnel plot interpretation:")
println("  Vertical line at pooled effect = " + str(round(pooled_effect, 4)))
println("  Symmetric distribution: no publication bias")
println("  Missing lower-left: small negative studies unpublished")
println("  Missing lower-right: small positive studies unpublished")

# Egger's test for funnel plot asymmetry
# Regress the standard normal deviate (effect/SE) against precision (1/SE)
# Intercept significantly different from zero suggests bias

let egger_x = log_or_studies |> map(|s| 1.0 / s.se)
let egger_y = log_or_studies |> map(|s| s.effect / s.se)

let egger_result = lm(egger_y, egger_x)
println("")
println("Egger's test for publication bias:")
println("  Intercept: " + str(round(egger_result.intercept, 4)))
println("  P-value for intercept: " + str(round(egger_result.pvalue, 4)))

if egger_result.pvalue < 0.10 then
  println("  -> Evidence of funnel plot asymmetry (potential publication bias)")
else
  println("  -> No significant asymmetry detected")
end

Subgroup Analysis: Exploring Heterogeneity

When heterogeneity is detected, subgroup analysis investigates whether study-level characteristics explain the differences. Common subgroup variables in genomics:

  • Ancestry: European vs East Asian vs African
  • Study design: Case-control vs cohort
  • Genotyping platform: Array vs sequencing
  • Phenotype definition: Strict vs broad diagnostic criteria
# Subgroup analysis by ancestry
# Suppose we have ancestry information for each study

let studies_with_ancestry = [
  { name: "Study A", effect: log(1.35), se: 0.12, ancestry: "European", n: 5000 },
  { name: "Study B", effect: log(1.18), se: 0.15, ancestry: "East Asian", n: 3200 },
  { name: "Study C", effect: log(1.28), se: 0.08, ancestry: "European", n: 8500 },
  { name: "Study D", effect: log(1.42), se: 0.10, ancestry: "European", n: 6200 },
  { name: "Study E", effect: log(1.22), se: 0.18, ancestry: "East Asian", n: 2100 },
  { name: "Study F", effect: log(1.15), se: 0.20, ancestry: "African", n: 1800 }
]

let ancestries = unique(studies_with_ancestry |> map(|s| s.ancestry))

println("=== Subgroup Analysis by Ancestry ===")
ancestries |> each(|anc|
  let subgroup = studies_with_ancestry |> filter(|s| s.ancestry == anc)

  # Fixed-effects meta-analysis within subgroup
  let sg_weights = subgroup |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se))
  let sg_total_w = sg_weights |> sum()
  let sg_pooled = subgroup |> map(|s|
    (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) * s.effect
  ) |> sum() / sg_total_w
  let sg_se = 1.0 / sqrt(sg_total_w)
  let sg_or = exp(sg_pooled)
  let sg_ci_lo = exp(sg_pooled - 1.96 * sg_se)
  let sg_ci_hi = exp(sg_pooled + 1.96 * sg_se)

  println(anc + " (" + str(len(subgroup)) + " studies):")
  println("  Pooled OR: " + str(round(sg_or, 3)) +
          " [" + str(round(sg_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
          str(round(sg_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
  println("")
)

# Test for interaction between subgroups
# Is the effect significantly different between ancestries?
# This uses a chi-squared test comparing subgroup estimates
println("If subgroup effects differ significantly, ancestry may modify the")
println("variant's effect on disease risk (gene-environment interaction)")

The Winner’s Curse

The winner’s curse is a form of selection bias that inflates effect sizes in the discovery cohort. When you test thousands of variants and select the “winners” (most significant), you are preferentially selecting those where random noise happened to increase the apparent effect.

# Demonstrate the winner's curse with simulation

# True effect: OR = 1.2 (log OR = 0.182)
let true_log_or = log(1.2)
let true_or = 1.2

# Simulate 100 studies, each with different SE
let simulated = rnorm(100) |> map(|noise|
  let se = 0.05 + abs(rnorm(1)[0]) * 0.15  # random SE between 0.05 and ~0.20
  let observed_effect = true_log_or + noise * se  # noisy observation
  let z = observed_effect / se
  let p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(z)))
  {
    effect: observed_effect,
    se: se,
    or: exp(observed_effect),
    pvalue: p,
    significant: p < 0.05
  }
)

# Compare significant vs non-significant studies
let sig_studies = simulated |> filter(|s| s.significant)
let nonsig_studies = simulated |> filter(|s| !s.significant)

let sig_mean_or = mean(sig_studies |> map(|s| s.or))
let nonsig_mean_or = mean(nonsig_studies |> map(|s| s.or))
let all_mean_or = mean(simulated |> map(|s| s.or))

println("=== Winner's Curse Demonstration ===")
println("True OR: " + str(true_or))
println("Mean OR across all 100 studies: " + str(round(all_mean_or, 3)))
println("Mean OR in significant studies only: " + str(round(sig_mean_or, 3)))
println("Mean OR in non-significant studies: " + str(round(nonsig_mean_or, 3)))
println("")
println("The significant studies overestimate the true effect by " +
        str(round(100 * (sig_mean_or - true_or) / true_or, 1)) + "%")
println("This is the winner's curse")
println("")
println("Implication: the first GWAS to discover an association will report")
println("an inflated effect size. Replication studies typically show smaller effects.")
println("Meta-analysis of all studies (including replications) corrects this bias.")

Multi-Trait Meta-Analysis

In genomics, we often want to combine evidence across related traits. For example, a variant may affect both fasting glucose and HbA1c — combining evidence across these correlated traits increases power.

# Multi-trait analysis: combine evidence across related phenotypes

let multi_trait = [
  { trait: "Type 2 diabetes", effect: log(1.30), se: 0.06, n: 15000 },
  { trait: "Fasting glucose", effect: 0.08, se: 0.02, n: 45000 },   # beta in mmol/L
  { trait: "HbA1c", effect: 0.05, se: 0.015, n: 50000 },            # beta in %
  { trait: "Fasting insulin", effect: 0.03, se: 0.025, n: 25000 }   # beta in pmol/L
]

# These are on different scales — cannot directly meta-analyze with IVW
# Convert to Z-scores for combination

let trait_zscores = multi_trait |> map(|t| {
  trait: t.trait,
  z: t.effect / t.se,
  pvalue: 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(t.effect / t.se))),
  n: t.n
})

println("=== Multi-Trait Evidence ===")
trait_zscores |> each(|t|
  println("  " + t.trait + ": Z=" + str(round(t.z, 2)) +
          " p=" + str(round(t.pvalue, 8)))
)

# Combine using Stouffer's method (weighted by sqrt(n))
let multi_w = trait_zscores |> map(|t| sqrt(t.n))
let multi_wz = trait_zscores |> map(|t| sqrt(t.n) * t.z) |> sum()
let multi_wss = multi_w |> map(|w| w * w) |> sum()
let multi_z_pooled = multi_wz / sqrt(multi_wss)
let multi_p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(multi_z_pooled)))

println("")
println("Multi-trait combined Z: " + str(round(multi_z_pooled, 2)))
println("Multi-trait combined p: " + str(round(multi_p, 12)))
println("")
println("Caution: traits are correlated, so this overstates significance")
println("Proper multi-trait methods (MTAG, ASSET) account for trait correlations")

Sensitivity Analysis

Robust meta-analyses include sensitivity analyses to check whether the results depend on any single study:

# Leave-one-out sensitivity analysis
println("=== Leave-One-Out Analysis ===")
println("Removing each study in turn to check robustness:")
println("")

log_or_studies |> each(|excluded|
  let remaining = log_or_studies |> filter(|s| s.name != excluded.name)

  let loo_w = remaining |> map(|s| s.weight) |> sum()
  let loo_sum = remaining |> map(|s| s.weight * s.effect) |> sum()
  let loo_effect = loo_sum / loo_w
  let loo_se = 1.0 / sqrt(loo_w)
  let loo_or = exp(loo_effect)
  let loo_ci_lo = exp(loo_effect - 1.96 * loo_se)
  let loo_ci_hi = exp(loo_effect + 1.96 * loo_se)

  println("  Excluding " + excluded.name + ": OR=" + str(round(loo_or, 3)) +
          " [" + str(round(loo_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
          str(round(loo_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
)

println("")
println("If the pooled OR changes substantially when any single study is removed,")
println("the meta-analysis is sensitive to that study — investigate why")

Cumulative Meta-Analysis

A cumulative meta-analysis adds studies one at a time (chronologically) to show how the pooled estimate evolved as evidence accumulated.

# Cumulative meta-analysis (chronological order)

let chronological = [
  { name: "Study A (2008)", effect: log(1.42), se: 0.15, year: 2008 },
  { name: "Study B (2010)", effect: log(1.35), se: 0.12, year: 2010 },
  { name: "Study C (2012)", effect: log(1.28), se: 0.10, year: 2012 },
  { name: "Study D (2015)", effect: log(1.25), se: 0.08, year: 2015 },
  { name: "Study E (2018)", effect: log(1.22), se: 0.06, year: 2018 },
  { name: "Study F (2022)", effect: log(1.20), se: 0.05, year: 2022 }
]

println("=== Cumulative Meta-Analysis ===")
println("Pooled estimate as each study is added chronologically:")
println("")

let included = []
chronological |> each(|study|
  included = concat(included, [study])

  let cum_w = included |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) |> sum()
  let cum_sum = included |> map(|s| (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) * s.effect) |> sum()
  let cum_effect = cum_sum / cum_w
  let cum_se = 1.0 / sqrt(cum_w)
  let cum_or = exp(cum_effect)
  let cum_ci_lo = exp(cum_effect - 1.96 * cum_se)
  let cum_ci_hi = exp(cum_effect + 1.96 * cum_se)
  let total_n = included |> len()

  println("  After " + study.name + " (" + str(total_n) + " studies):")
  println("    OR=" + str(round(cum_or, 3)) + " [" +
          str(round(cum_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " + str(round(cum_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
)

println("")
println("Notice how:")
println("  1. The CI narrows as more studies are added (more precision)")
println("  2. The OR decreases slightly over time (winner's curse correction)")
println("  3. The first study had the largest OR (discovery bias)")

Building a Complete Meta-Analysis Pipeline

# Complete meta-analysis pipeline function

# Step 1: Collect study-level data
let all_studies = [
  { name: "Grant 2006", effect: log(1.45), se: 0.10, n: 3500, ancestry: "European" },
  { name: "Scott 2007", effect: log(1.37), se: 0.08, n: 5500, ancestry: "European" },
  { name: "Sladek 2007", effect: log(1.40), se: 0.12, n: 2800, ancestry: "European" },
  { name: "DIAGRAM 2008", effect: log(1.36), se: 0.05, n: 12000, ancestry: "European" },
  { name: "Takeuchi 2009", effect: log(1.25), se: 0.14, n: 3000, ancestry: "East Asian" },
  { name: "Unoki 2008", effect: log(1.20), se: 0.11, n: 4200, ancestry: "East Asian" },
  { name: "Waters 2010", effect: log(1.30), se: 0.18, n: 1500, ancestry: "African" },
  { name: "DIAGRAM 2012", effect: log(1.32), se: 0.04, n: 22000, ancestry: "European" },
  { name: "Mahajan 2018", effect: log(1.29), se: 0.03, n: 74000, ancestry: "Multi-ethnic" }
]

# Step 2: Fixed-effects analysis
let fe_w = all_studies |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se))
let fe_total = fe_w |> sum()
let fe_pooled = all_studies |> map(|s| (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) * s.effect) |> sum() / fe_total
let fe_se = 1.0 / sqrt(fe_total)

# Step 3: Heterogeneity
let q = all_studies |> map(|s|
  let w = 1.0 / (s.se * s.se)
  w * pow(s.effect - fe_pooled, 2)
) |> sum()
let df = len(all_studies) - 1
let i2 = max(0, (q - df) / q) * 100.0

# Step 4: Random-effects if needed
let c_val = fe_total - (fe_w |> map(|w| w * w) |> sum()) / fe_total
let tau2 = max(0, (q - df) / c_val)
let re_w = all_studies |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se + tau2))
let re_total = re_w |> sum()
let re_pooled = all_studies |> map(|s|
  (1.0 / (s.se * s.se + tau2)) * s.effect
) |> sum() / re_total
let re_se_val = 1.0 / sqrt(re_total)

# Step 5: Report
println("=== Complete Meta-Analysis Report ===")
println("Variant: rs7903146 (TCF7L2)")
println("Trait: Type 2 Diabetes")
println("Studies: " + str(len(all_studies)))
println("Total sample: " + str(all_studies |> map(|s| s.n) |> sum()))
println("")
println("Fixed-effects: OR=" + str(round(exp(fe_pooled), 3)) +
        " [" + str(round(exp(fe_pooled - 1.96 * fe_se), 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(exp(fe_pooled + 1.96 * fe_se), 3)) + "]")
println("Random-effects: OR=" + str(round(exp(re_pooled), 3)) +
        " [" + str(round(exp(re_pooled - 1.96 * re_se_val), 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(exp(re_pooled + 1.96 * re_se_val), 3)) + "]")
println("Heterogeneity: Q=" + str(round(q, 2)) + " I2=" +
        str(round(i2, 1)) + "%")
println("Tau2=" + str(round(tau2, 6)))

# Step 6: Forest plot
let final_fp = all_studies |> map(|s| {
  study: s.name + " (" + s.ancestry + ")",
  estimate: exp(s.effect),
  ci_lower: exp(s.effect - 1.96 * s.se),
  ci_upper: exp(s.effect + 1.96 * s.se),
  weight: round(100.0 * (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) / fe_total, 1)
})

let final_fp = concat(final_fp, [{
  study: "RE Pooled",
  estimate: exp(re_pooled),
  ci_lower: exp(re_pooled - 1.96 * re_se_val),
  ci_upper: exp(re_pooled + 1.96 * re_se_val),
  weight: 100.0
}])

forest_plot(table(final_fp), "study", "estimate", "ci_lower", "ci_upper",
  title: "rs7903146 and Type 2 Diabetes: Meta-Analysis",
  x_label: "Odds Ratio",
  null_value: 1.0)
  |> save_plot("tcf7l2_forest_plot.svg")

Interpretation

Reading a Forest Plot

The forest plot tells you everything you need to know at a glance:

  1. Individual study squares: Size proportional to weight. Position shows the point estimate. Horizontal lines are 95% CIs.
  2. The diamond: The pooled estimate. Width represents the pooled CI.
  3. The null line: Vertical line at OR=1 (or beta=0). If a CI crosses this line, that study’s result is not significant.
  4. Overlap: If all CIs overlap substantially, there is little heterogeneity. If some CIs are completely separated from others, the studies genuinely disagree.

When Studies Disagree

Heterogeneity is not always bad. It can reveal important biology:

  • Ancestry-specific effects: A variant may have OR=1.4 in Europeans but OR=1.1 in East Asians due to different linkage disequilibrium patterns or gene-environment interactions.
  • Phenotype heterogeneity: “Type 2 diabetes” diagnosed by fasting glucose vs oral glucose tolerance test may capture different patient populations.
  • Winner’s curse: Early studies with smaller samples report larger effects. This is expected and should decrease over time.
# Investigating heterogeneity sources

# Stratify by ancestry
let european = all_studies |> filter(|s| s.ancestry == "European")
let east_asian = all_studies |> filter(|s| s.ancestry == "East Asian")

let eur_w = european |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se))
let eur_total = eur_w |> sum()
let eur_pooled = european |> map(|s| (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) * s.effect) |> sum() / eur_total

let ea_w = east_asian |> map(|s| 1.0 / (s.se * s.se))
let ea_total = ea_w |> sum()
let ea_pooled = east_asian |> map(|s| (1.0 / (s.se * s.se)) * s.effect) |> sum() / ea_total

println("Ancestry-stratified results:")
println("  European: OR=" + str(round(exp(eur_pooled), 3)))
println("  East Asian: OR=" + str(round(exp(ea_pooled), 3)))

# Test for difference between subgroups
let diff = eur_pooled - ea_pooled
let diff_se = sqrt(1.0 / eur_total + 1.0 / ea_total)
let diff_z = diff / diff_se
let diff_p = 2.0 * (1.0 - pnorm(abs(diff_z)))

println("  Difference test: Z=" + str(round(diff_z, 2)) +
        " p=" + str(round(diff_p, 4)))

if diff_p < 0.05 then
  println("  -> Effect size differs significantly between ancestries")
else
  println("  -> No significant difference between ancestries")
end

Decision

Fixed-Effects vs Random-Effects: Which to Use?

CriterionFixed-EffectsRandom-Effects
AssumptionAll studies estimate the same true effectTrue effect varies between studies
When to useStudies are homogeneous (I^2 < 25%)Studies are heterogeneous (I^2 > 50%)
CI widthNarrowerWider
Weight distributionDominated by large studiesMore balanced across studies
Interpretation“The” common effectThe average effect across studies

In practice: report both. If they agree, it does not matter which you emphasize. If they disagree, explain why (heterogeneity) and use the random-effects estimate as the primary result.

The Meta-Analysis Checklist

Before publishing a meta-analysis, verify:

println("=== Meta-Analysis Quality Checklist ===")
println("")
println("1. Search strategy")
println("   [ ] Systematic search of multiple databases")
println("   [ ] Predefined inclusion/exclusion criteria")
println("   [ ] PRISMA flow diagram")
println("")
println("2. Data extraction")
println("   [ ] Effect sizes on a consistent scale")
println("   [ ] Standard errors (not just p-values)")
println("   [ ] Sample sizes for each study")
println("")
println("3. Analysis")
println("   [ ] Fixed-effects AND random-effects results")
println("   [ ] Heterogeneity assessment (Q, I-squared)")
println("   [ ] Forest plot")
println("   [ ] Publication bias assessment (funnel plot, Egger's test)")
println("")
println("4. Sensitivity")
println("   [ ] Leave-one-out analysis")
println("   [ ] Subgroup analysis (ancestry, phenotype, study design)")
println("   [ ] Cumulative meta-analysis (chronological)")
println("")
println("5. Reporting")
println("   [ ] PRISMA guidelines followed")
println("   [ ] Effect sizes with CIs, not just p-values")
println("   [ ] Heterogeneity statistics reported")
println("   [ ] Limitations discussed (publication bias, heterogeneity)")

Pitfalls

Pitfall 1: Combining Incompatible Effect Sizes

You cannot meta-analyze odds ratios from one study with beta coefficients from another without proper conversion. You cannot combine standardized effect sizes (Cohen’s d) with unstandardized effect sizes. Always verify that all studies are on the same scale.

# Common scale conversions

# Odds ratio to log odds ratio (for meta-analysis)
let or_value = 1.35
let log_or = log(or_value)
println("OR " + str(or_value) + " -> log(OR) = " + str(round(log_or, 4)))

# Cohen's d to log odds ratio (approximate)
let d = 0.5
let log_or_from_d = d * 3.14159 / sqrt(3)
println("Cohen's d " + str(d) + " -> log(OR) ≈ " + str(round(log_or_from_d, 4)))

# Correlation r to Cohen's d (approximate)
let r = 0.3
let d_from_r = 2 * r / sqrt(1 - r * r)
println("r " + str(r) + " -> Cohen's d ≈ " + str(round(d_from_r, 4)))

Pitfall 2: Ignoring Study Overlap

If two meta-analyzed studies share participants (e.g., both used UK Biobank), treating them as independent inflates the effective sample size and produces falsely precise pooled estimates. Always check for sample overlap.

Pitfall 3: The File Drawer Problem

You can only meta-analyze published studies. If negative results remain unpublished, your meta-analysis overestimates the true effect. The funnel plot and Egger’s test help detect this, but they cannot fix it.

Pitfall 4: Confusing Statistical Significance with Clinical Significance

A meta-analysis of 100,000 participants may produce a p-value of 1e-30 for an odds ratio of 1.05. Statistically significant — but an OR of 1.05 means a 5% increase in risk. Is that clinically meaningful?

# Distinguish statistical from clinical significance

let examples = [
  { or: 1.05, p: 1e-30, n: 100000, interpretation: "Tiny effect, huge study" },
  { or: 1.50, p: 0.04,  n: 200,    interpretation: "Moderate effect, small study" },
  { or: 2.00, p: 1e-15, n: 5000,   interpretation: "Large effect, well-powered" }
]

println("Statistical vs clinical significance:")
examples |> each(|e|
  println("  OR=" + str(e.or) + " p=" + str(e.p) + " n=" + str(e.n))
  println("    " + e.interpretation)
  println("")
)

Pitfall 5: Cherry-Picking Studies

Including or excluding studies based on their results (rather than predefined criteria) is a form of p-hacking. The inclusion criteria must be defined before looking at the data. This is why systematic reviews require a registered protocol.

Where Next?

Mendelian randomization — A causal inference method that uses genetic variants as “instruments” to estimate causal effects. It is a specialized form of meta-analysis where you combine multiple genetic instruments, each with an exposure effect and an outcome effect, to estimate the causal relationship between exposure and outcome.

Network meta-analysis — When multiple treatments have been compared in different studies (Drug A vs placebo, Drug B vs placebo, Drug A vs Drug B), network meta-analysis combines both direct and indirect comparisons to rank all treatments simultaneously.

Mega-analysis vs meta-analysis — Instead of combining summary statistics from published studies, mega-analysis combines individual-level data from all studies. This is more powerful (you can adjust for individual-level covariates) but requires data sharing agreements. The PGC and UK Biobank increasingly enable mega-analysis.

Multi-ancestry meta-analysis — Methods like MANTRA and MR-MEGA account for ancestry-specific linkage disequilibrium patterns when combining GWAS across ancestries. These are critical for identifying causal variants (which are shared across ancestries) vs tag variants (which are ancestry-specific).

# Summary of meta-analysis methods

println("=== Method Selection Guide ===")
println("")
println("Have effect sizes + SEs?")
println("  -> Inverse-variance weighted meta-analysis")
println("  -> Fixed-effects if I2 < 25%, random-effects otherwise")
println("")
println("Have only p-values?")
println("  -> Fisher's method or Stouffer's weighted Z")
println("")
println("Combining across traits?")
println("  -> Stouffer's Z (simple) or MTAG (accounts for correlations)")
println("")
println("Combining across ancestries?")
println("  -> Random-effects (treats ancestry as source of heterogeneity)")
println("  -> MANTRA or MR-MEGA (models LD differences)")
println("")
println("Suspecting publication bias?")
println("  -> Funnel plot + Egger's test")
println("  -> Trim-and-fill method to estimate corrected effect")

Prediction Intervals in Random-Effects Models

The confidence interval of a random-effects meta-analysis tells you the uncertainty in the average effect. But the prediction interval tells you the range in which a future study’s true effect is likely to fall. The prediction interval is always wider than the confidence interval because it includes both the uncertainty in the mean AND the between-study variance.

# Prediction interval for a random-effects meta-analysis
# PI = pooled +/- t(df=k-2) * sqrt(tau2 + SE_pooled^2)

let t_crit = qnorm(0.975)  # approximate for df > 5
let pi_se = sqrt(tau2 + re_se_val * re_se_val)
let pi_lo = exp(re_pooled - t_crit * pi_se)
let pi_hi = exp(re_pooled + t_crit * pi_se)

println("=== Prediction Interval ===")
println("95% CI for the average effect: [" +
        str(round(re_pooled_ci_lo, 3)) + ", " +
        str(round(re_pooled_ci_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("95% PI for a new study: [" +
        str(round(pi_lo, 3)) + ", " + str(round(pi_hi, 3)) + "]")
println("")
println("The CI tells you: where is the average effect?")
println("The PI tells you: what would a new study likely find?")
println("")
println("If the PI includes 1.0 (for OR), a new study might find no effect")
println("even if the pooled estimate is significant")

Trim-and-Fill Method for Publication Bias Adjustment

When the funnel plot is asymmetric (suggesting publication bias), the trim-and-fill method estimates how many studies are missing and imputes their results to produce a bias-corrected pooled estimate.

# Trim-and-fill concept
# 1. Estimate the number of missing studies from funnel asymmetry
# 2. "Fill" the funnel with imputed mirror-image studies
# 3. Recompute the pooled estimate

# The adjusted estimate is always closer to the null (OR = 1.0)
# than the original estimate

println("Trim-and-fill adjusts for publication bias by:")
println("  1. Identifying the most extreme studies on the asymmetric side")
println("  2. Removing ('trimming') them to estimate the true center")
println("  3. Replacing them and adding their mirror images ('filling')")
println("  4. Recomputing the pooled estimate with all studies (real + imputed)")
println("")
println("If the original and adjusted estimates are similar,")
println("publication bias is unlikely to change the conclusion")
println("If they differ substantially, the result should be interpreted cautiously")

Handling Correlated Studies

In practice, GWAS meta-analyses often include studies with partially overlapping samples. This violates the independence assumption and produces anti-conservative (too-small) p-values.

# Detecting sample overlap

# Common scenario: Study A uses UK Biobank discovery (n=200,000)
# Study B uses UK Biobank + additional samples (n=250,000)
# These overlap by 200,000 individuals

# Solutions:
# 1. Remove the overlapping study (lose information)
# 2. Use the subset without overlap from Study B
# 3. Use methods designed for correlated studies (e.g., MTAG)

println("Sample overlap detection checklist:")
println("  1. Check if studies used the same biobank or cohort")
println("  2. Check author affiliations and acknowledgments")
println("  3. Check supplementary tables for cohort-level details")
println("  4. When in doubt, contact the study authors")
println("")
println("Approximate correction: reduce effective sample size by")
println("the estimated overlap fraction")

# Example: correcting for 50% overlap between two studies
let n1 = 10000
let n2 = 8000
let overlap = 4000  # shared individuals

# Effective combined sample size (not n1 + n2)
let effective_n = n1 + n2 - overlap
println("Naive combined N: " + str(n1 + n2))
println("Effective N (corrected): " + str(effective_n))

Practical Workflow: GWAS Meta-Analysis Pipeline

# End-to-end GWAS meta-analysis workflow

# Step 1: Harmonize summary statistics
println("Step 1: Harmonize effect alleles across studies")
println("  - Ensure all studies report effects on the same allele")
println("  - Flip sign of beta (or invert OR) when effect allele differs")
println("  - Align to a common reference (e.g., GRCh38)")
println("")

# Step 2: Quality control per study
println("Step 2: Per-study QC")
println("  - Remove variants with MAF < 0.01")
println("  - Remove variants with imputation quality < 0.3")
println("  - Check lambda (genomic inflation factor) per study")
println("  - Flag outlier studies with lambda > 1.1")
println("")

# Step 3: Meta-analyze
println("Step 3: Meta-analysis")
println("  - Inverse-variance weighted fixed-effects")
println("  - Also compute random-effects for comparison")
println("  - Calculate heterogeneity (I-squared) per variant")
println("")

# Step 4: Post-meta-analysis QC
println("Step 4: Post-meta QC")
println("  - QQ plot of meta-analysis p-values")
println("  - Check genomic inflation of meta-analysis lambda")
println("  - Filter variants present in < 75% of studies")
println("  - Flag variants with I-squared > 75%")
println("")

# Step 5: Identify hits
println("Step 5: Identify genome-wide significant hits")
println("  - Threshold: p < 5e-8")
println("  - Define independent loci (LD clumping)")
println("  - Identify lead SNPs per locus")
println("")

# Step 6: Replication and follow-up
println("Step 6: Replication")
println("  - Test top hits in independent replication cohorts")
println("  - Compare discovery effect sizes to replication")
println("  - Expect regression to the mean (winner's curse)")

Effect Size Inflation and Correction

The winner’s curse can be formally corrected using conditional likelihood methods or bootstrap approaches:

# Simple winner's curse correction
# The conditional MLE adjusts the effect size for selection bias

# If a variant was selected because |z| > z_threshold:
let z_threshold = qnorm(1.0 - 5e-8 / 2)  # genome-wide significance
println("Genome-wide significance z-threshold: " + str(round(z_threshold, 2)))

# Observed z-score for a hit
let observed_z = 6.5
let observed_se = 0.05
let observed_beta = observed_z * observed_se

# Expected true effect (conditional MLE approximation)
# The true beta is approximately: observed_beta * (1 - threshold^2 / z^2)
let correction_factor = 1.0 - (z_threshold * z_threshold) / (observed_z * observed_z)
let corrected_beta = observed_beta * max(correction_factor, 0.0)

println("Observed beta: " + str(round(observed_beta, 4)))
println("Correction factor: " + str(round(correction_factor, 4)))
println("Corrected beta: " + str(round(corrected_beta, 4)))
println("Shrinkage: " + str(round(100 * (1 - correction_factor), 1)) + "%")
println("")
println("Variants barely passing the threshold are most inflated")
println("Variants far exceeding the threshold are least affected")

Summary

ConceptKey Takeaway
Fixed-effectsAssumes one true effect; appropriate when I^2 < 25%
Random-effectsAllows varying true effects; always has wider CI
I-squaredQuantifies heterogeneity: 0%=none, 50%=moderate, 75%=high
Q statisticTests null hypothesis of no heterogeneity
Forest plotEssential visualization: estimates, CIs, weights, pooled result
Funnel plotDetects publication bias: asymmetry = concern
Winner’s curseDiscovery studies overestimate true effects
Fisher’s methodCombines p-values; sensitive to single strong result
Stouffer’s methodWeighted p-value combination; better for unequal sample sizes
Leave-one-outSensitivity check: is the result driven by one study?
Subgroup analysisExplores sources of heterogeneity
Prediction intervalWhere a new study’s effect would fall
Publication biasThe biggest threat to meta-analysis validity

Appendix A: Statistical Decision Flowchart

This appendix provides a practical decision tree for choosing the right statistical test. Start with your question and data type, then follow the branches to the appropriate BioLang function.

Step 1: What Is Your Question?

Before choosing a test, classify your question:

  • Comparing groups: Is there a difference between two or more groups?
  • Association: Are two variables related?
  • Prediction: Can one variable predict another?
  • Structure: Are there natural groups in the data?
  • Survival: Does time-to-event differ between groups?
  • Combining evidence: What is the pooled result across studies?

Step 2: Comparing Two Groups

Are the observations paired or independent?

Paired means each observation in group A has a matched observation in group B (e.g., before/after treatment on the same patient, matched tumor/normal from the same individual).

Independent means the observations in the two groups come from different subjects with no inherent pairing.

Paired observations

If your data is continuous and approximately normal:

# Paired t-test
# Example: expression before vs after treatment, same patients
let before = [5.2, 4.8, 6.1, 5.5, 4.9, 5.8, 6.3]
let after = [4.1, 3.9, 5.2, 4.6, 3.8, 4.7, 5.1]
let result = ttest_paired(before, after)
println("Paired t-test: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

If your data is continuous but not normal, or ordinal:

# Wilcoxon signed-rank test (paired version)
let result = wilcoxon(before, after)
println("Wilcoxon signed-rank: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

Independent observations, continuous outcome

If data is approximately normal (check with QQ plot, Shapiro-Wilk):

# Two-sample t-test (Welch's, unequal variance)
let group_a = [12.3, 14.5, 11.8, 13.2, 15.1]
let group_b = [9.8, 10.2, 8.5, 11.0, 9.3]
let result = ttest(group_a, group_b)
println("Two-sample t-test: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

If data is not normal, or has outliers, or is ordinal:

# Wilcoxon rank-sum test (Mann-Whitney U)
let result = wilcoxon(group_a, group_b)
println("Wilcoxon rank-sum: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

Independent observations, categorical outcome

If you have a 2x2 table (two groups, two outcomes):

# If expected cell counts >= 5: chi-squared test
let observed = [45, 155, 12, 188]  # flattened 2x2
let result = chi_square(observed)
println("Chi-squared: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

# If any expected cell count < 5: Fisher's exact test
let table = [[45, 155], [12, 188]]
let result = fisher_exact(table)
println("Fisher's exact: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

One-sample test (comparing to a known value)

# One-sample t-test: is the mean different from a hypothesized value?
let measurements = [5.2, 4.8, 5.5, 5.1, 4.9, 5.3]
let result = ttest_one(measurements, mu: 5.0)
println("One-sample t-test: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

Step 3: Comparing Three or More Groups

Are the observations independent?

If independent and continuous, approximately normal:

# One-way ANOVA
let group1 = [12.1, 13.4, 11.8, 14.2]
let group2 = [9.5, 10.1, 8.8, 11.3]
let group3 = [15.2, 16.0, 14.5, 15.8]
let result = anova(group1, group2, group3)
println("ANOVA F-test: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))
# If significant, follow up with pairwise t-tests + p_adjust

If independent and not normal or ordinal:

# Kruskal-Wallis test (use ks_test as non-parametric alternative)
# For pairwise comparisons: Wilcoxon with p_adjust
let p1 = wilcoxon(group1, group2).pvalue
let p2 = wilcoxon(group1, group3).pvalue
let p3 = wilcoxon(group2, group3).pvalue
let adjusted = p_adjust([p1, p2, p3], "BH")
println("Adjusted p-values: " + str(adjusted))

If categorical outcome (contingency table larger than 2x2):

# Chi-squared test of independence
let observed = [30, 20, 50, 45, 35, 20]  # flattened contingency table
let result = chi_square(observed)
println("Chi-squared: p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

Step 4: Association Between Two Variables

Both continuous?

If linear relationship expected and bivariate normal:

# Pearson correlation
let x = [1.2, 2.3, 3.1, 4.5, 5.2]
let y = [2.1, 4.0, 5.8, 8.2, 10.1]
let result = cor(x, y)
println("Pearson r: " + str(round(result, 4)))

If monotonic but not necessarily linear, or non-normal:

# Spearman rank correlation
let result = spearman(x, y)
println("Spearman rho: " + str(round(result.rho, 4)) +
        " p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

If robustness to outliers is critical:

# Kendall's tau (more robust than Spearman)
let result = kendall(x, y)
println("Kendall tau: " + str(round(result.tau, 4)) +
        " p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

One continuous, one categorical (2+ categories)?

This is the same as comparing groups (Step 2 or Step 3). The “association” between a continuous variable and a categorical variable is tested by comparing the continuous variable across the categories.

Step 5: Prediction and Modeling

Continuous outcome, one or more continuous predictors?

# Linear regression
let outcome = [10, 12, 14, 16, 18]
let predictor = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
let result = lm(outcome, predictor)
println("Linear model: R2=" + str(round(result.r_squared, 4)) +
        " p=" + str(round(result.pvalue, 4)))

Binary outcome (yes/no, case/control)?

# Logistic regression (GLM with binomial family)
let outcome = [0, 0, 1, 1, 1, 0, 1, 1]
let predictor_table = table([
  { age: 45, bmi: 22 },
  { age: 50, bmi: 25 },
  { age: 55, bmi: 28 },
  { age: 60, bmi: 30 },
  { age: 65, bmi: 32 },
  { age: 48, bmi: 23 },
  { age: 62, bmi: 29 },
  { age: 70, bmi: 31 }
])
let result = glm(outcome, predictor_table, family: "binomial")
println("Logistic regression: " + str(result))

Count outcome (number of mutations, reads)?

# Poisson or negative binomial GLM
let counts = [5, 12, 3, 45, 8, 22]
let result = glm(counts, predictor_table, family: "poisson")
println("Poisson GLM: " + str(result))

Time-to-event outcome?

# Cox proportional hazards model
let times = [12, 24, 6, 36, 18, 30]
let events = [1, 0, 1, 0, 1, 1]  # 1=event, 0=censored
let covariates = table([{ age: 55, stage: 2 }, { age: 60, stage: 1 },
  { age: 48, stage: 3 }, { age: 65, stage: 1 },
  { age: 52, stage: 2 }, { age: 70, stage: 3 }])
let result = cox_ph(times, events, covariates)
println("Cox PH: " + str(result))

# Kaplan-Meier survival curves (non-parametric)
let km = kaplan_meier(times, events)
println("Median survival: " + str(km.median_survival))

Step 6: Multivariate Structure

Dimensionality reduction?

# PCA — linear, deterministic, quantitative
let result = pca(data_matrix, n_components: 10)

# UMAP — nonlinear, for visualization
let result = umap(pca_coords, n_neighbors: 15, min_dist: 0.1)

# t-SNE — nonlinear, local structure emphasis
let result = tsne(pca_coords, perplexity: 30)

Clustering?

# K-means — when you know K, spherical clusters
let result = kmeans(data_matrix, k: 5)

# Leiden — graph-based, no K required, standard for single-cell
let labels = leiden(pca_coords, resolution: 1.0)

Step 7: Multiple Testing Correction

Whenever you perform more than one test, adjust p-values:

# Benjamini-Hochberg (FDR control) — standard for genomics
let adjusted = p_adjust(pvalues, "BH")

# Bonferroni (FWER control) — conservative, for few tests
let adjusted = p_adjust(pvalues, "bonferroni")

Quick Reference Table

QuestionData TypeNormal?TestBioLang
2 groups, pairedContinuousYesPaired t-testttest_paired
2 groups, pairedContinuousNoWilcoxon signed-rankwilcoxon
2 groups, independentContinuousYesWelch’s t-testttest
2 groups, independentContinuousNoWilcoxon rank-sumwilcoxon
2 groups, independentCategoricalN/AChi-squared / Fisher’schi_square / fisher_exact
3+ groups, independentContinuousYesANOVAanova
3+ groups, independentContinuousNoPairwise Wilcoxon + FDRwilcoxon + p_adjust
1 sample vs valueContinuousYesOne-sample t-testttest_one
AssociationBoth continuousYesPearson correlationcor
AssociationBoth continuousNoSpearman correlationspearman
